# Sutta Nipāta — English (Bhikkhu Sujato, CC0)

## Vagga 1 ^vagga1-0

### The Serpent (Anthology of Discourses 1.1) ^snp1-1-0

When anger surges, they drive it out, ^snp1-1-1-1

as with medicine a snake’s spreading venom. ^snp1-1-1-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-1-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-1-4

They’ve cut off greed entirely, ^snp1-1-2-1

like a lotus plucked flower and stalk. ^snp1-1-2-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-2-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-2-4

They’ve cut off craving entirely, ^snp1-1-3-1

drying up that swift-flowing stream. ^snp1-1-3-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-3-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-3-4

They’ve swept away conceit entirely, ^snp1-1-4-1

as a fragile bridge of reeds by a great flood. ^snp1-1-4-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-4-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-4-4

In future lives they find no worth, ^snp1-1-5-1

as an inspector of fig trees finds no flower. ^snp1-1-5-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-5-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-5-4

They hide no anger within, ^snp1-1-6-1

passed beyond any kind of existence. ^snp1-1-6-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-6-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-6-4

Their mental vibrations are cleared away, ^snp1-1-7-1

internally clipped off entirely. ^snp1-1-7-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-7-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-7-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-8-1

but have gone beyond all this proliferation. ^snp1-1-8-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-8-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-8-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-9-1

for they know that nothing in the world <j>is what it seems. ^snp1-1-9-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-9-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-9-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-10-1

knowing nothing is what it seems, free of greed. ^snp1-1-10-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-10-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-10-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-11-1

knowing nothing is what it seems, free of lust. ^snp1-1-11-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-11-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-11-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-12-1

knowing nothing is what it seems, free of hate. ^snp1-1-12-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-12-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-12-4

They have not run too far nor run back, ^snp1-1-13-1

knowing nothing is what it seems, free of delusion. ^snp1-1-13-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-13-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-13-4

They have no underlying tendencies at all, ^snp1-1-14-1

and are rid of unskillful roots, ^snp1-1-14-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-14-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-14-4

They have nothing born of distress at all ^snp1-1-15-1

to make them to return to the near shore. ^snp1-1-15-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-15-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-15-4

No snarl grows in them at all, ^snp1-1-16-1

that would shackle them to a new life. ^snp1-1-16-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-16-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-16-4

They’ve given up the five hindrances, ^snp1-1-17-1

untroubled, rid of indecision, free of thorns. ^snp1-1-17-2

Such a mendicant sheds the near shore and the far, ^snp1-1-17-3

as a serpent its old worn-out skin. ^snp1-1-17-4

### The Wealthy Rancher (Anthology of Discourses 1.2) ^snp1-2-0

“I’ve boiled my rice and drawn my milk,” ^snp1-2-1-1

said Dhaniya the rancher, ^snp1-2-1-2

“I stay with my family along the bank of the Mahī. ^snp1-2-1-3

My hut is roofed, my sacred fire kindled: ^snp1-2-1-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-1-5

“I boil not with anger <j>and have drawn out hard-heartedness,” ^snp1-2-2-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-2-2

“I stay for one night along the bank of the Mahī. ^snp1-2-2-3

My hut is wide open, my fire is quenched: ^snp1-2-2-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-2-5

“No gadflies or mosquitoes are found,” ^snp1-2-3-1

said Dhaniya, ^snp1-2-3-2

“cows graze on the lush meadow grass. ^snp1-2-3-3

They get by even when the rain comes: ^snp1-2-3-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-3-5

“I bound a raft and prepared it well,” ^snp1-2-4-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-4-2

“and with it I crossed over, went to the far shore, <j>and dispelled the flood. ^snp1-2-4-3

Now I have no need for a raft: ^snp1-2-4-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-4-5

“My wife is obedient, not wanton,” ^snp1-2-5-1

said Dhaniya, ^snp1-2-5-2

“long have we lived together happily. ^snp1-2-5-3

I hear nothing bad about her: ^snp1-2-5-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-5-5

“My mind is obedient and freed,” ^snp1-2-6-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-6-2

“long nurtured and well-tamed. ^snp1-2-6-3

Nothing bad is found in me: ^snp1-2-6-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-6-5

“I am self-employed,” ^snp1-2-7-1

said Dhaniya, ^snp1-2-7-2

“and my healthy children likewise. ^snp1-2-7-3

I hear nothing bad about them: ^snp1-2-7-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-7-5

“I am no-one’s lackey,” ^snp1-2-8-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-8-2

“with what I have earned I wander the world. ^snp1-2-8-3

I have no need for wages: ^snp1-2-8-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-8-5

“I have heifers and sucklings,” ^snp1-2-9-1

said Dhaniya, ^snp1-2-9-2

“cows in calf and breeding cows. ^snp1-2-9-3

I’ve also got a bull, captain of the herd here: ^snp1-2-9-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-9-5

“I have no heifers or sucklings,” ^snp1-2-10-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-10-2

“no cows in calf or breeding cows. ^snp1-2-10-3

I haven’t got a bull, captain of the herd here: ^snp1-2-10-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-10-5

“The stakes are driven in, unshakable,” ^snp1-2-11-1

said Dhaniya, ^snp1-2-11-2

“The grass halters are new and well-woven, ^snp1-2-11-3

not even the sucklings can break them: ^snp1-2-11-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-11-5

“Like a bull I broke the bonds,” ^snp1-2-12-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-12-2

“like an elephant I snapped the vine. ^snp1-2-12-3

I will never lie in a womb again: ^snp1-2-12-4

so rain forth, heavens, if you wish.” ^snp1-2-12-5

Right then a vast cloud rained forth, ^snp1-2-13-1

soaking the uplands and valleys. ^snp1-2-13-2

Hearing the heavens rain down, ^snp1-2-13-3

Dhaniya said this: ^snp1-2-13-4

“It is no small gain for us ^snp1-2-14-1

that we have seen the Buddha. ^snp1-2-14-2

We come to you for refuge, Clear-eyed One. ^snp1-2-14-3

O great sage, please be our Teacher. ^snp1-2-14-4

My wife and I, obedient, ^snp1-2-15-1

shall lead the spiritual life under the Holy One. ^snp1-2-15-2

Gone beyond birth and death, ^snp1-2-15-3

we shall make an end of suffering.” ^snp1-2-15-4

“Children bring you delight!” ^snp1-2-16-1

said Māra the Wicked, ^snp1-2-16-2

“Cattle also bring you delight! ^snp1-2-16-3

For attachments are a man’s delight; ^snp1-2-16-4

without attachments there’s no delight.” ^snp1-2-16-5

“Your children bring you sorrow,” ^snp1-2-17-1

said the Buddha, ^snp1-2-17-2

“Your cattle also bring you sorrow. ^snp1-2-17-3

For attachments are a man’s sorrow; ^snp1-2-17-4

without attachments there are no sorrows.” ^snp1-2-17-5

### The Horned Rhino (Anthology of Discourses 1.3) ^snp1-3-0

When you’ve laid down violence toward all creatures, ^snp1-3-1-1

not harming even a single one, ^snp1-3-1-2

don’t wish for a child, let alone a companion: ^snp1-3-1-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-1-4

Those with close relationships have affection, ^snp1-3-2-1

following which this pain arises. ^snp1-3-2-2

Seeing this danger born of affection, ^snp1-3-2-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-2-4

When feelings for friends and loved ones ^snp1-3-3-1

are tied up in selfish love, you miss out on the goal. ^snp1-3-3-2

Seeing this peril in intimacy, ^snp1-3-3-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-3-4

As a spreading bamboo gets entangled, ^snp1-3-4-1

so does concern for partners and children. ^snp1-3-4-2

Like a bamboo shoot unobstructed, ^snp1-3-4-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-4-4

As a wild deer loose in the forest ^snp1-3-5-1

grazes wherever it wants, ^snp1-3-5-2

a smart person looking for freedom would ^snp1-3-5-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-5-4

When among friends, whether staying in place ^snp1-3-6-1

or going on a journey, you’re always on call. ^snp1-3-6-2

Looking for the uncoveted freedom, ^snp1-3-6-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-6-4

Among friends you have fun and games, ^snp1-3-7-1

and for children you are full of fondness. ^snp1-3-7-2

Though loathe to depart from those you hold dear, ^snp1-3-7-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-7-4

At ease in any quarter, unresisting, ^snp1-3-8-1

content with whatever comes your way; ^snp1-3-8-2

prevailing over adversities, dauntless, ^snp1-3-8-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-8-4

Even some renunciates are hard to please, ^snp1-3-9-1

as are some layfolk dwelling at home. ^snp1-3-9-2

Don’t worry about others’ children, ^snp1-3-9-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-9-4

Having shed the marks of the home life, ^snp1-3-10-1

like the fallen leaves of the Shady Orchid Tree; ^snp1-3-10-2

having cut the bonds of the home life, a hero would ^snp1-3-10-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-10-4

If you find an alert companion, ^snp1-3-11-1

an attentive friend to live happily together, ^snp1-3-11-2

then, overcoming all adversities, ^snp1-3-11-3

wander with them, joyful and mindful. ^snp1-3-11-4

If you find no alert companion, ^snp1-3-12-1

an attentive friend to live happily together, ^snp1-3-12-2

then, like a king who flees his conquered realm, ^snp1-3-12-3

wander alone like a tusker in the wilds. ^snp1-3-12-4

Clearly we praise the blessing of a friend, ^snp1-3-13-1

it’s good to be with friends your equal or better. ^snp1-3-13-2

But failing to find them, eating blamelessly, ^snp1-3-13-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-13-4

Though made of shining gold, <j>well-finished by a smith, ^snp1-3-14-1

when two bracelets share the same arm ^snp1-3-14-2

they clash up against each other. Seeing this, ^snp1-3-14-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-14-4

Thinking, “So too, if I had a partner, ^snp1-3-15-1

there’d be flattery or curses.” ^snp1-3-15-2

Seeing this peril in the future, ^snp1-3-15-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-15-4

Sensual pleasures are diverse, sweet, delightful, ^snp1-3-16-1

appearing in disguise they disturb the mind. ^snp1-3-16-2

Seeing danger in sensual stimulations, ^snp1-3-16-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-16-4

This is a calamity, a boil, a disaster, ^snp1-3-17-1

an illness, a dart, and a danger for me. ^snp1-3-17-2

Seeing this peril in sensuality, ^snp1-3-17-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-17-4

Heat and cold, hunger and thirst, ^snp1-3-18-1

wind and sun, flies and snakes: ^snp1-3-18-2

having put up with all these things, ^snp1-3-18-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-18-4

As a full-grown elephant, lotus-fond, magnificent, ^snp1-3-19-1

forsaking the herd, ^snp1-3-19-2

stays as it pleases in the forest, ^snp1-3-19-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-19-4

It’s impossible for one who delights in company ^snp1-3-20-1

to experience even temporary freedom. ^snp1-3-20-2

Heeding the speech of the Kinsman of the Sun, ^snp1-3-20-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-20-4

Thinking, “I have left twisted views behind, ^snp1-3-21-1

reached the sure way, and gained the path; ^snp1-3-21-2

I’ve given rise to knowledge, <j>and need no-one to guide me”, ^snp1-3-21-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-21-4

No greed, no guile, no thirst, no slur, ^snp1-3-22-1

dross and delusion is smelted off; ^snp1-3-22-2

free of hoping for anything in the world, ^snp1-3-22-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-22-4

Avoid a wicked companion, ^snp1-3-23-1

blind to the good, habitually immoral. ^snp1-3-23-2

Don’t befriend the heedless and the hankering, but ^snp1-3-23-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-23-4

Spend time with the learned <j>who have memorized the teachings, ^snp1-3-24-1

an eloquent and uplifting friend. ^snp1-3-24-2

When you understand the meanings <j>and have dispelled doubt, ^snp1-3-24-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-24-4

When you realize that worldly fun and games ^snp1-3-25-1

and pleasure are inadequate, <j>unconcerned with them, ^snp1-3-25-2

as one unadorned, a speaker of truth, ^snp1-3-25-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-25-4

Children, partner, father, mother, ^snp1-3-26-1

wealth and grain and relatives: ^snp1-3-26-2

having given up sensual pleasures to this extent, ^snp1-3-26-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-26-4

“This is a chain. Here there’s hardly any happiness, ^snp1-3-27-1

little gratification, and it’s full of drawbacks. ^snp1-3-27-2

It’s a hook.” Knowing this, a thoughtful person would ^snp1-3-27-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-27-4

Having burst apart the fetters, ^snp1-3-28-1

like a fish that tears the net and swims free, ^snp1-3-28-2

or a fire not returning to ground it has burned, ^snp1-3-28-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-28-4

Eyes downcast, not footloose, ^snp1-3-29-1

senses guarded, mind protected, ^snp1-3-29-2

not festering or burning with desire, ^snp1-3-29-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-29-4

Having shed the marks of the home life, ^snp1-3-30-1

like the fallen leaves of the Orchid Tree, ^snp1-3-30-2

and gone forth in the ocher robe, ^snp1-3-30-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-30-4

Not wanton, nor rousing greed for tastes, ^snp1-3-31-1

providing for no other, <j>wandering indiscriminately for alms, ^snp1-3-31-2

not attached to this family or that, ^snp1-3-31-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-31-4

When you’ve given up five mental obstacles, ^snp1-3-32-1

and cast aside all corruptions, ^snp1-3-32-2

and cut off affection and hate, being independent, ^snp1-3-32-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-32-4

When you’ve put pleasure and pain behind you, ^snp1-3-33-1

and former happiness and sadness, ^snp1-3-33-2

and gained equanimity serene and pure, ^snp1-3-33-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-33-4

With energy roused to reach the ultimate goal, ^snp1-3-34-1

not sluggish in mind or lazy, ^snp1-3-34-2

vigorous, strong and powerful, ^snp1-3-34-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-34-4

Not neglecting retreat and absorption, ^snp1-3-35-1

always living in line with the teaching, ^snp1-3-35-2

comprehending the danger in rebirths, ^snp1-3-35-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-35-4

One whose aim is the ending of craving— ^snp1-3-36-1

diligent, clever, learned, mindful, resolute— ^snp1-3-36-2

who has appraised the teaching and is assured, destined for awakening, should ^snp1-3-36-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-36-4

Like a lion not startled by sounds, ^snp1-3-37-1

like wind not caught in a net, ^snp1-3-37-2

like water not sticking to a lotus, ^snp1-3-37-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-37-4

Like the fierce-fanged lion, king of beasts, ^snp1-3-38-1

who wanders as conqueror and victor, ^snp1-3-38-2

you should frequent remote lodgings, and ^snp1-3-38-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-38-4

In time, cultivate freedom through ^snp1-3-39-1

love, compassion, rejoicing, and equanimity. ^snp1-3-39-2

Not upset by anything in the world, ^snp1-3-39-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-39-4

Having given up greed, hate, and delusion, ^snp1-3-40-1

having burst apart the fetters, ^snp1-3-40-2

unafraid at the end of life, ^snp1-3-40-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-40-4

They befriend you and serve you for their own sake; ^snp1-3-41-1

these days it’s hard to find friends <j>lacking ulterior motive. ^snp1-3-41-2

Impure folk cleverly profit themselves— ^snp1-3-41-3

live alone like a horned rhino. ^snp1-3-41-4

### With Bhāradvāja the Farmer (Anthology of Discourses 1.4) ^snp1-4-0

So I have heard. ^snp1-4-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying in the land of the Magadhans in the Southern Hills near the brahmin village of Ekanāḷa. ^snp1-4-1-2

Now at that time the brahmin Bhāradvāja the Farmer had harnessed around five hundred plows, it being the season for sowing. ^snp1-4-1-3

Then the Buddha robed up in the morning and, taking his bowl and robe, went to where Bhāradvāja the Farmer was working. ^snp1-4-1-4

Now at that time Bhāradvāja the Farmer was distributing food. ^snp1-4-1-5

Then the Buddha went to where the distribution was taking place and stood to one side. ^snp1-4-1-6

Bhāradvāja the Farmer saw him standing for alms ^snp1-4-2-1

and said to him, ^snp1-4-2-2

“I plough and sow, ascetic, and then I eat. ^snp1-4-2-3

You too should plough and sow, then you may eat.” ^snp1-4-2-4

“I too plough and sow, brahmin, and then I eat.” ^snp1-4-3-1

“I don’t see the worthy Gotama with a yoke or plow or plowshare or goad or oxen, yet he says: ^snp1-4-3-2

‘I too plough and sow, brahmin, and then I eat.’” ^snp1-4-3-4

Then Bhāradvāja the Farmer addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp1-4-4-1

“You claim to be a farmer, ^snp1-4-5-1

but I don’t see you farming. ^snp1-4-5-2

Tell me your farming when asked, ^snp1-4-5-3

so I can recognize your farming.” ^snp1-4-5-4

“Faith is my seed, fervor my rain, ^snp1-4-6-1

and wisdom is my yoke and plough. ^snp1-4-6-2

Conscience is my draft-pole, mind my strap, ^snp1-4-6-3

mindfulness my plowshare and goad. ^snp1-4-6-4

Guarded in body and speech, ^snp1-4-7-1

I restrict my intake of food. ^snp1-4-7-2

I use truth as my scythe, ^snp1-4-7-3

and gentleness is my release. ^snp1-4-7-4

Energy is my harnessed beast, ^snp1-4-8-1

transporting me to sanctuary from the yoke. ^snp1-4-8-2

It goes without turning back ^snp1-4-8-3

where there is no sorrow. ^snp1-4-8-4

That’s how to do the farming ^snp1-4-9-1

that has freedom from death as its fruit. ^snp1-4-9-2

When you finish this farming ^snp1-4-9-3

you’re released from all suffering.” ^snp1-4-9-4

Then Bhāradvāja the Farmer poured milk-rice in a large bronze cup and presented it to the Buddha: ^snp1-4-10-1

“May the worthy Gotama eat the milk-rice! ^snp1-4-10-2

The worthy one is a farmer. ^snp1-4-10-3

For the worthy Gotama does the farming that has freedom from death as its fruit.” ^snp1-4-10-4

“Food enchanted by a verse isn’t fit for me to eat. ^snp1-4-11-1

That’s not the principle of those who see, brahmin. ^snp1-4-11-2

The Buddhas cast aside things enchanted with verses. ^snp1-4-11-3

Since there is such a principle, brahmin, <j>that’s how they live. ^snp1-4-11-4

Serve with other food and drink ^snp1-4-12-1

the consummate one, the great seer, ^snp1-4-12-2

with defilements ended and remorse stilled. ^snp1-4-12-3

For he is the field for the seeker of merit.” ^snp1-4-12-4

“Then, worthy Gotama, to whom should I give the milk-rice?” ^snp1-4-13-1

“Brahmin, I don’t see anyone in this world—with its gods, Māras, and Divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, its gods and humans—who can properly digest this milk-rice, except for the Realized One or one of his disciples. ^snp1-4-13-2

Well then, brahmin, throw out the milk-rice where there is little that grows, or drop it into water that has no living creatures.” ^snp1-4-13-3

So Bhāradvāja the Farmer dropped the milk-rice in water that had no living creatures. ^snp1-4-14-1

And when the milk-rice was placed in the water, it sizzled and hissed, steaming and fuming. ^snp1-4-14-2

Suppose there was an iron cauldron that had been heated all day. If you placed it in the water, it would sizzle and hiss, steaming and fuming. ^snp1-4-14-3

In the same way, when the milk-rice was placed in the water, it sizzled and hissed, steaming and fuming. ^snp1-4-14-4

Then Bhāradvāja the Farmer, shocked and awestruck,  went up to the Buddha, bowed down with his head at the Buddha’s feet, and said, ^snp1-4-15-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! ^snp1-4-15-2

As if he were righting the overturned, or revealing the hidden, or pointing out the path to the lost, or lighting a lamp in the dark so people with clear eyes can see what’s there, worthy Gotama has made the teaching clear in many ways. ^snp1-4-15-3

I go for refuge to the worthy Gotama, to the teaching, and to the mendicant Saṅgha. ^snp1-4-15-4

May I receive the going forth, the ordination in the worthy Gotama’s presence?” ^snp1-4-15-5

And Bhāradvāja the Farmer received the going forth, the ordination in the Buddha’s presence. ^snp1-4-16-1

Not long after his ordination, Venerable Bhāradvāja, living alone, withdrawn, diligent, keen, and resolute, soon realized the supreme end of the spiritual path in this very life. He lived having achieved with his own insight the goal for which gentlemen rightly go forth from the lay life to homelessness. ^snp1-4-16-2

He understood: “Rebirth is ended; the spiritual journey has been completed; what had to be done has been done; there is nothing further for this place.” ^snp1-4-16-3

And Venerable Bhāradvāja became one of the perfected. ^snp1-4-16-4

### With Cunda (Anthology of Discourses 1.5) ^snp1-5-0

“I ask the sage abounding in wisdom,” ^snp1-5-1-1

said Cunda the smith, ^snp1-5-1-2

“the Buddha, master of the teaching, free of craving, ^snp1-5-1-3

best of men, excellent charioteer, please tell me this: ^snp1-5-1-4

how many ascetics are there in the world?” ^snp1-5-1-5

“There are four ascetics, not a fifth.” ^snp1-5-2-1

said the Buddha to Cunda, ^snp1-5-2-2

“Being asked to bear witness, <j>I will explain them to you: ^snp1-5-2-3

the path-victor, the path-teacher, ^snp1-5-2-4

the path-liver, and the path-wrecker.” ^snp1-5-2-5

“Who is a path-victor according to the Buddhas?” ^snp1-5-3-1

said Cunda the smith, ^snp1-5-3-2

“and how is one an unequaled explainer of the path? ^snp1-5-3-3

Tell me when asked about one who lives the path, ^snp1-5-3-4

then declare the path-wrecker.” ^snp1-5-3-5

“Rid of indecision, free of thorns, ^snp1-5-4-1

delighting in extinguishment, not fawning, ^snp1-5-4-2

a guide for the world with its gods. ^snp1-5-4-3

The Buddhas say one such is victor of the path. ^snp1-5-4-4

Knowing the ultimate as ultimate, ^snp1-5-5-1

they explain and analyze the teaching right here. ^snp1-5-5-2

That sage unstirred, with doubt cut off, ^snp1-5-5-3

is the second mendicant, I say, the teacher of the path. ^snp1-5-5-4

Living restrained and mindful on the path ^snp1-5-6-1

of the well-taught passage of teaching, ^snp1-5-6-2

cultivating blameless states, ^snp1-5-6-3

is the third mendicant, I say, the path-liver. ^snp1-5-6-4

Dressed like one true to their vows, ^snp1-5-7-1

pushy, rude, a corrupter of families, ^snp1-5-7-2

devious, unrestrained, chaff, ^snp1-5-7-3

the path-wrecker’s life is a sham. ^snp1-5-7-4

A layperson who gets this, ^snp1-5-8-1

a learned, wise noble disciple, ^snp1-5-8-2

knows that ‘They are not all like that one’. ^snp1-5-8-3

So when they see them they don’t lose their faith. ^snp1-5-8-4

For how could one equate them— ^snp1-5-8-5

the corrupt with the uncorrupt, <j>the pure with the impure?” ^snp1-5-8-6

### Downfalls (Anthology of Discourses 1.6) ^snp1-6-0

So I have heard. ^snp1-6-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp1-6-1-2

Then, late at night, a glorious deity, lighting up the entire Jeta’s Grove, went up to the Buddha, bowed, and stood to one side. ^snp1-6-1-3

Standing to one side, that deity addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp1-6-1-4

“We ask Gotama ^snp1-6-2-1

about a man’s downfall. ^snp1-6-2-2

We have come to ask you, sir: ^snp1-6-2-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-2-4

“It’s easy to understand success, ^snp1-6-3-1

understanding downfall is just as easy. ^snp1-6-3-2

One who loves the teaching succeeds, ^snp1-6-3-3

but a hater of the teaching meets their downfall.” ^snp1-6-3-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-4-1

this is the first downfall. ^snp1-6-4-2

Tell us the second, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-4-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-4-4

“The bad are dear to him, ^snp1-6-5-1

he has no love for the good. ^snp1-6-5-2

He believes the teaching of the bad; ^snp1-6-5-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-5-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-6-1

this is the second downfall. ^snp1-6-6-2

Tell us the third, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-6-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-6-4

“Fond of sleep, fond of company, ^snp1-6-7-1

a man who does no work; ^snp1-6-7-2

he’s lazy, marked by anger, ^snp1-6-7-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-7-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-8-1

this is the third downfall. ^snp1-6-8-2

Tell us the fourth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-8-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-8-4

“Though able, he does not look after ^snp1-6-9-1

his mother and father ^snp1-6-9-2

when elderly, past their prime, ^snp1-6-9-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-9-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-10-1

this is the fourth downfall. ^snp1-6-10-2

Tell us the fifth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-10-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-10-4

“He deceives with lies ^snp1-6-11-1

ascetics and brahmins ^snp1-6-11-2

and other supplicants, ^snp1-6-11-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-11-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-12-1

this is the fifth downfall. ^snp1-6-12-2

Tell us the sixth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-12-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-12-4

“A man with plenty of wealth, ^snp1-6-13-1

including gold coin and food, ^snp1-6-13-2

eats delicacies alone, ^snp1-6-13-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-13-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-14-1

this is the sixth downfall. ^snp1-6-14-2

Tell us the seventh, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-14-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-14-4

“Vain of birth, wealth, ^snp1-6-15-1

and clan, a man ^snp1-6-15-2

looks down on his own family, ^snp1-6-15-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-15-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-16-1

this is the seventh downfall. ^snp1-6-16-2

Tell us the eighth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-16-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-16-4

“In womanizing, drinking, ^snp1-6-17-1

and gambling, a man ^snp1-6-17-2

wastes all that he has earned, ^snp1-6-17-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-17-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-18-1

this is the eighth downfall. ^snp1-6-18-2

Tell us the ninth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-18-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-18-4

“Not content with his own partners, ^snp1-6-19-1

he debauches himself with prostitutes, ^snp1-6-19-2

and with others’ partners, ^snp1-6-19-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-19-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-20-1

this is the ninth downfall. ^snp1-6-20-2

Tell us the tenth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-20-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-20-4

“A man well past his prime ^snp1-6-21-1

marries a girl with budding breasts; ^snp1-6-21-2

he cannot sleep for jealousy, ^snp1-6-21-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-21-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-22-1

this is the tenth downfall. ^snp1-6-22-2

Tell us the eleventh, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-22-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-22-4

“He places in authority ^snp1-6-23-1

a woman or a man ^snp1-6-23-2

who’s a drunkard and a wastrel, ^snp1-6-23-3

and that leads to his downfall.” ^snp1-6-23-4

“We understand what you’re saying, ^snp1-6-24-1

this is the eleventh downfall. ^snp1-6-24-2

Tell us the twelfth, Blessed One: ^snp1-6-24-3

what leads to downfall?” ^snp1-6-24-4

“A man of little wealth and strong craving, ^snp1-6-25-1

born into an aristocratic family, ^snp1-6-25-2

sets his sights on kingship, ^snp1-6-25-3

and that leads to his downfall. ^snp1-6-25-4

Seeing these downfalls in the world, ^snp1-6-26-1

an astute and noble person, ^snp1-6-26-2

accomplished in vision, ^snp1-6-26-3

will enjoy a world of grace.” ^snp1-6-26-4

### The Lowlife (Anthology of Discourses 1.7) ^snp1-7-0

So I have heard. ^snp1-7-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp1-7-1-2

Then the Buddha robed up in the morning and, taking his bowl and robe, entered Sāvatthī for alms. ^snp1-7-1-3

Now at that time in the brahmin Bhāradvāja the Fire-Worshiper’s home the sacred flame had been kindled and the oblation prepared. ^snp1-7-1-4

Wandering indiscriminately for almsfood in Sāvatthī, the Buddha approached Bhāradvāja the Fire-Worshiper’s house. ^snp1-7-1-5

Bhāradvāja the Fire-Worshiper saw the Buddha coming off in the distance ^snp1-7-2-1

and said to him, ^snp1-7-2-2

“Stop right there, shaveling! ^snp1-7-2-3

Right there, fake ascetic! ^snp1-7-2-4

Right there, lowlife!” ^snp1-7-2-5

When he said this, the Buddha said to him, ^snp1-7-3-1

“But brahmin, do you know what is a lowlife or what are the qualities that make you a lowlife?” ^snp1-7-3-2

“No I do not, worthy Gotama. ^snp1-7-3-3

Please, worthy Gotama, teach me this matter so I can understand what is a lowlife or what are the qualities that make you a lowlife.” ^snp1-7-3-4

“Well then, brahmin, listen and apply your mind well, I will speak.” ^snp1-7-3-5

“Yes, worthy sir,” Bhāradvāja the Fire-Worshiper replied. ^snp1-7-3-6

The Buddha said this: ^snp1-7-3-7

“Irritable and acrimonious, ^snp1-7-4-1

wicked and offensive, ^snp1-7-4-2

a man deficient in view, deceitful: ^snp1-7-4-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-4-4

He harms living creatures ^snp1-7-5-1

born of womb or of egg, ^snp1-7-5-2

and has no kindness for creatures: ^snp1-7-5-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-5-4

He destroys and devastates ^snp1-7-6-1

villages and towns, ^snp1-7-6-2

a notorious oppressor: ^snp1-7-6-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-6-4

Whether in village or wilderness, ^snp1-7-7-1

he steals what belongs to others, ^snp1-7-7-2

taking what has not been given: ^snp1-7-7-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-7-4

Having fallen into debt, ^snp1-7-8-1

when pressed to pay up he flees, saying ^snp1-7-8-2

‘I don’t owe you anything!’: ^snp1-7-8-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-8-4

Wanting some item or other, ^snp1-7-9-1

he attacks a person in the street ^snp1-7-9-2

and takes it: ^snp1-7-9-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-9-4

For his own sake or the sake of another, ^snp1-7-10-1

or for the sake of wealth, a man ^snp1-7-10-2

tells a lie when asked to bear witness: ^snp1-7-10-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-10-4

He is spied among the partners ^snp1-7-11-1

of relatives and friends, ^snp1-7-11-2

by force or seduction: ^snp1-7-11-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-11-4

Though able, he does not look after ^snp1-7-12-1

his mother and father ^snp1-7-12-2

when elderly, past their prime: ^snp1-7-12-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-12-4

He hits or verbally abuses ^snp1-7-13-1

his mother or father, ^snp1-7-13-2

brother, sister, or mother-in-law: ^snp1-7-13-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-13-4

When asked about the good, ^snp1-7-14-1

he teaches what is bad, ^snp1-7-14-2

giving secretive advice: ^snp1-7-14-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-14-4

Having done a bad deed, he wishes, ^snp1-7-15-1

‘May no-one find me out!’ ^snp1-7-15-2

His deeds are underhand: ^snp1-7-15-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-15-4

When visiting another family ^snp1-7-16-1

he eats their delicious food, ^snp1-7-16-2

but does not return the honor: ^snp1-7-16-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-16-4

He deceives with lies ^snp1-7-17-1

ascetics and brahmins ^snp1-7-17-2

and other supplicants: ^snp1-7-17-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-17-4

When time comes to offer a meal ^snp1-7-18-1

to brahmins or ascetics, ^snp1-7-18-2

he abuses them and does not give: ^snp1-7-18-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-18-4

He talks about what never happened, ^snp1-7-19-1

being wrapped up in delusion, ^snp1-7-19-2

chasing after some item or other: ^snp1-7-19-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-19-4

He extols himself ^snp1-7-20-1

and disparages others, ^snp1-7-20-2

brought down by his pride: ^snp1-7-20-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-20-4

He’s a bully and a miser, ^snp1-7-21-1

of corrupt wishes, stingy, and devious, ^snp1-7-21-2

shameless, imprudent: ^snp1-7-21-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-21-4

He insults the Buddha ^snp1-7-22-1

or his disciple, ^snp1-7-22-2

whether lay or renunciate: ^snp1-7-22-3

know him as a lowlife. ^snp1-7-22-4

He claims to be a perfected one, ^snp1-7-23-1

when he really is no such thing. ^snp1-7-23-2

In the world with its divinities, ^snp1-7-23-3

that crook is truly the lowest lowlife. ^snp1-7-23-4

These who are called lowlifes ^snp1-7-23-5

I have explained to you. ^snp1-7-23-6

You’re not a lowlife by birth, ^snp1-7-24-1

nor by birth are you a brahmin. ^snp1-7-24-2

You’re a lowlife by your deeds, ^snp1-7-24-3

by deeds you’re a brahmin. ^snp1-7-24-4

And also you should know ^snp1-7-25-1

according to this example. ^snp1-7-25-2

A corpse-worker’s son of the dog-eating caste ^snp1-7-25-3

became renowned as Mātaṅga. ^snp1-7-25-4

Mātaṅga achieved the highest fame ^snp1-7-26-1

so very hard to find. ^snp1-7-26-2

Lots of aristocrats and brahmins ^snp1-7-26-3

came to serve him. ^snp1-7-26-4

He ascended the stainless highway ^snp1-7-27-1

that leads to the heavens; ^snp1-7-27-2

having discarded sensual desire, ^snp1-7-27-3

he was reborn in a realm of divinity. ^snp1-7-27-4

His birth did not prevent him ^snp1-7-27-5

from rebirth in the realm of divinity. ^snp1-7-27-6

Those born in a brahmin family ^snp1-7-28-1

who recite as kinsmen of the hymns, ^snp1-7-28-2

are often discovered ^snp1-7-28-3

in the midst of wicked deeds. ^snp1-7-28-4

Blameworthy in this very life, ^snp1-7-29-1

and in the next, a bad destination. ^snp1-7-29-2

Their birth does not prevent them ^snp1-7-29-3

from blame or bad destiny. ^snp1-7-29-4

You’re not a lowlife by birth, ^snp1-7-30-1

nor by birth are you a brahmin. ^snp1-7-30-2

You’re a lowlife by your deeds, ^snp1-7-30-3

by deeds you’re a brahmin.” ^snp1-7-30-4

When he had spoken, the brahmin Bhāradvāja the Fire-Worshiper said to the Buddha, ^snp1-7-31-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! … ^snp1-7-31-2

From this day forth, may the worthy Gotama remember me as a lay follower who has gone for refuge for life.” ^snp1-7-31-3

### The Discourse on Love (Anthology of Discourses 1.8) ^snp1-8-0

Those who are skilled in the meaning of scripture ^snp1-8-1-1

should practice as follows <j>to realize the state of peace. ^snp1-8-1-2

Let them be capable and upright, very upright, ^snp1-8-1-3

easy to speak to, gentle and humble; ^snp1-8-1-4

content and unburdensome, ^snp1-8-2-1

unbusied, living lightly, ^snp1-8-2-2

alert, with senses calmed, ^snp1-8-2-3

courteous, not fawning on families. ^snp1-8-2-4

Let them not do the slightest thing ^snp1-8-3-1

that others who are wise would blame. ^snp1-8-3-2

May they be happy and safe! ^snp1-8-3-3

May all beings be happy! ^snp1-8-3-4

Whatever living creatures there are ^snp1-8-4-1

with not a one left out— ^snp1-8-4-2

frail or firm, long or large, ^snp1-8-4-3

medium, small, tiny or round, ^snp1-8-4-4

seen or unseen, ^snp1-8-5-1

living far or near, ^snp1-8-5-2

those who have been born <j>and those about to be born— ^snp1-8-5-3

may all beings be happy! ^snp1-8-5-4

Let none deceive another, ^snp1-8-6-1

nor look down on anyone anywhere. ^snp1-8-6-2

Though provoked or aggrieved, ^snp1-8-6-3

let them not wish pain on each other. ^snp1-8-6-4

Even as a mother would protect with her life ^snp1-8-7-1

her child, her only child, ^snp1-8-7-2

so too for all creatures ^snp1-8-7-3

unfold a boundless heart. ^snp1-8-7-4

With love for the whole world, ^snp1-8-8-1

unfold a boundless heart: ^snp1-8-8-2

above, below, all round, ^snp1-8-8-3

unconstricted, without enmity or foe. ^snp1-8-8-4

When standing, walking, sitting, ^snp1-8-9-1

or lying down while yet unweary, ^snp1-8-9-2

keep this ever in mind; ^snp1-8-9-3

for this, they say, is a divine meditation <j>in this life. ^snp1-8-9-4

Avoiding harmful views, ^snp1-8-10-1

virtuous, accomplished in insight, ^snp1-8-10-2

with desire for sensual pleasures dispelled, ^snp1-8-10-3

they never return to a womb again. ^snp1-8-10-4

### With Hemavata (Anthology of Discourses 1.9) ^snp1-9-0

“Today is the fifteenth day sabbath,” ^snp1-9-1-1

said Sātāgira, the native spirit of mount Sātā, ^snp1-9-1-2

“a holy night is at hand. ^snp1-9-1-3

Come now, let us see Gotama, ^snp1-9-1-4

the Teacher of peerless name.” ^snp1-9-1-5

“Isn’t his mind well-disposed,” ^snp1-9-2-1

said Hemavata, the native spirit of the Himalayas, ^snp1-9-2-2

“impartial toward all creatures? ^snp1-9-2-3

And aren’t his thoughts under control ^snp1-9-2-4

when it comes to likes and dislikes?” ^snp1-9-2-5

“His mind is well-disposed,” ^snp1-9-3-1

said Sātāgira, ^snp1-9-3-2

“impartial towards all creatures. ^snp1-9-3-3

His thoughts are under control ^snp1-9-3-4

when it comes to his likes and dislikes.” ^snp1-9-3-5

“Doesn’t he not steal?” ^snp1-9-4-1

said Hemavata, ^snp1-9-4-2

“And doesn’t he harm not a creature? ^snp1-9-4-3

Isn’t he far from negligence? ^snp1-9-4-4

And doesn’t he not neglect absorption?” ^snp1-9-4-5

“He does not take what is not given,” ^snp1-9-5-1

said Sātāgira, ^snp1-9-5-2

“and he harms not a creature. ^snp1-9-5-3

He is far from negligence— ^snp1-9-5-4

the Buddha does not neglect absorption.” ^snp1-9-5-5

“Doesn’t he avoid lying?” ^snp1-9-6-1

said Hemavata, ^snp1-9-6-2

“And doesn’t he not speak sharply? ^snp1-9-6-3

Doesn’t he avoid backbiting, ^snp1-9-6-4

as well as speaking nonsense?” ^snp1-9-6-5

“He does not lie,” ^snp1-9-7-1

said Sātāgira, ^snp1-9-7-2

“nor does he speak sharply. ^snp1-9-7-3

He avoids backbiting, ^snp1-9-7-4

and thoughtfully speaks wise counsel.” ^snp1-9-7-5

“Doesn’t he find sensual pleasures unattractive?” ^snp1-9-8-1

said Hemavata, ^snp1-9-8-2

“And isn’t his mind unclouded? ^snp1-9-8-3

Hasn’t he escaped delusion? ^snp1-9-8-4

Does his eye see clearly in all things?” ^snp1-9-8-5

“He does not find sensual pleasures attractive,” ^snp1-9-9-1

said Sātāgira, ^snp1-9-9-2

“and his mind is unclouded. ^snp1-9-9-3

He has escaped all delusion— ^snp1-9-9-4

the Buddha, whose eye sees clearly in all things.” ^snp1-9-9-5

“Isn’t he accomplished in knowledge?” ^snp1-9-10-1

said Hemavata, ^snp1-9-10-2

“And doesn’t he live a pure life? ^snp1-9-10-3

Aren’t his defilements all ended? ^snp1-9-10-4

Doesn’t he have no future lives?” ^snp1-9-10-5

“He is accomplished in knowledge,” ^snp1-9-11-1

said Sātāgira, ^snp1-9-11-2

“and he does live a pure life. ^snp1-9-11-3

His defilements are all ended, ^snp1-9-11-4

there’ll be no more future lives for him.” ^snp1-9-11-5

“Accomplished is the sage’s mind ^snp1-9-12-1

in action and in speech, ^snp1-9-12-2

and he’s accomplished in knowledge and conduct ^snp1-9-12-3

as per the teaching you praise.” ^snp1-9-12-4

“Accomplished is the sage’s mind ^snp1-9-13-1

in action and in speech, ^snp1-9-13-2

and he’s accomplished in knowledge and conduct ^snp1-9-13-3

as per the teaching you rejoice in. ^snp1-9-13-4

Accomplished is the sage’s mind ^snp1-9-14-1

in action and in speech, ^snp1-9-14-2

and he’s accomplished in knowledge and conduct: ^snp1-9-14-3

come now, let us see Gotama.” ^snp1-9-14-4

“The hero so lean, with antelope calves, ^snp1-9-15-1

not greedy, eating little, ^snp1-9-15-2

the sage meditating alone in the forest, ^snp1-9-15-3

come now, let us see Gotama. ^snp1-9-15-4

A giant, wandering alone like a lion, ^snp1-9-16-1

unconcerned for sensual pleasures, ^snp1-9-16-2

let’s approach him and ask about ^snp1-9-16-3

release from the snare of death.” ^snp1-9-16-4

“The communicator, the instructor, ^snp1-9-17-1

who has gone beyond all things, ^snp1-9-17-2

Awakened, beyond enmity and fear, ^snp1-9-17-3

let us ask Gotama.” ^snp1-9-17-4

“What has the world arisen in?” ^snp1-9-18-1

said Hemavata, ^snp1-9-18-2

“What does it get close to? ^snp1-9-18-3

By grasping what ^snp1-9-18-4

is the world troubled in what?” ^snp1-9-18-5

“The world’s arisen in six,” ^snp1-9-19-1

said the Buddha to Hemavata. ^snp1-9-19-2

“It gets close to six. ^snp1-9-19-3

Derived from the six, ^snp1-9-19-4

the world’s troubled in six.” ^snp1-9-19-5

“What is that grasping ^snp1-9-20-1

by which the world is troubled? ^snp1-9-20-2

Tell us the exit when asked: ^snp1-9-20-3

how is one released from all suffering?” ^snp1-9-20-4

“The world has five kinds of sensual stimulation, ^snp1-9-21-1

and the mind is said to be the sixth. ^snp1-9-21-2

When you’ve discarded desire for these, ^snp1-9-21-3

you’re released from all suffering. ^snp1-9-21-4

This is the exit from the world, ^snp1-9-22-1

explained in accord with the truth. ^snp1-9-22-2

The way I’ve explained it is how ^snp1-9-22-3

you’re released from all suffering.” ^snp1-9-22-4

“Who here crosses the flood, ^snp1-9-23-1

Who crosses the deluge? ^snp1-9-23-2

Who, not standing and unsupported, ^snp1-9-23-3

does not sink in the deep?” ^snp1-9-23-4

“Someone who is always endowed with ethics, ^snp1-9-24-1

wise and serene, ^snp1-9-24-2

inwardly reflective, mindful, ^snp1-9-24-3

crosses the flood so hard to cross. ^snp1-9-24-4

Someone who desists from sensual perception, ^snp1-9-25-1

who has escaped all fetters, ^snp1-9-25-2

and has ended relish for rebirth, ^snp1-9-25-3

does not sink in the deep.” ^snp1-9-25-4

“Behold him of wisdom deep <j>who sees the subtle meaning, ^snp1-9-26-1

who has nothing, unattached to sensual life, ^snp1-9-26-2

everywhere free, ^snp1-9-26-3

the great seer treading the holy road. ^snp1-9-26-4

Behold him of peerless name <j>who sees the subtle meaning, ^snp1-9-27-1

giver of wisdom, <j>unattached to the realm of sensuality: ^snp1-9-27-2

the all-knower, so very intelligent, ^snp1-9-27-3

the great seer treading the noble road. ^snp1-9-27-4

It was a fine sight for us today, ^snp1-9-28-1

a good dawn, a good rising, ^snp1-9-28-2

to see the Awakened One, ^snp1-9-28-3

the undefiled one who has crossed the flood. ^snp1-9-28-4

These thousand native spirits ^snp1-9-29-1

powerful and glorious, ^snp1-9-29-2

all go to you for refuge, ^snp1-9-29-3

you are our supreme Teacher. ^snp1-9-29-4

We shall journey ^snp1-9-30-1

village to village, peak to peak, ^snp1-9-30-2

paying homage to the Buddha, ^snp1-9-30-3

and the clear rightness of the teaching!” ^snp1-9-30-4

### With Āḷavaka (Anthology of Discourses 1.10) ^snp1-10-0

So I have heard. ^snp1-10-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Āḷavī in the haunt of the native spirit Āḷavaka. ^snp1-10-1-2

Then the native spirit Āḷavaka went up to the Buddha, and said to him: ^snp1-10-1-3

“Get out, ascetic!” ^snp1-10-1-4

Saying, “All right, respectable sir,” the Buddha went out. ^snp1-10-1-5

“Get in, ascetic!” ^snp1-10-1-6

Saying, “All right, respectable sir,” the Buddha went in. ^snp1-10-1-7

For a second time … ^snp1-10-2-1

And for a third time the native spirit Āḷavaka said to the Buddha, ^snp1-10-2-2

“Get out, ascetic!” ^snp1-10-2-3

Saying, “All right, respectable sir,” the Buddha went out. ^snp1-10-2-4

“Get in, ascetic!” ^snp1-10-2-5

Saying, “All right, respectable sir,” the Buddha went in. ^snp1-10-2-6

And for a fourth time the native spirit Āḷavaka said to the Buddha, ^snp1-10-3-1

“Get out, ascetic!” ^snp1-10-3-2

“No, respectable sir, I won’t get out. ^snp1-10-3-3

Do what you must.” ^snp1-10-3-4

“I will ask you a question, ascetic. ^snp1-10-4-1

If you don’t answer me, I’ll drive you insane, or explode your heart, or grab you by the feet and throw you to the far shore of the Ganges!” ^snp1-10-4-2

“I don’t see anyone in this world with its gods, Māras, and Divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, its gods and humans who could do that to me. ^snp1-10-5-1

But anyway, ask what you wish.” ^snp1-10-5-2

Then the native spirit Āḷavaka addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp1-10-5-3

“What’s a person’s best wealth? ^snp1-10-6-1

What brings happiness when practiced well? ^snp1-10-6-2

What’s the sweetest taste of all? ^snp1-10-6-3

The one they say has the best life: how do they live?” ^snp1-10-6-4

“Faith here is a person’s best wealth. ^snp1-10-7-1

the teaching brings happiness when practiced well. ^snp1-10-7-2

Truth is the sweetest taste of all. ^snp1-10-7-3

The one they say has the best life lives by wisdom.” ^snp1-10-7-4

“How do you cross the flood? ^snp1-10-8-1

How do you cross the deluge? ^snp1-10-8-2

How do you get over suffering? ^snp1-10-8-3

How do you get purified?” ^snp1-10-8-4

“By faith you cross the flood, ^snp1-10-9-1

and by diligence the deluge. ^snp1-10-9-2

By energy you get past suffering, ^snp1-10-9-3

and you’re purified by wisdom.” ^snp1-10-9-4

“How do you get wisdom? ^snp1-10-10-1

How do you earn wealth? ^snp1-10-10-2

How do you get a good reputation? ^snp1-10-10-3

How do you hold on to friends? ^snp1-10-10-4

How do the departed not grieve ^snp1-10-10-5

when passing from this world to the next?” ^snp1-10-10-6

“One who is diligent and discerning ^snp1-10-11-1

gains wisdom by wanting to learn, ^snp1-10-11-2

having faith in the perfected ones, ^snp1-10-11-3

and the teaching for becoming extinguished. ^snp1-10-11-4

Being responsible, acting appropriately, ^snp1-10-12-1

and working hard you earn wealth. ^snp1-10-12-2

Truthfulness wins you a good reputation. ^snp1-10-12-3

You hold on to friends by giving. ^snp1-10-12-4

A faithful householder of discernment ^snp1-10-13-1

who possesses these four qualities ^snp1-10-13-2

does not grieve after passing away: ^snp1-10-13-3

truth, principle, steadfastness, and generosity. ^snp1-10-13-4

Go ahead, ask others as well, ^snp1-10-14-1

there are many ascetics and brahmins. ^snp1-10-14-2

See whether anything better is found ^snp1-10-14-3

than truth, principle, generosity, and patience.” ^snp1-10-14-4

“Why now would I question ^snp1-10-15-1

the many ascetics and brahmins? ^snp1-10-15-2

Today I understand ^snp1-10-15-3

what’s good for the next life. ^snp1-10-15-4

It was truly for my benefit ^snp1-10-16-1

that the Buddha came to stay at Āḷavī. ^snp1-10-16-2

Today I understand ^snp1-10-16-3

where a gift is very fruitful. ^snp1-10-16-4

I myself will journey ^snp1-10-17-1

village to village, town to town, ^snp1-10-17-2

paying homage to the Buddha, ^snp1-10-17-3

and the clear rightness of the teaching!” ^snp1-10-17-4

### Victory Over Desire for the Body (Anthology of Discourses 1.11) ^snp1-11-0

Walking and standing, ^snp1-11-1-1

sitting and lying down, ^snp1-11-1-2

extending and contracting the limbs: ^snp1-11-1-3

these are the movements of the body. ^snp1-11-1-4

Linked together by bones and sinews, ^snp1-11-2-1

plastered over with flesh and hide, ^snp1-11-2-2

and covered by the skin, ^snp1-11-2-3

the body is not seen as it is. ^snp1-11-2-4

It’s full of guts and belly, ^snp1-11-3-1

liver and bladder, ^snp1-11-3-2

heart and lungs, ^snp1-11-3-3

kidney and spleen, ^snp1-11-3-4

spit and snot, ^snp1-11-4-1

sweat and fat, ^snp1-11-4-2

blood and synovial fluid, ^snp1-11-4-3

bile and grease. ^snp1-11-4-4

Then in nine streams ^snp1-11-5-1

the filth is always flowing. ^snp1-11-5-2

There is muck from the eyes, ^snp1-11-5-3

wax from the ears, ^snp1-11-5-4

and snot from the nostrils. ^snp1-11-6-1

The mouth sometimes vomits ^snp1-11-6-2

bile and sometimes phlegm. ^snp1-11-6-3

And from the body, sweat and waste. ^snp1-11-6-4

Then there is the hollow head ^snp1-11-7-1

all filled with brains. ^snp1-11-7-2

Governed by ignorance, ^snp1-11-7-3

the fool thinks it’s lovely. ^snp1-11-7-4

And when it lies dead, ^snp1-11-8-1

bloated and livid, ^snp1-11-8-2

dumped in a charnel ground, ^snp1-11-8-3

the relatives forget it. ^snp1-11-8-4

It’s devoured by dogs, ^snp1-11-9-1

by jackals, wolves, and worms. ^snp1-11-9-2

It’s devoured by crows and vultures, ^snp1-11-9-3

and any other creatures there. ^snp1-11-9-4

A wise mendicant here, ^snp1-11-10-1

having heard the Buddha’s words, ^snp1-11-10-2

fully understands it, ^snp1-11-10-3

for they see it as it is. ^snp1-11-10-4

“As this is, so is that, ^snp1-11-11-1

as that is, so is this.” ^snp1-11-11-2

They’d blanch desire for the body ^snp1-11-11-3

inside and out. ^snp1-11-11-4

That wise mendicant here ^snp1-11-12-1

rid of desire and lust, ^snp1-11-12-2

has found the peace free of death, ^snp1-11-12-3

extinguishment, the state that does not pass. ^snp1-11-12-4

This two-legged body is dirty and stinking, ^snp1-11-13-1

full of different carcasses, ^snp1-11-13-2

and oozing all over the place— ^snp1-11-13-3

but still it is cherished! ^snp1-11-13-4

And if, on account of such a body, ^snp1-11-14-1

someone prides themselves ^snp1-11-14-2

or looks down on others— ^snp1-11-14-3

what is that but a failure to see? ^snp1-11-14-4

### The Sage (Anthology of Discourses 1.12) ^snp1-12-0

Peril stems from intimacy, ^snp1-12-1-1

dust comes from an abode. ^snp1-12-1-2

Freedom from abode and intimacy: ^snp1-12-1-3

that is the sage’s vision. ^snp1-12-1-4

Having cut down what’s grown, <j>they wouldn’t replant, ^snp1-12-2-1

nor would they nurture what’s growing. ^snp1-12-2-2

That’s who they call a sage wandering alone, ^snp1-12-2-3

the great seer has seen the state of peace. ^snp1-12-2-4

Having appraised the fields and measured the seeds, ^snp1-12-3-1

they wouldn’t nurture them with moisture. ^snp1-12-3-2

Truly that sage who sees the ending of rebirth ^snp1-12-3-3

has left speculation behind and is beyond reckoning. ^snp1-12-3-4

Understanding all the planes of rebirth, ^snp1-12-4-1

not wanting a single one of them; ^snp1-12-4-2

truly that sage freed of greed ^snp1-12-4-3

need not swim, <j>for they have reached the far shore. ^snp1-12-4-4

The vanquisher of all, knower of all, so very intelligent, ^snp1-12-5-1

unsullied in the midst of all things, ^snp1-12-5-2

has given up all, freed in the ending of craving: ^snp1-12-5-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-5-4

Strong in wisdom, <j>with precepts and observances intact, ^snp1-12-6-1

serene, loving absorption, mindful, ^snp1-12-6-2

released from chains, kind, undefiled: ^snp1-12-6-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-6-4

The diligent sage wandering alone, ^snp1-12-7-1

is unshaken by blame and praise— ^snp1-12-7-2

like a lion not startled by sounds, ^snp1-12-7-3

like wind not caught in a net, ^snp1-12-7-4

like water not sticking to a lotus. ^snp1-12-7-5

Leader of others, not by others led: ^snp1-12-7-6

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-7-7

Steady as a post in a bathing-place ^snp1-12-8-1

when others speak endlessly against them, ^snp1-12-8-2

freed of greed, with senses stilled: ^snp1-12-8-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-8-4

Steadfast, straight as a shuttle, ^snp1-12-9-1

horrified by wicked deeds, ^snp1-12-9-2

discerning the just and the unjust: ^snp1-12-9-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-9-4

Restrained, they do no evil, ^snp1-12-10-1

young or middle-aged, the sage is self-controlled. ^snp1-12-10-2

Irreproachable, he does not insult anyone: ^snp1-12-10-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-10-4

When one who lives on charity receives alms, ^snp1-12-11-1

from the top, the middle, or the leftovers, ^snp1-12-11-2

they think it unworthy to praise or put down: ^snp1-12-11-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-11-4

The sage lives refraining from sex, ^snp1-12-12-1

even when young is not tied down, ^snp1-12-12-2

refraining from indulgence and negligence, freed: ^snp1-12-12-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-12-4

Understanding the world, <j>the seer of the ultimate goal, ^snp1-12-13-1

the unaffected one, <j>having crossed the flood and the ocean, ^snp1-12-13-2

has cut the ties, unattached and undefiled: ^snp1-12-13-3

that’s who the attentive know as a sage. ^snp1-12-13-4

The two are not the same, <j>far apart in lifestyle and conduct— ^snp1-12-14-1

the householder providing for a wife, <j>and the selfless one true to their vows. ^snp1-12-14-2

The unrestrained householder kills other creatures, ^snp1-12-14-3

while the restrained sage <j>always protects living creatures. ^snp1-12-14-4

As the crested blue-necked peacock <j>flying through the sky ^snp1-12-15-1

never approaches the speed of the swan, ^snp1-12-15-2

so the householder cannot compete <j>with the mendicant, ^snp1-12-15-3

the sage meditating secluded in the woods. ^snp1-12-15-4

## Vagga 2 ^vagga2-0

### Gems (Anthology of Discourses 2.1) ^snp2-1-0

Whatever beings have gathered here, ^snp2-1-1-1

on the ground or in the sky: ^snp2-1-1-2

may beings all be of happy heart, ^snp2-1-1-3

and listen carefully to what is said. ^snp2-1-1-4

So pay heed, all you beings, ^snp2-1-2-1

have love for humankind, ^snp2-1-2-2

who day and night bring offerings; ^snp2-1-2-3

please protect them diligently. ^snp2-1-2-4

There’s no wealth in this life or the next, ^snp2-1-3-1

no sublime gem in the heavens, ^snp2-1-3-2

that equals the Realized One. ^snp2-1-3-3

This sublime gem is in the Buddha: ^snp2-1-3-4

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-3-5

Ending, dispassion, the undying, the sublime, ^snp2-1-4-1

attained by the Sakyan Sage immersed in samādhi; ^snp2-1-4-2

there is nothing equal to that Dhamma. ^snp2-1-4-3

This sublime gem is in the Dhamma: ^snp2-1-4-4

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-4-5

The purity praised by the highest Buddha ^snp2-1-5-1

is said to be the “immersion with immediate fruit”; ^snp2-1-5-2

no equal to that immersion is found. ^snp2-1-5-3

This sublime gem is in the Dhamma: ^snp2-1-5-4

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-5-5

The eight individual persons praised by the good ^snp2-1-6-1

are the four pairs of the Holy One’s disciples; ^snp2-1-6-2

they are worthy of religious donations, ^snp2-1-6-3

what’s given to them is very fruitful. ^snp2-1-6-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-6-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-6-6

Dedicated to Gotama’s dispensation, ^snp2-1-7-1

strong-minded, free of sense desire, ^snp2-1-7-2

they’ve attained the goal, <j>plunged into freedom from death, ^snp2-1-7-3

and enjoy the quenching they’ve freely gained. ^snp2-1-7-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-7-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-7-6

As Indra’s pillar rooted in earth ^snp2-1-8-1

is not shaken by the four winds, ^snp2-1-8-2

I say a true person is like this, ^snp2-1-8-3

who sees the noble truths in experience. ^snp2-1-8-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-8-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-8-6

Those who fathom the noble truths ^snp2-1-9-1

taught by the one of deep wisdom, ^snp2-1-9-2

do not take an eighth life, ^snp2-1-9-3

even if they are hugely negligent. ^snp2-1-9-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-9-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-9-6

When they attain to vision ^snp2-1-10-1

they give up three things: ^snp2-1-10-2

substantialist view, doubt, and any ^snp2-1-10-3

attachment to precepts and observances. ^snp2-1-10-4

They’re freed from the four places of loss, ^snp2-1-11-1

and cannot do six things. ^snp2-1-11-2

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-11-3

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-11-4

Even if they do a bad deed ^snp2-1-12-1

by body, speech, or mind, ^snp2-1-12-2

they are unable to conceal it; ^snp2-1-12-3

this inability applies to <j>one who has seen the truth, they say. ^snp2-1-12-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-12-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-12-6

Like a tall forest tree crowned with flowers ^snp2-1-13-1

in the first month of summer; ^snp2-1-13-2

that’s how he taught the superb Dhamma, ^snp2-1-13-3

leading to extinguishment, the ultimate benefit. ^snp2-1-13-4

This sublime gem is in the Buddha: ^snp2-1-13-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-13-6

The superb, knower of the superb, <j>giver of the superb, bringer of the superb; ^snp2-1-14-1

taught the superb Dhamma supreme. ^snp2-1-14-2

This sublime gem is in the Buddha: ^snp2-1-14-3

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-14-4

The old is ended, nothing new is produced. ^snp2-1-15-1

their minds have no desire for future rebirth. ^snp2-1-15-2

Withered are the seeds, there’s no desire for growth, ^snp2-1-15-3

those attentive ones are extinguished like this lamp. ^snp2-1-15-4

This sublime gem is in the Saṅgha: ^snp2-1-15-5

by this truth, may you be well! ^snp2-1-15-6

Whatever beings have gathered here, ^snp2-1-16-1

on the ground or in the sky: ^snp2-1-16-2

the Realized One is honored by gods and humans! ^snp2-1-16-3

We bow to the Buddha! May you be safe! ^snp2-1-16-4

Whatever beings have gathered here, ^snp2-1-17-1

on the ground or in the sky: ^snp2-1-17-2

the Realized One is honored by gods and humans! ^snp2-1-17-3

We bow to the Dhamma! May you be safe! ^snp2-1-17-4

Whatever beings have gathered here, ^snp2-1-18-1

on the ground or in the sky: ^snp2-1-18-2

the Realized One is honored by gods and humans! ^snp2-1-18-3

We bow to the Saṅgha! May you be safe! ^snp2-1-18-4

### Putrefaction (Anthology of Discourses 2.2) ^snp2-2-0

“The good eat properly obtained ^snp2-2-1-1

millet, wild grains, broomcorn, ^snp2-2-1-2

greens, tubers, and squashes. ^snp2-2-1-3

They don’t lie to get what they want. ^snp2-2-1-4

But when you eat delicious food, ^snp2-2-2-1

nicely cooked and prepared, and offered by others, ^snp2-2-2-2

enjoying a dish of fine rice, ^snp2-2-2-3

Kassapa, you eat putrefaction. ^snp2-2-2-4

‘Putrefaction is not appropriate for me’— ^snp2-2-3-1

so you said, O brahmin only by kin. ^snp2-2-3-2

Yet here you are enjoying a dish of fine rice, ^snp2-2-3-3

well prepared with the flesh of fowl. ^snp2-2-3-4

I’m asking you this, Kassapa: ^snp2-2-3-5

what do you take to be putrefaction?” ^snp2-2-3-6

“Killing creatures, mutilation, murder, abduction; ^snp2-2-4-1

stealing, lying, cheating and fraud, ^snp2-2-4-2

learning crooked spells, adultery: ^snp2-2-4-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-4-4

People here with unbridled sensuality, ^snp2-2-5-1

greedy for tastes, mixed up in impurity, ^snp2-2-5-2

nihilists, immoral, intractable: ^snp2-2-5-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-5-4

Tough, callous calumniators, ^snp2-2-6-1

pitiless and arrogant betrayers of friends, ^snp2-2-6-2

misers who never give anything: ^snp2-2-6-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-6-4

Anger, vanity, obstinacy, contrariness, ^snp2-2-7-1

deceit, jealousy, boastfulness, ^snp2-2-7-2

haughtiness, intimacy with the wicked: ^snp2-2-7-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-7-4

The ill-behaved, debt-evaders, informants, ^snp2-2-8-1

business cheats and con-artists, ^snp2-2-8-2

vile men committing sin: ^snp2-2-8-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-8-4

People here who can’t stop harming living creatures, ^snp2-2-9-1

taking from others, intent on hurting, ^snp2-2-9-2

immoral, cruel, harsh, lacking regard for others: ^snp2-2-9-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-9-4

Greedy, hostile, aggressive to others, ^snp2-2-10-1

and addicted to evil—<j>those beings pass into darkness, ^snp2-2-10-2

falling headlong into hell: ^snp2-2-10-3

this is putrefaction, not eating meat. ^snp2-2-10-4

Not fish or flesh or fasting, ^snp2-2-11-1

nudity or baldness, or dreadlocks or dirt, ^snp2-2-11-2

not rough hides or serving the sacred flame, ^snp2-2-11-3

or the many endless mortifications in the world, ^snp2-2-11-4

not hymns or oblations, <j>sacrifices or seasonal observances, ^snp2-2-11-5

will cleanse a mortal not free of doubt. ^snp2-2-11-6

Guarding the streams of sense impressions, <j>wander with faculties conquered, ^snp2-2-12-1

standing on the teaching, delighting in sincerity and tenderness. ^snp2-2-12-2

The wise have slipped their chains <j>and given up all suffering; ^snp2-2-12-3

they don’t cling to the seen and the heard.” ^snp2-2-12-4

The Buddha explained this matter to him <j>again and again, ^snp2-2-13-1

until the master of hymns understood it. ^snp2-2-13-2

It was illustrated with colorful verses ^snp2-2-13-3

by the sage free of putrefaction, <j>unattached, hard to trace. ^snp2-2-13-4

Having heard the fine words of the Buddha, ^snp2-2-14-1

that are free of putrefaction, <j>casting aside all suffering; ^snp2-2-14-2

humbled, he bowed to the Realized One, ^snp2-2-14-3

and right away begged to go forth. ^snp2-2-14-4

### Conscientious Companionship (Anthology of Discourses 2.3) ^snp2-3-0

Flouting conscience, loathing it, ^snp2-3-1-1

saying “I’m on your side”, ^snp2-3-1-2

but not following up in deeds— ^snp2-3-1-3

know they’re not on your side. ^snp2-3-1-4

Some say nice things to their friends ^snp2-3-2-1

without following it up. ^snp2-3-2-2

The wise will recognize ^snp2-3-2-3

one who talks without doing. ^snp2-3-2-4

No true friend relentlessly ^snp2-3-3-1

suspects betrayal, looking for fault. ^snp2-3-3-2

One on whom you rest, like a child on the breast, ^snp2-3-3-3

is a true friend, not split from you by others. ^snp2-3-3-4

One whose reward is the fruit ^snp2-3-4-1

of bearing the burden of service ^snp2-3-4-2

develops a happy state, ^snp2-3-4-3

producing joy and attracting praise. ^snp2-3-4-4

One who has drunk the nectar of seclusion ^snp2-3-5-1

and the nectar of peace, ^snp2-3-5-2

free of stress, free of evil, ^snp2-3-5-3

drinks the joyous nectar of Dhamma. ^snp2-3-5-4

### Blessings (Anthology of Discourses 2.4) ^snp2-4-0

So I have heard. ^snp2-4-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp2-4-1-2

Then, late at night, a glorious deity, lighting up the entire Jeta’s Grove, went up to the Buddha, bowed, and stood to one side. ^snp2-4-1-3

Standing to one side, that deity addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp2-4-1-4

“Many gods and humans ^snp2-4-2-1

have thought about blessings ^snp2-4-2-2

desiring well-being: ^snp2-4-2-3

declare the highest blessing.” ^snp2-4-2-4

“Not to fraternize with fools, ^snp2-4-3-1

but to fraternize with the wise, ^snp2-4-3-2

and honoring those worthy of honor: ^snp2-4-3-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-3-4

Living in a suitable region, ^snp2-4-4-1

having made merit in the past, ^snp2-4-4-2

being rightly resolved in oneself, ^snp2-4-4-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-4-4

Education and a craft, ^snp2-4-5-1

discipline and training, ^snp2-4-5-2

and well-spoken speech: ^snp2-4-5-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-5-4

Caring for mother and father, ^snp2-4-6-1

kindness to children and partners, ^snp2-4-6-2

and unstressful work: ^snp2-4-6-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-6-4

Giving and righteous conduct, ^snp2-4-7-1

kindness to relatives, ^snp2-4-7-2

blameless deeds: ^snp2-4-7-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-7-4

Desisting and abstaining from evil, ^snp2-4-8-1

avoiding drinking liquor, ^snp2-4-8-2

diligence in good qualities: ^snp2-4-8-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-8-4

Respect and placidity, ^snp2-4-9-1

contentment and gratitude, ^snp2-4-9-2

and timely listening to the teaching: ^snp2-4-9-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-9-4

Patience, being easy to admonish, ^snp2-4-10-1

the sight of ascetics, ^snp2-4-10-2

and timely discussion of the teaching: ^snp2-4-10-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-10-4

Fervor and chastity ^snp2-4-11-1

seeing the noble truths, ^snp2-4-11-2

and realization of extinguishment: ^snp2-4-11-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-11-4

Though touched by worldly things, ^snp2-4-12-1

their mind does not tremble; ^snp2-4-12-2

sorrowless, stainless, secure, ^snp2-4-12-3

this is the highest blessing. ^snp2-4-12-4

Having completed these things, ^snp2-4-13-1

undefeated everywhere; ^snp2-4-13-2

everywhere they go in safety: ^snp2-4-13-3

this is their highest blessing.” ^snp2-4-13-4

### With Spiky (Anthology of Discourses 2.5) ^snp2-5-0

So I have heard. ^snp2-5-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Gayā on the cut-stone ledge in the haunt of Spiky the native spirit. ^snp2-5-1-2

Now at that time the native spirits Shaggy and Spiky were passing by not far from the Buddha. ^snp2-5-1-3

So Shaggy said to Spiky, ^snp2-5-1-4

“That’s an ascetic.” ^snp2-5-1-5

“That’s no ascetic, he’s a faker! ^snp2-5-1-6

I’ll soon find out whether he’s an ascetic or a faker.” ^snp2-5-1-7

Then Spiky went up to the Buddha and leaned up against his body, ^snp2-5-2-1

but the Buddha pulled away. ^snp2-5-2-2

Then Spiky said to the Buddha, ^snp2-5-2-3

“Are you afraid, ascetic?” ^snp2-5-2-4

“No, respectable sir, I’m not afraid. ^snp2-5-2-5

But your touch is nasty.” ^snp2-5-2-6

“I will ask you a question, ascetic. ^snp2-5-3-1

If you don’t answer me, I’ll drive you insane, or explode your heart, or grab you by the feet and throw you to the far shore of the Ganges!” ^snp2-5-3-2

“I don’t see anyone in this world with its gods, Māras, and Divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, its gods and humans who could do that to me. ^snp2-5-4-1

But anyway, ask what you wish.” ^snp2-5-4-2

Then Spiky addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp2-5-4-3

“Where do greed and hate come from? ^snp2-5-5-1

From where spring discontent, desire, and terror? ^snp2-5-5-2

Where do the mind’s thoughts originate, ^snp2-5-5-3

like a crow let loose by boys.” ^snp2-5-5-4

“Greed and hate come from here; ^snp2-5-6-1

from here spring discontent, desire, and terror; ^snp2-5-6-2

here’s where the mind’s thoughts originate, ^snp2-5-6-3

like a crow let loose by boys. ^snp2-5-6-4

Born of affection, originating in oneself, ^snp2-5-7-1

like prop roots sprouting from a banyan; ^snp2-5-7-2

the many kinds of attachment to sensual pleasures ^snp2-5-7-3

are like camel’s foot creeper <j>creeping through the woods. ^snp2-5-7-4

Those who understand where they come from ^snp2-5-8-1

get rid of them—listen up, spirit! ^snp2-5-8-2

They cross this flood so hard to cross, ^snp2-5-8-3

not crossed before, so as to not be reborn.” ^snp2-5-8-4

### A Righteous Life (Anthology of Discourses 2.6) ^snp2-6-0

A righteous life, a spiritual life, ^snp2-6-1-1

they call this the supreme treasure. ^snp2-6-1-2

But if someone goes forth ^snp2-6-1-3

from the lay life to homelessness ^snp2-6-1-4

who is of scurrilous character, ^snp2-6-2-1

a beast and a bully, ^snp2-6-2-2

their life gets worse, ^snp2-6-2-3

as poison grows inside them. ^snp2-6-2-4

A mendicant who loves to quarrel, ^snp2-6-3-1

wrapped in delusion, ^snp2-6-3-2

doesn’t even know what’s been explained ^snp2-6-3-3

in the Dhamma taught by the Buddha. ^snp2-6-3-4

Harassing those who are evolved, ^snp2-6-4-1

governed by ignorance, ^snp2-6-4-2

they don’t know that corruption ^snp2-6-4-3

is the path that leads to hell. ^snp2-6-4-4

Entering the underworld, ^snp2-6-5-1

passing from womb to womb, <j>from darkness to darkness, ^snp2-6-5-2

such a mendicant ^snp2-6-5-3

falls into suffering after death. ^snp2-6-5-4

One such as that is ^snp2-6-6-1

like a sewer ^snp2-6-6-2

brimful with years of filth ^snp2-6-6-3

for it’s hard to clean one full of grime. ^snp2-6-6-4

Mendicants, knowing that someone is like this, ^snp2-6-7-1

attached to the domestic life, ^snp2-6-7-2

of corrupt wishes and wicked intent, ^snp2-6-7-3

of bad behavior and alms-resort, ^snp2-6-7-4

then having gathered in harmony, ^snp2-6-8-1

you should expel them. ^snp2-6-8-2

Throw out the trash! ^snp2-6-8-3

Get rid of the rubbish! ^snp2-6-8-4

And sweep away the scraps— ^snp2-6-9-1

they’re not ascetics, they just think they are. ^snp2-6-9-2

When you’ve thrown out those of corrupt wishes, ^snp2-6-9-3

of bad behavior and alms-resort, ^snp2-6-9-4

dwell in communion, ever mindful, ^snp2-6-10-1

the pure with the pure. ^snp2-6-10-2

Then in harmony, alert, ^snp2-6-10-3

you’ll make an end of suffering. ^snp2-6-10-4

### Brahmanical Traditions (Anthology of Discourses 2.7) ^snp2-7-0

So I have heard. ^snp2-7-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp2-7-1-2

Then several old and well-to-do brahmins of Kosala—elderly and senior, who were advanced in years and had reached the final stage of life—went up to the Buddha, and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp2-7-1-3

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, they sat down to one side and said to the Buddha: ^snp2-7-1-4

“Worthy Gotama, are the ancient traditions of the brahmins exhibited these days among brahmins?” ^snp2-7-1-5

“No, brahmins, they are not.” ^snp2-7-1-6

“If you wouldn’t mind, worthy Gotama, please teach us the ancient traditions of the brahmins.” ^snp2-7-1-7

“Well then, brahmins, listen and apply your mind well, I will speak.” ^snp2-7-1-8

“Yes, worthy sir,” they replied. ^snp2-7-1-9

The Buddha said this: ^snp2-7-1-10

“The ancient seers used to be ^snp2-7-2-1

restrained and austere. ^snp2-7-2-2

Having given up the five sensual titillations, ^snp2-7-2-3

they lived for their own true good. ^snp2-7-2-4

Brahmins used to own no cattle, ^snp2-7-3-1

nor gold coin or grain. ^snp2-7-3-2

Chanting was their money and grain, ^snp2-7-3-3

which they guarded as a gift from god. ^snp2-7-3-4

Food was prepared for them ^snp2-7-4-1

and left beside their doors. ^snp2-7-4-2

People believed that food prepared in faith ^snp2-7-4-3

should be given to them. ^snp2-7-4-4

With colorful clothes, ^snp2-7-5-1

lodgings and houses, ^snp2-7-5-2

prosperous nations and countries ^snp2-7-5-3

honored those brahmins. ^snp2-7-5-4

Brahmins used to be inviolable and ^snp2-7-6-1

invincible, protected by principle. ^snp2-7-6-2

No-one ever turned them away ^snp2-7-6-3

from the doors of families. ^snp2-7-6-4

For forty-eight years ^snp2-7-7-1

they led the spiritual life. ^snp2-7-7-2

The brahmins of old pursued ^snp2-7-7-3

their quest for knowledge and conduct. ^snp2-7-7-4

Brahmins never transgressed with another, ^snp2-7-8-1

nor did they purchase a wife. ^snp2-7-8-2

They lived together in love, ^snp2-7-8-3

joining together by mutual consent. ^snp2-7-8-4

Brahmins never approached their wives for sex ^snp2-7-9-1

during the time outside ^snp2-7-9-2

the fertile phase of the menstrual cycle ^snp2-7-9-3

after menstruation. ^snp2-7-9-4

They praised chastity and morality, ^snp2-7-10-1

integrity, tenderness, and fervor, ^snp2-7-10-2

gentleness and harmlessness, ^snp2-7-10-3

and also patience. ^snp2-7-10-4

He who was supreme among them, ^snp2-7-11-1

godlike, staunchly vigorous, ^snp2-7-11-2

did not engage in sex ^snp2-7-11-3

even in a dream. ^snp2-7-11-4

Training in line with their duties, ^snp2-7-12-1

many smart people here ^snp2-7-12-2

praised chastity and morality, ^snp2-7-12-3

and also patience. ^snp2-7-12-4

They begged for rice, ^snp2-7-13-1

bedding, clothes, ghee, and oil. ^snp2-7-13-2

Having collected them legitimately, ^snp2-7-13-3

they arranged a sacrifice. ^snp2-7-13-4

But they slew no cows ^snp2-7-14-1

while serving at the sacrifice. ^snp2-7-14-2

Like a mother, father, or brother, ^snp2-7-14-3

or some other relative, ^snp2-7-14-4

cows are our best friends, ^snp2-7-14-5

the fonts of medicine. ^snp2-7-14-6

They give food and strength, ^snp2-7-15-1

and beauty and happiness. ^snp2-7-15-2

Knowing these benefits, ^snp2-7-15-3

they slew no cows. ^snp2-7-15-4

The brahmins were delicate and tall, ^snp2-7-16-1

beautiful and glorious. ^snp2-7-16-2

They were keen on all the duties ^snp2-7-16-3

required by their own traditions. ^snp2-7-16-4

So long as they continued in the world, ^snp2-7-16-5

people flourished happily. ^snp2-7-16-6

But perversion crept into them ^snp2-7-17-1

little by little when they saw ^snp2-7-17-2

the transformation of the king ^snp2-7-17-3

and the ladies in all their finery. ^snp2-7-17-4

Their chariots were harnessed with thoroughbreds, ^snp2-7-18-1

well-made with bright canopies, ^snp2-7-18-2

and their homes and houses ^snp2-7-18-3

were neatly divided section by section. ^snp2-7-18-4

They were lavished with herds of cattle, ^snp2-7-19-1

and furnished with bevies of lovely ladies. ^snp2-7-19-2

This extravagant human wealth ^snp2-7-19-3

was coveted by the brahmins. ^snp2-7-19-4

They compiled hymns to that end, ^snp2-7-20-1

approached King Okkāka and said, ^snp2-7-20-2

‘You have plenty of money and grain. ^snp2-7-20-3

Sacrifice! For you have much treasure. ^snp2-7-20-4

Sacrifice! For you have much money.’ ^snp2-7-20-5

Persuaded by the brahmins, ^snp2-7-21-1

the king, chief of charioteers, performed ^snp2-7-21-2

horse sacrifice, human sacrifice, ^snp2-7-21-3

the sacrifices of the ‘casting of the yoke-pin’, <j>the ‘royal soma drinking’, and the ‘unimpeded’. ^snp2-7-21-4

When he had carried out these sacrifices, ^snp2-7-21-5

he gave riches to the brahmins. ^snp2-7-21-6

There were cattle, bedding, and clothes, ^snp2-7-22-1

and ladies in all their finery; ^snp2-7-22-2

chariots harnessed with thoroughbreds, ^snp2-7-22-3

well-made with bright canopies; ^snp2-7-22-4

and lovely homes, ^snp2-7-23-1

nicely divided section by section. ^snp2-7-23-2

Having furnished them with different grains, ^snp2-7-23-3

he gave riches to the brahmins. ^snp2-7-23-4

When they got hold of that wealth, ^snp2-7-24-1

they arranged to store it up. ^snp2-7-24-2

Falling under the sway of desire, ^snp2-7-24-3

their craving grew and grew. ^snp2-7-24-4

They compiled hymns to that end, ^snp2-7-24-5

approached King Okkāka once more and said, ^snp2-7-24-6

‘Like water and earth, ^snp2-7-25-1

gold coin, riches, and grain, ^snp2-7-25-2

are cows for humankind, ^snp2-7-25-3

as they are essential for creatures. ^snp2-7-25-4

Sacrifice! For you have much treasure. ^snp2-7-25-5

Sacrifice! For you have much money.’ ^snp2-7-25-6

Persuaded by the brahmins, ^snp2-7-26-1

the king, chief of charioteers, ^snp2-7-26-2

had many hundred thousand cows ^snp2-7-26-3

slain at the sacrifice. ^snp2-7-26-4

Neither with feet nor with horns ^snp2-7-27-1

do cows harm anyone at all. ^snp2-7-27-2

Cows meek as lambs ^snp2-7-27-3

supply buckets of milk. ^snp2-7-27-4

But taking them by the horns, ^snp2-7-27-5

the king slew them with a sword. ^snp2-7-27-6

At that the gods and the ancestors, ^snp2-7-28-1

with Indra, the titans and monsters, ^snp2-7-28-2

roared out: ‘This is a crime against nature!’ ^snp2-7-28-3

as the sword fell on the cows. ^snp2-7-28-4

There used to be three kinds of illness: ^snp2-7-29-1

greed, starvation, and old age. ^snp2-7-29-2

But due to the slaughter of cows, ^snp2-7-29-3

this grew to be ninety-eight. ^snp2-7-29-4

This crime of the rods ^snp2-7-30-1

is of ancient descent. ^snp2-7-30-2

Killing innocent creatures, ^snp2-7-30-3

the sacrificers forsake righteousness. ^snp2-7-30-4

And that is how this mean old practice ^snp2-7-31-1

was criticized by sensible people. ^snp2-7-31-2

Wherever they see such a thing, ^snp2-7-31-3

folk criticize the sacrificer. ^snp2-7-31-4

With righteousness gone, ^snp2-7-32-1

peasants and menials were split, ^snp2-7-32-2

as were many aristocrats, ^snp2-7-32-3

and wives looked down on their husbands. ^snp2-7-32-4

Aristocrats and brahmins only by kin, ^snp2-7-33-1

and others protected by their clan, ^snp2-7-33-2

neglecting their genealogy, ^snp2-7-33-3

fell under the sway of sensual pleasures.” ^snp2-7-33-4

When he had spoken, those well-to-do brahmins said to the Buddha, ^snp2-7-34-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! … ^snp2-7-34-2

From this day forth, may the worthy Gotama remember us as lay followers who have gone for refuge for life.” ^snp2-7-34-3

### The Boat (Anthology of Discourses 2.8) ^snp2-8-0

If you would learn the teaching from someone, ^snp2-8-1-1

honor them as the gods honor Indra. ^snp2-8-1-2

Then they will have confidence in you, ^snp2-8-1-3

and being learned, they reveal the teaching. ^snp2-8-1-4

Heeding well, an attentive one, ^snp2-8-2-1

practicing in line with that teaching ^snp2-8-2-2

grows intelligent, discerning, and subtle ^snp2-8-2-3

through diligently sticking close to such a person. ^snp2-8-2-4

But associating with a petty fool ^snp2-8-3-1

who falls short of the goal, jealous, ^snp2-8-3-2

then unable to discern the teaching in this life, ^snp2-8-3-3

one proceeds to death still plagued by doubts. ^snp2-8-3-4

It’s like a man who has plunged into a river, ^snp2-8-4-1

a rushing torrent in spate. ^snp2-8-4-2

As they are swept away downstream, ^snp2-8-4-3

how could they help others across? ^snp2-8-4-4

Just so, one unable to discern the teaching, ^snp2-8-5-1

who hasn’t studied the meaning under the learned, ^snp2-8-5-2

not knowing it oneself, still plagued by doubts, ^snp2-8-5-3

how could they help others to contemplate? ^snp2-8-5-4

But one who has embarked on a strong boat ^snp2-8-6-1

equipped with rudder and oar, ^snp2-8-6-2

would bring many others across there ^snp2-8-6-3

with skill, care, and intelligence. ^snp2-8-6-4

So too one who understands—a knowledge master, ^snp2-8-7-1

evolved, learned, and unflappable— ^snp2-8-7-2

can help others to contemplate, ^snp2-8-7-3

so long as they are prepared to listen carefully. ^snp2-8-7-4

That’s why you should spend time with a true person, ^snp2-8-8-1

intelligent and learned. ^snp2-8-8-2

Having understood the meaning, <j>putting it into practice, ^snp2-8-8-3

one who understands the teaching <j>would find happiness. ^snp2-8-8-4

### What Morality? (Anthology of Discourses 2.9) ^snp2-9-0

“With what morality, what conduct, ^snp2-9-1-1

fostering what deeds, ^snp2-9-1-2

would a person lay the foundations right, ^snp2-9-1-3

and reach the highest goal?” ^snp2-9-1-4

“Honoring elders without jealousy, ^snp2-9-2-1

they’d know the time to visit their teachers. ^snp2-9-2-2

Treasuring the chance for a Dhamma talk, ^snp2-9-2-3

they’d listen carefully to the well-spoken words. ^snp2-9-2-4

At the right time, they’d enter the teacher’s presence ^snp2-9-3-1

in placid manner, leaving obstinacy behind. ^snp2-9-3-2

They’d call to mind and put into practice ^snp2-9-3-3

the meaning, the teaching, self-control, <j>and the spiritual life. ^snp2-9-3-4

Delighting in the teaching, enjoying the teaching, ^snp2-9-4-1

standing on the teaching, evaluating the teaching, ^snp2-9-4-2

they’d never say anything that degraded the teaching, ^snp2-9-4-3

but would be guided by genuine words well-spoken. ^snp2-9-4-4

Giving up mirth, muttered prayer, weeping, ill will, ^snp2-9-5-1

deceit, fawning, greed, conceit, ^snp2-9-5-2

aggression, crudeness, stains, and indulgence, ^snp2-9-5-3

they’d wander free of vanity, steadfast. ^snp2-9-5-4

Understanding is the essence of well-spoken words, ^snp2-9-6-1

stillness is the essence of learning and understanding. ^snp2-9-6-2

Wisdom and learning do not flourish ^snp2-9-6-3

in a hasty and negligent person. ^snp2-9-6-4

Those happy with the teaching <j>proclaimed by the Noble One ^snp2-9-7-1

are supreme in speech, mind, and deed. ^snp2-9-7-2

Abiding in peace, gentleness, and stillness, ^snp2-9-7-3

they’ve realized the essence of learning and wisdom.” ^snp2-9-7-4

### Get Up! (Anthology of Discourses 2.10) ^snp2-10-0

Get up and meditate! ^snp2-10-1-1

What’s the point in your sleeping? ^snp2-10-1-2

How can the afflicted slumber ^snp2-10-1-3
