when inflicted by an arrow strike? ^snp2-10-1-4

Get up and meditate! ^snp2-10-2-1

Train hard for peace! ^snp2-10-2-2

The King of Death has caught you heedless— ^snp2-10-2-3

don’t let him fool you under his sway. ^snp2-10-2-4

Needy gods and humans ^snp2-10-3-1

are held back by clinging: ^snp2-10-3-2

get over it. ^snp2-10-3-3

Don’t let the moment pass you by. ^snp2-10-3-4

For if you miss your moment ^snp2-10-3-5

you’ll grieve when sent to hell. ^snp2-10-3-6

Negligence is always dust; ^snp2-10-4-1

dust follows right behind negligence. ^snp2-10-4-2

Through diligence and knowledge, ^snp2-10-4-3

pluck out the dart from yourself. ^snp2-10-4-4

### With Rāhula (Anthology of Discourses 2.11) ^snp2-11-0

“Does familiarity breed contempt, ^snp2-11-1-1

even for the man of wisdom? ^snp2-11-1-2

Do you honor he who holds aloft ^snp2-11-1-3

the torch for all humanity?” ^snp2-11-1-4

“Familiarity breeds no contempt ^snp2-11-2-1

for the man of wisdom. ^snp2-11-2-2

I always honor he who holds aloft ^snp2-11-2-3

the torch for all humanity.” ^snp2-11-2-4

“One who’s given up the five sensual stimulations, ^snp2-11-3-1

so pleasing and delightful, ^snp2-11-3-2

and who’s left the home life out of faith— ^snp2-11-3-3

let them make an end to suffering! ^snp2-11-3-4

Mix with spiritual friends, ^snp2-11-4-1

stay in remote lodgings, ^snp2-11-4-2

secluded and quiet, ^snp2-11-4-3

and eat in moderation. ^snp2-11-4-4

Robes, almsfood, ^snp2-11-5-1

requisites and lodgings: ^snp2-11-5-2

don’t crave such things; ^snp2-11-5-3

don’t come back to this world again. ^snp2-11-5-4

Be restrained in the monastic code ^snp2-11-6-1

and the five sense faculties. ^snp2-11-6-2

With mindfulness immersed in the body, ^snp2-11-6-3

be full of disillusionment. ^snp2-11-6-4

Turn away from the sign ^snp2-11-7-1

that’s attractive, provoking lust. ^snp2-11-7-2

With mind unified and serene, ^snp2-11-7-3

meditate on the ugly aspects of the body. ^snp2-11-7-4

Meditate on the signless, ^snp2-11-8-1

give up the tendency to conceit; ^snp2-11-8-2

and when you comprehend conceit, ^snp2-11-8-3

you will live at peace.” ^snp2-11-8-4

That is how the Buddha regularly advised Venerable Rāhula with these verses. ^snp2-11-9-1

### Vaṅgīsa and His Mentor Nigrodhakappa (Anthology of Discourses 2.12) ^snp2-12-0

So I have heard. ^snp2-12-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Āḷavī, at Āḷavī’s premier shrine. ^snp2-12-1-2

Now at that time it was not long after Venerable Vaṅgīsa’s mentor, the senior monk named Nigrodhakappa, had become fully quenched. ^snp2-12-1-3

Then as Vaṅgīsa was in private retreat this thought came to his mind: ^snp2-12-1-4

“Has my mentor become quenched or not?” ^snp2-12-1-5

Then in the late afternoon, Venerable Vaṅgīsa came out of retreat and went to the Buddha. He bowed, sat down to one side, and said to him: ^snp2-12-1-6

“Just now, sir, as I was in private retreat this thought came to mind. ^snp2-12-1-7

‘Has my mentor become quenched or not?’” ^snp2-12-1-8

Then Venerable Vaṅgīsa rose from his seat, arranged his robe over one shoulder, raised his cupped palms toward the Buddha, and addressed him in verse: ^snp2-12-1-9

“I ask the teacher unrivaled in wisdom, ^snp2-12-2-1

who has cut off all doubts in this very life: ^snp2-12-2-2

a monk has died at Āḷavī’s premier shrine, ^snp2-12-2-3

who was well-known, famous, and quenched. ^snp2-12-2-4

Nigrodhakappa was his name; ^snp2-12-3-1

it was given to that brahmin by you, Blessed One. ^snp2-12-3-2

He wandered in your honor, yearning for freedom, ^snp2-12-3-3

energetic, a resolute Seer of Truth. ^snp2-12-3-4

O Sakyan, All-seer, ^snp2-12-4-1

we all wish to know about that disciple. ^snp2-12-4-2

Our ears are eager to hear, ^snp2-12-4-3

for you are the most excellent teacher. ^snp2-12-4-4

Cut off our doubt, declare this to us; ^snp2-12-5-1

your wisdom is vast, tell us of his quenching! ^snp2-12-5-2

All-seer, speak among us, ^snp2-12-5-3

like the thousand-eyed Sakka <j>in the midst of the gods! ^snp2-12-5-4

Whatever ties there are, or paths to delusion, ^snp2-12-6-1

or things on the side of unknowing, <j>or that are bases of doubt ^snp2-12-6-2

vanish on reaching a Realized One, ^snp2-12-6-3

for his eye is the best of all people’s. ^snp2-12-6-4

If no man were ever to disperse corruptions, ^snp2-12-7-1

like the wind dispersing the stormclouds, ^snp2-12-7-2

darkness would shroud the whole world; ^snp2-12-7-3

not even brilliant men would shine. ^snp2-12-7-4

The attentive are bringers of light; ^snp2-12-8-1

my hero, that is what I think of you. ^snp2-12-8-2

We’ve come for your discernment and knowledge: ^snp2-12-8-3

here in this assembly, declare to us about Kappāyana. ^snp2-12-8-4

Swiftly send forth your sweet, sweet voice, ^snp2-12-9-1

like a goose stretching its neck, gently honking, ^snp2-12-9-2

lucid-flowing, with lovely tone: ^snp2-12-9-3

alert, we all listen to you. ^snp2-12-9-4

You have entirely abandoned birth and death; ^snp2-12-10-1

restrained and pure, I urge you to speak the Dhamma! ^snp2-12-10-2

For while ordinary people have no wish-granter, ^snp2-12-10-3

Realized Ones have a comprehensibility-granter. ^snp2-12-10-4

Your answer is definitive, and we will adopt it, ^snp2-12-11-1

for you have perfect understanding. ^snp2-12-11-2

We raise our cupped palms one last time, ^snp2-12-11-3

one of unrivaled wisdom, <j>don’t deliberately confuse us. ^snp2-12-11-4

Knowing the noble teaching from top to bottom, ^snp2-12-12-1

unrivaled hero, don’t deliberately confuse us. ^snp2-12-12-2

As a man baking in the summer sun <j>would long for water, ^snp2-12-12-3

I long for your voice, so let the sound rain  forth. ^snp2-12-12-4

Surely Kappāyana did not <j>lead the spiritual life in vain? ^snp2-12-13-1

Did he realize quenching, ^snp2-12-13-2

or did he still have a residue? ^snp2-12-13-3

Let us hear what kind of liberation he had!” ^snp2-12-13-4

“He cut off craving for name and form right here,” ^snp2-12-14-1

said the Buddha, ^snp2-12-14-2

“the river of darkness that had long lain within him. ^snp2-12-14-3

He has entirely crossed over birth and death.” ^snp2-12-14-4

So declared the Blessed One, the leader of the five. ^snp2-12-14-5

“Now that I have heard your words, ^snp2-12-15-1

seventh of seers, I am confident. ^snp2-12-15-2

My question, it seems, was not in vain, ^snp2-12-15-3

the brahmin did not deceive me. ^snp2-12-15-4

As he said, so he did— ^snp2-12-16-1

he was a disciple of the Buddha. ^snp2-12-16-2

He cut the net of death the deceiver, ^snp2-12-16-3

so extended and strong. ^snp2-12-16-4

Blessed One, Kappāyana saw ^snp2-12-17-1

the starting point of grasping. ^snp2-12-17-2

He has indeed gone far beyond ^snp2-12-17-3

Death’s dominion so hard to pass.” ^snp2-12-17-4

### The Right Way to Wander (Anthology of Discourses 2.13) ^snp2-13-0

“I ask the sage abounding in wisdom— ^snp2-13-1-1

crossed-over, gone beyond, quenched, steadfast: ^snp2-13-1-2

when a mendicant has left home, casting aside sensuality, ^snp2-13-1-3

what’s the right way to wander the world?” ^snp2-13-1-4

“When they’ve eradicated superstitions,” ^snp2-13-2-1

said the Buddha, ^snp2-13-2-2

“about celestial portents, dreams, or bodily marks; ^snp2-13-2-3

with the stain of superstitions left behind, ^snp2-13-2-4

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-2-5

A mendicant ought dispel desire ^snp2-13-3-1

for pleasures human or heavenly; ^snp2-13-3-2

with rebirth transcended and truth comprehended, ^snp2-13-3-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-3-4

Putting backbiting behind them, ^snp2-13-4-1

a mendicant gives up anger and stinginess; ^snp2-13-4-2

with favoring and opposing left behind, ^snp2-13-4-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-4-4

When the loved and the unloved are both left behind, ^snp2-13-5-1

not grasping or dependent on anything; ^snp2-13-5-2

freed from all things that fetter, ^snp2-13-5-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-5-4

Finding no core in attachments, ^snp2-13-6-1

rid of desire for things they’ve acquired, ^snp2-13-6-2

independent, needing no-one to guide them, ^snp2-13-6-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-6-4

Not hostile in speech, mind, or deed, ^snp2-13-7-1

they’ve rightly understood the teaching. ^snp2-13-7-2

Aspiring to the state of extinguishment, ^snp2-13-7-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-7-4

Not gloating that, ‘they bow to me’; ^snp2-13-8-1

though reviled, they’d still stay in touch; ^snp2-13-8-2

not besotted when getting food from others, ^snp2-13-8-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-8-4

When greed and craving to live again are cast off, ^snp2-13-9-1

a mendicant refrains from violence and abduction; ^snp2-13-9-2

rid of indecision, free of thorns, ^snp2-13-9-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-9-4

Knowing what is suitable for themselves, ^snp2-13-10-1

a mendicant would hurt no-one in the world; ^snp2-13-10-2

understanding the teaching in line with the truth, ^snp2-13-10-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-10-4

They have no underlying tendencies at all, ^snp2-13-11-1

and are rid of unskillful roots; ^snp2-13-11-2

free of hope, with no need for hope, ^snp2-13-11-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-11-4

Defilements ended, conceit given up, ^snp2-13-12-1

beyond all manner of desire; ^snp2-13-12-2

tamed, quenched, and steadfast, ^snp2-13-12-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-12-4

Faithful, learned, seer of the sure path, ^snp2-13-13-1

an attentive one joins no faction among sects; ^snp2-13-13-2

rid of greed, hate, and aversion, ^snp2-13-13-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-13-4

A purified victor with veil cast off, ^snp2-13-14-1

among worldly things master, transcendent, stilled; ^snp2-13-14-2

expert in knowledge of conditions’ cessation, ^snp2-13-14-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-14-4

They’re over speculating on the future or past, ^snp2-13-15-1

and understand what it means to be pure; ^snp2-13-15-2

freed from all the sense fields, ^snp2-13-15-3

they’d rightly wander the world. ^snp2-13-15-4

The state of peace is understood, <j>the truth is comprehended, ^snp2-13-16-1

they’ve openly seen defilements cast off; ^snp2-13-16-2

and with the ending of all attachments, ^snp2-13-16-3

they’d rightly wander the world.” ^snp2-13-16-4

“Clearly, Blessed One, it is just as you say. ^snp2-13-17-1

One who lives like this is a tamed mendicant, ^snp2-13-17-2

passed beyond all fetters and yokes: ^snp2-13-17-3

they’d rightly wander the world.” ^snp2-13-17-4

### With Dhammika (Anthology of Discourses 2.14) ^snp2-14-0

So I have heard. ^snp2-14-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp2-14-1-2

Then the lay follower Dhammika, together with five hundred lay followers, went up to the Buddha, bowed, sat down to one side, and addressed him in verse: ^snp2-14-1-3

“I ask you, Gotama, whose wisdom is vast: ^snp2-14-2-1

what does one do to become a good disciple, ^snp2-14-2-2

both one who has left the home, ^snp2-14-2-3

and the lay followers staying at home? ^snp2-14-2-4

For you understand the course and destiny ^snp2-14-3-1

of the world with all its gods. ^snp2-14-3-2

There is no equal to you who sees the subtle meaning, ^snp2-14-3-3

for you are the Buddha most excellent, they say. ^snp2-14-3-4

Having experienced all knowledge, ^snp2-14-4-1

you explain the teaching out of sympathy for beings. ^snp2-14-4-2

All-seer, you have cast off the veil, ^snp2-14-4-3

and immaculate, you shine on the whole world. ^snp2-14-4-4

The dragon king Erāvaṇa, <j>hearing you called ‘Victor’, ^snp2-14-5-1

came into your presence. ^snp2-14-5-2

He consulted with you then, <j>having heard your words, ^snp2-14-5-3

left consoled, saying ‘Excellent!’ ^snp2-14-5-4

And King Kuvera Vessavaṇa also ^snp2-14-6-1

approached to ask about the teaching. ^snp2-14-6-2

You also answered him, O attentive one, ^snp2-14-6-3

and hearing you he too was consoled. ^snp2-14-6-4

Those monastics of other religions given to debate, ^snp2-14-7-1

whether Ājīvakas or Jains, ^snp2-14-7-2

all fail to overtake you in wisdom, ^snp2-14-7-3

like a standing man next to a sprinter. ^snp2-14-7-4

Those brahmins given to debate, ^snp2-14-8-1

some of whom are quite senior, ^snp2-14-8-2

all end up beholden to you for the meaning, ^snp2-14-8-3

and others too who think themselves debaters. ^snp2-14-8-4

So subtle and pleasant is the teaching ^snp2-14-9-1

that is well proclaimed by you, Blessed One. ^snp2-14-9-2

It’s all we long to hear. So when asked, ^snp2-14-9-3

O Best of Buddhas, tell us! ^snp2-14-9-4

All these mendicants have gathered, ^snp2-14-10-1

and the layfolk too are here to listen. ^snp2-14-10-2

Let them hear the teaching <j>the immaculate one discovered, ^snp2-14-10-3

like gods listening to the fine words of Vāsava.” ^snp2-14-10-4

“Listen to me, mendicants, I will educate you ^snp2-14-11-1

in the cleansing teaching; all bear it in mind. ^snp2-14-11-2

An intelligent person, seeing the meaning, ^snp2-14-11-3

would adopt the deportment <j>proper to a renunciate. ^snp2-14-11-4

No way would a mendicant go out at the wrong time; ^snp2-14-12-1

at the right time, they’d walk the village for alms. ^snp2-14-12-2

For chains bind one who wanders at the wrong time, ^snp2-14-12-3

which is why the Buddhas avoid it. ^snp2-14-12-4

Sights, sounds, tastes, smells, and touches, ^snp2-14-13-1

which drive beings mad— ^snp2-14-13-2

dispel desire for such things, ^snp2-14-13-3

and enter for the morning meal at the right time. ^snp2-14-13-4

After receiving alms for the day, ^snp2-14-14-1

on returning a mendicant would sit in private alone. ^snp2-14-14-2

Inwardly reflective, they’d curb their mind ^snp2-14-14-3

from outside things, keeping themselves collected. ^snp2-14-14-4

Should they converse with a disciple, ^snp2-14-15-1

with anyone else, or with a mendicant, ^snp2-14-15-2

they’d bring up only the sublime teaching, ^snp2-14-15-3

not dividing or blaming. ^snp2-14-15-4

For some contend in debate, ^snp2-14-16-1

but we praise not those of little wisdom. ^snp2-14-16-2

In place after place they are bound in chains, ^snp2-14-16-3

for they send their mind over there far away. ^snp2-14-16-4

Alms, a dwelling, a bed and seat, ^snp2-14-17-1

and water for rinsing the dust from the cloak— ^snp2-14-17-2

after hearing the teaching of the Holy One, ^snp2-14-17-3

a disciple of outstanding wisdom would use these after appraisal. ^snp2-14-17-4

That’s why, when it comes to alms and lodgings, ^snp2-14-18-1

and water for rinsing the dust from the cloak, ^snp2-14-18-2

a mendicant is unsullied in the midst of these things, ^snp2-14-18-3

like a droplet on a lotus-leaf. ^snp2-14-18-4

Now I shall tell you the householder’s duty, ^snp2-14-19-1

doing which one becomes a good disciple. ^snp2-14-19-2

For one burdened with possessions <j>does not get to realize ^snp2-14-19-3

the whole of the mendicant’s practice. ^snp2-14-19-4

They’d not kill any creature, nor have them killed, ^snp2-14-20-1

nor grant permission for others to kill. ^snp2-14-20-2

They’ve laid aside violence towards all creatures ^snp2-14-20-3

frail or firm that there are in the world. ^snp2-14-20-4

Next, a disciple would avoid knowingly ^snp2-14-21-1

taking anything not given at all, ^snp2-14-21-2

they’d not get others to do it, <j>nor grant them permission to steal; ^snp2-14-21-3

they’d avoid <em>all</em> theft. ^snp2-14-21-4

A sensible person would avoid the unchaste life, ^snp2-14-22-1

like a burning pit of coals. ^snp2-14-22-2

But if unable to remain chaste, ^snp2-14-22-3

they’d not transgress with another’s partner. ^snp2-14-22-4

In a council or assembly, ^snp2-14-23-1

or one on one, they would not lie. ^snp2-14-23-2

They’d not get others to lie, <j>nor grant them permission to lie; ^snp2-14-23-3

they’d avoid <em>all</em> untruths. ^snp2-14-23-4

A householder espousing this teaching ^snp2-14-24-1

would not consume liquor or drink. ^snp2-14-24-2

They’d not get others to drink, <j>nor grant them permission to drink; ^snp2-14-24-3

knowing that ends in intoxication. ^snp2-14-24-4

For drunken fools do bad things, ^snp2-14-25-1

and encourage other heedless folk. ^snp2-14-25-2

Shun this field of demerit, ^snp2-14-25-3

the maddening, deluding frolic of fools. ^snp2-14-25-4

You shouldn’t kill living creatures, or steal, ^snp2-14-26-1

or lie, or drink liquor. ^snp2-14-26-2

Be chaste, refraining from sex, ^snp2-14-26-3

and don’t eat at night, the wrong time. ^snp2-14-26-4

Not wearing garlands or applying fragrance, ^snp2-14-27-1

you should sleep on a low bed, <j>or a mat on the ground. ^snp2-14-27-2

This is the eight-factored sabbath, they say, ^snp2-14-27-3

explained by the Buddha, <j>who has gone to suffering’s end. ^snp2-14-27-4

Then having rightly undertaken the sabbath ^snp2-14-28-1

complete in all its eight factors ^snp2-14-28-2

on the fourteenth, fifteenth, <j>and eighth of the fortnight, ^snp2-14-28-3

as well as on the fortnightly special displays, ^snp2-14-28-4

at daybreak after the sabbath ^snp2-14-29-1

a clever person, rejoicing with confident heart, ^snp2-14-29-2

would distribute food and drink ^snp2-14-29-3

to the mendicant Saṅgha as is fitting. ^snp2-14-29-4

One should rightfully support one’s parents, ^snp2-14-30-1

and undertake a legitimate business. ^snp2-14-30-2

A diligent layperson observing these duties ^snp2-14-30-3

ascends to the gods called Self-luminous.” ^snp2-14-30-4

## Vagga 3 ^vagga3-0

### Going Forth (Anthology of Discourses 3.1) ^snp3-1-0

“I shall extol going forth ^snp3-1-1-1

with the example of the Clear-eyed One, ^snp3-1-1-2

the course of inquiry that led to ^snp3-1-1-3

his choice to go forth. ^snp3-1-1-4

‘This life at home is cramped, ^snp3-1-2-1

a realm of dirt.’ ^snp3-1-2-2

‘The life of one gone forth is like an open space.’ ^snp3-1-2-3

Seeing this, he went forth. ^snp3-1-2-4

Having gone forth, he shunned ^snp3-1-3-1

bad deeds of body. ^snp3-1-3-2

And leaving verbal misconduct behind, ^snp3-1-3-3

he purified his livelihood. ^snp3-1-3-4

The Buddha went to Rājagaha, ^snp3-1-4-1

the Mountainfold of the Magadhans. ^snp3-1-4-2

He betook himself for alms, ^snp3-1-4-3

replete with excellent marks. ^snp3-1-4-4

Bimbisāra saw him ^snp3-1-5-1

while standing atop his longhouse. ^snp3-1-5-2

Noticing that he was endowed with marks, ^snp3-1-5-3

he said the following: ^snp3-1-5-4

‘Pay heed, sirs, to this one, ^snp3-1-6-1

handsome, majestic, radiant; ^snp3-1-6-2

accomplished in deportment, ^snp3-1-6-3

he looks just a plough’s length in front. ^snp3-1-6-4

Eyes downcast, mindful, ^snp3-1-7-1

unlike one from a low family. ^snp3-1-7-2

Let the king’s messengers run out, ^snp3-1-7-3

and find where the mendicant will go.’ ^snp3-1-7-4

The messengers sent out ^snp3-1-8-1

followed right behind, thinking ^snp3-1-8-2

‘Where will the mendicant go? ^snp3-1-8-3

Where shall he find a place to stay?’ ^snp3-1-8-4

Wandering indiscriminately for alms, ^snp3-1-9-1

sense doors guarded and well restrained, ^snp3-1-9-2

aware and mindful, ^snp3-1-9-3

his bowl was quickly filled. ^snp3-1-9-4

Having wandered for alms, ^snp3-1-10-1

the sage left the city. ^snp3-1-10-2

He betook himself to Mount Paṇḍava, ^snp3-1-10-3

thinking, ‘Here is the place I shall stay.’ ^snp3-1-10-4

Seeing that he had arrived at a place to stay, ^snp3-1-11-1

the messengers withdrew nearby, ^snp3-1-11-2

but one of them returned ^snp3-1-11-3

to inform the king. ^snp3-1-11-4

‘Great king, the mendicant ^snp3-1-12-1

is on the east flank of Mount Paṇḍava. ^snp3-1-12-2

There he sits, like a tiger or a bull, ^snp3-1-12-3

like a lion in a mountain cave.’ ^snp3-1-12-4

Hearing the messenger’s report, ^snp3-1-13-1

the aristocrat set out ^snp3-1-13-2

hurriedly in his fine chariot ^snp3-1-13-3

towards Mount Paṇḍava. ^snp3-1-13-4

He went as far as vehicles could go, ^snp3-1-14-1

then dismounted from his chariot, ^snp3-1-14-2

approached on foot, ^snp3-1-14-3

and reaching him, drew near. ^snp3-1-14-4

Seated, the king greeted him ^snp3-1-15-1

and made polite conversation. ^snp3-1-15-2

When the courtesies were over, ^snp3-1-15-3

he said the following: ^snp3-1-15-4

‘You are young, just a youth, ^snp3-1-16-1

a lad in the prime of life. ^snp3-1-16-2

You are endowed with beauty and stature, ^snp3-1-16-3

like an aristocrat of good birth ^snp3-1-16-4

in glory at the army’s head, ^snp3-1-17-1

surrounded by a troop of elephants. ^snp3-1-17-2

I offer you pleasures—enjoy them! ^snp3-1-17-3

But please tell me of your birth.’ ^snp3-1-17-4

‘Up north, O King, ^snp3-1-18-1

on the slopes of the Himalayas, ^snp3-1-18-2

full of wealth and strength, lies the land ^snp3-1-18-3

of one whose abode is among the Kosalans. ^snp3-1-18-4

Their clan is named for the Sun, ^snp3-1-19-1

they are Sakyans by birth. ^snp3-1-19-2

I have gone forth from that family— ^snp3-1-19-3

I do not yearn for sensual pleasure. ^snp3-1-19-4

Seeing the danger in sensual pleasures, ^snp3-1-20-1

seeing renunciation as sanctuary, ^snp3-1-20-2

I shall go on to strive; ^snp3-1-20-3

that is where my mind delights.’” ^snp3-1-20-4

### Striving (Anthology of Discourses 3.2) ^snp3-2-0

“During my time of resolute striving ^snp3-2-1-1

back on the bank of the Nerañjara River, ^snp3-2-1-2

I was meditating very hard ^snp3-2-1-3

for the sake of sanctuary from the yoke. ^snp3-2-1-4

Namucī approached, ^snp3-2-2-1

speaking words of kindness: ^snp3-2-2-2

‘You’re thin, discolored, ^snp3-2-2-3

on the verge of death. ^snp3-2-2-4

Death has a thousand parts of you, ^snp3-2-3-1

one fraction is left to life. ^snp3-2-3-2

Live, worthy sir! Life is better! ^snp3-2-3-3

Living, you can make merits. ^snp3-2-3-4

While leading the spiritual life ^snp3-2-4-1

and serving the sacred flame, ^snp3-2-4-2

you can pile up abundant merit— ^snp3-2-4-3

so what will striving do for you? ^snp3-2-4-4

Hard to walk is the path of striving, ^snp3-2-5-1

hard to do, a hard challenge to win.’” ^snp3-2-5-2

These are the verses Māra spoke ^snp3-2-5-3

as he stood beside the Buddha. ^snp3-2-5-4

When Māra had spoken in this way, ^snp3-2-6-1

the Buddha said this: ^snp3-2-6-2

“O Wicked One, kinsman of the negligent, ^snp3-2-6-3

you’re here for your own purpose. ^snp3-2-6-4

I have no need for ^snp3-2-7-1

the slightest bit of merit. ^snp3-2-7-2

Those with need for merit ^snp3-2-7-3

are fit for Māra to address. ^snp3-2-7-4

I have faith and energy too, ^snp3-2-8-1

and wisdom is found in me. ^snp3-2-8-2

When I am so resolute, ^snp3-2-8-3

why do you beg me to live? ^snp3-2-8-4

The rivers and streams ^snp3-2-9-1

might be dried by the wind, ^snp3-2-9-2

so why, when I am resolute, ^snp3-2-9-3

should it not dry up my blood? ^snp3-2-9-4

And while the blood is drying up, ^snp3-2-10-1

the bile and phlegm dry too. ^snp3-2-10-2

And as my muscles waste away, ^snp3-2-10-3

my mind grows more serene. ^snp3-2-10-4

And all the stronger grow mindfulness ^snp3-2-10-5

and wisdom and immersion. ^snp3-2-10-6

As I meditate like this, ^snp3-2-11-1

having attained the supreme feeling, ^snp3-2-11-2

my mind has no interest in sensual pleasures: ^snp3-2-11-3

behold a being’s purity! ^snp3-2-11-4

Sensual pleasures are your first army, ^snp3-2-12-1

the second is called discontent, ^snp3-2-12-2

hunger and thirst are the third, ^snp3-2-12-3

and the fourth is said to be craving. ^snp3-2-12-4

Your fifth is dullness and drowsiness, ^snp3-2-13-1

the sixth is said to be cowardice, ^snp3-2-13-2

your seventh is doubt, ^snp3-2-13-3

contempt and obstinacy are your eighth. ^snp3-2-13-4

Profit, praise, and honor, ^snp3-2-14-1

and misbegotten fame; ^snp3-2-14-2

the extolling of oneself ^snp3-2-14-3

while scorning others. ^snp3-2-14-4

This is your army, Namucī, ^snp3-2-15-1

the strike force of the Dark One. ^snp3-2-15-2

Only a hero can defeat it, ^snp3-2-15-3

but in victory there lies bliss. ^snp3-2-15-4

Let me gird myself— ^snp3-2-16-1

curse my life! ^snp3-2-16-2

I’d rather die in battle ^snp3-2-16-3

than live on in defeat. ^snp3-2-16-4

Here some ascetics and brahmins ^snp3-2-17-1

are swallowed up, not to be seen again. ^snp3-2-17-2

And they do not know the path ^snp3-2-17-3

traveled by those true to their vows. ^snp3-2-17-4

Seeing Māra ready with his legions, ^snp3-2-18-1

surrounded by his bannered forces, ^snp3-2-18-2

I shall meet them in battle— ^snp3-2-18-3

they’ll never make me retreat! ^snp3-2-18-4

That army of yours has never been beaten ^snp3-2-19-1

by the world with all its gods. ^snp3-2-19-2

Yet I shall smash it with wisdom, ^snp3-2-19-3

like an unfired pot with a stone. ^snp3-2-19-4

Having brought my thoughts under control, ^snp3-2-20-1

and established mindfulness well, ^snp3-2-20-2

I shall wander from country to country, ^snp3-2-20-3

guiding many disciples. ^snp3-2-20-4

Diligent and resolute, ^snp3-2-21-1

following my instructions, ^snp3-2-21-2

they will proceed despite your will, ^snp3-2-21-3

to where there is no sorrow.” ^snp3-2-21-4

“For seven years I followed ^snp3-2-22-1

step by step behind the Blessed One. ^snp3-2-22-2

I found no vulnerability ^snp3-2-22-3

in the mindful Awakened One. ^snp3-2-22-4

A crow once circled a stone ^snp3-2-23-1

that looked like a lump of fat. ^snp3-2-23-2

‘Perhaps I’ll find something tender,’ it thought, ^snp3-2-23-3

‘perhaps there’s something tasty.’ ^snp3-2-23-4

But finding nothing tasty, ^snp3-2-24-1

the crow left that place. ^snp3-2-24-2

Like the crow that pecked the stone, ^snp3-2-24-3

I leave Gotama disappointed.” ^snp3-2-24-4

So stricken with sorrow ^snp3-2-25-1

that his harp dropped from his armpit, ^snp3-2-25-2

that spirit, downcast, ^snp3-2-25-3

vanished right there. ^snp3-2-25-4

### Well-Spoken Words (Anthology of Discourses 3.3) ^snp3-3-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-3-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp3-3-1-2

There the Buddha addressed the mendicants, ^snp3-3-1-3

“Mendicants!” ^snp3-3-1-4

“Venerable sir,” they replied. ^snp3-3-1-5

The Buddha said this: ^snp3-3-1-6

“Mendicants, speech that has four factors is well spoken, not poorly spoken. It’s blameless and is not criticized by sensible people. ^snp3-3-2-1

What four? ^snp3-3-2-2

It’s when a mendicant speaks well, not poorly; their speech is principled, not unprincipled; they speak pleasantly, not unpleasantly; and they speak truthfully, not falsely. ^snp3-3-2-3

Speech with these four factors is well spoken, not poorly spoken. It’s blameless and is not criticized by sensible people.” ^snp3-3-2-4

That is what the Buddha said. ^snp3-3-2-5

Then the Holy One, the Teacher, went on to say: ^snp3-3-2-6

“Good people say well-spoken words are foremost; ^snp3-3-3-1

second, speech ought be principled, not unprincipled; ^snp3-3-3-2

third, speak pleasantly, not unpleasantly; ^snp3-3-3-3

and fourth, speak truthfully, not falsely.” ^snp3-3-3-4

Then Venerable Vaṅgīsa rose from his seat, arranged his robe over one shoulder, raised his cupped palms toward the Buddha, and said, ^snp3-3-4-1

“I feel inspired to speak, Blessed One! I feel inspired to speak, Holy One!” ^snp3-3-4-2

“Then speak as you feel inspired,” said the Buddha. ^snp3-3-4-3

Then Vaṅgīsa extolled the Buddha in his presence with fitting verses: ^snp3-3-4-4

“Speak only such words ^snp3-3-5-1

that do not hurt yourself ^snp3-3-5-2

nor harm others; ^snp3-3-5-3

such speech is truly well spoken. ^snp3-3-5-4

Speak only pleasing words, ^snp3-3-6-1

words gladly welcomed. ^snp3-3-6-2

Pleasing words are those ^snp3-3-6-3

that bring nothing bad to others. ^snp3-3-6-4

Truth itself is undying speech: ^snp3-3-7-1

this is an ancient teaching. ^snp3-3-7-2

Good people say the teaching and meaning ^snp3-3-7-3

are grounded in truth. ^snp3-3-7-4

The words spoken by the Buddha ^snp3-3-8-1

for finding the sanctuary, extinguishment, ^snp3-3-8-2

for making an end of suffering, ^snp3-3-8-3

this really is the best kind of speech.” ^snp3-3-8-4

### With Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā on the Sacrificial Cake (Anthology of Discourses 3.4) ^snp3-4-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-4-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying in the Kosalan lands on the bank of the Sundarikā river. ^snp3-4-1-2

Now at that time the brahmin Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā was serving the sacred flame and performing the fire sacrifice on the bank of the Sundarikā. ^snp3-4-1-3

Then he rose from his seat and surveyed the four quarters, wondering, ^snp3-4-1-4

“Now who might eat the leftovers of this oblation?” ^snp3-4-1-5

He saw the Buddha meditating at the root of a certain tree with his robe pulled over his head. ^snp3-4-1-6

Taking the leftovers of the oblation in his left hand and a pitcher in the right he approached the Buddha. ^snp3-4-1-7

When he heard Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā’s footsteps the Buddha uncovered his head. ^snp3-4-2-1

Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā thought, “This man is shaven, he is shaven!” And he wanted to turn back. ^snp3-4-2-2

But he thought, ^snp3-4-2-3

“Even some brahmins are shaven. Why don’t I go to him and ask about his birth?” ^snp3-4-2-4

Then the brahmin Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā went up to the Buddha, and said to him, ^snp3-4-2-5

“Sir, what are you by birth?” ^snp3-4-2-6

Then the Buddha addressed Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā in verse: ^snp3-4-3-1

“I am no brahmin, nor am I a ruler, ^snp3-4-4-1

nor peasant nor anything else. ^snp3-4-4-2

Fully understanding the clan of ordinary people, ^snp3-4-4-3

I wander in the world owning nothing, reflective. ^snp3-4-4-4

Clad in my cloak, I wander without home, ^snp3-4-5-1

my hair shorn, quenched. ^snp3-4-5-2

Since I’m unburdened by the sons of Manu, ^snp3-4-5-3

it’s inappropriate to ask me about clan.” ^snp3-4-5-4

“Actually sir, when brahmins meet they politely ^snp3-4-6-1

ask each other whether they are brahmins.” ^snp3-4-6-2

“Well, if you say that you’re a brahmin, ^snp3-4-7-1

and that I am not, ^snp3-4-7-2

I shall question you on that Sāvitrī Prayer, ^snp3-4-7-3

with its three lines and twenty-four syllables.” ^snp3-4-7-4

“On what grounds have seers and men, ^snp3-4-8-1

aristocrats and brahmins here in the world ^snp3-4-8-2

performed so many different sacrifices to the gods?” ^snp3-4-8-3

“During a sacrifice, should a past-master, <j>a knowledge master, ^snp3-4-9-1

receive an oblation, it profits the donor, I say.” ^snp3-4-9-2

“Then clearly my oblation will be profitable,” ^snp3-4-10-1

said the brahmin, ^snp3-4-10-2

“since I have met such a knowledge master. ^snp3-4-10-3

It’s because I’d never met anyone like you ^snp3-4-10-4

that others ate the sacrificial cake.” ^snp3-4-10-5

“So then, brahmin, since you have approached me ^snp3-4-11-1

as a seeker of the good, ask. ^snp3-4-11-2

Perhaps you may find here someone intelligent, ^snp3-4-11-3

peaceful, unclouded, untroubled, <j>with no need for hope.” ^snp3-4-11-4

“Worthy Gotama, I like to sacrifice ^snp3-4-12-1

and wish to perform a sacrifice. Please advise me, ^snp3-4-12-2

for I do not understand ^snp3-4-12-3

where an oblation is profitable; tell me this.” ^snp3-4-12-4

“Well then, brahmin, listen up, I will teach you the Dhamma. ^snp3-4-13-1

Don’t ask about birth, ask about conduct; ^snp3-4-14-1

for the fire of Jātaveda is born from sticks. ^snp3-4-14-2

A steadfast sage, even though from a low class family, ^snp3-4-14-3

is a thoroughbred checked by conscience. ^snp3-4-14-4

Tamed by truth, fulfilled by taming, ^snp3-4-15-1

a complete knowledge master <j>who has completed the spiritual journey— ^snp3-4-15-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-4-15-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-4-15-4

Those who have left sensuality behind, <j>wandering homeless, ^snp3-4-16-1

self-controlled, straight as a shuttle— ^snp3-4-16-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-4-16-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-4-16-4

Those freed of greed, with senses stilled, ^snp3-4-17-1

like the moon released from the eclipse— ^snp3-4-17-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-4-17-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-4-17-4

They wander the world unobstructed, ^snp3-4-18-1

always mindful, calling nothing their own— ^snp3-4-18-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-4-18-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-4-18-4

Having left sensuality behind, <j>wandering triumphant, ^snp3-4-19-1

knowing the end of rebirth and death, ^snp3-4-19-2

quenched and cool as a lake: ^snp3-4-19-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-19-4

Good among the good, far from the bad, ^snp3-4-20-1

the Realized One has infinite wisdom. ^snp3-4-20-2

Unsullied in this life and the next: ^snp3-4-20-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-20-4

In whom dwells no deceit or conceit, ^snp3-4-21-1

rid of greed, unselfish, with no need for hope, ^snp3-4-21-2

with anger cast aside, quenched, ^snp3-4-21-3

a brahmin rid of sorrow’s stain: ^snp3-4-21-4

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-21-5

He has given up the mind’s home, ^snp3-4-22-1

and has no possessions at all. ^snp3-4-22-2

Not grasping to this life or the next: ^snp3-4-22-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-22-4

Serene, he has crossed the flood, ^snp3-4-23-1

and has understood the teaching with ultimate view. ^snp3-4-23-2

With defilements ended, bearing his final body: ^snp3-4-23-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-23-4

In whom desire to be reborn, and caustic speech ^snp3-4-24-1

are cleared and ended, and are no more; ^snp3-4-24-2

that knowledge master, everywhere free: ^snp3-4-24-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-24-4

He has slipped his chains, he’s chained no more, ^snp3-4-25-1

among those caught in conceit he is free of conceit; ^snp3-4-25-2

he has fully understood suffering <j>with its field and ground: ^snp3-4-25-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-25-4

Not relying on hope, seeing seclusion, ^snp3-4-26-1

well past the views proclaimed by others. ^snp3-4-26-2

In him there are no supporting conditions at all: ^snp3-4-26-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-26-4

He has comprehended all things, high and low, ^snp3-4-27-1

cleared them and ended them, so they are no more. ^snp3-4-27-2

Peaceful, freed in the ending of grasping: ^snp3-4-27-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-27-4

He sees the ending of rebirth’s fetter, ^snp3-4-28-1

and has cast aside all manner of desire. ^snp3-4-28-2

Pure, stainless, immaculate, flawless: ^snp3-4-28-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-28-4

Not seeing himself in terms of a self, ^snp3-4-29-1

he is stilled, sincere, and steadfast. ^snp3-4-29-2

Imperturbable, kind, wishless: ^snp3-4-29-3

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake. ^snp3-4-29-4

He harbors no delusions within at all, ^snp3-4-30-1

he has insight into all things. ^snp3-4-30-2

He bears his final body, ^snp3-4-30-3

attained to the state of grace, the supreme awakening. ^snp3-4-30-4

That’s how the purity of a spirit is defined: ^snp3-4-30-5

the Realized One is worthy of the sacrificial cake.” ^snp3-4-30-6

“Let my oblation be a true offering, ^snp3-4-31-1

since I have found such a knowledge master! ^snp3-4-31-2

I see divinity in person! <j>Accept my offering, Blessed One: ^snp3-4-31-3

please eat my sacrificial cake.” ^snp3-4-31-4

“Food enchanted by a verse isn’t fit for me to eat. ^snp3-4-32-1

That’s not the principle of those who see, brahmin. ^snp3-4-32-2

The Buddhas cast aside things enchanted with verses. ^snp3-4-32-3

Since there is such a principle, brahmin, <j>that’s how they live. ^snp3-4-32-4

Serve with other food and drink ^snp3-4-33-1

the consummate one, the great seer, ^snp3-4-33-2

with defilements ended and remorse stilled. ^snp3-4-33-3

For he is the field for the seeker of merit.” ^snp3-4-33-4

“Please, Blessed One, help me understand: ^snp3-4-34-1

now that I have found your teaching, ^snp3-4-34-2

when I look for someone during a sacrifice, ^snp3-4-34-3

who should eat the <j>religious donation of one like me?” ^snp3-4-34-4

“One who is rid of aggression, ^snp3-4-35-1

whose mind is unclouded, ^snp3-4-35-2

who is liberated from sensual pleasures, ^snp3-4-35-3

and who has cast aside dullness. ^snp3-4-35-4

One who has erased perimeters and limits, ^snp3-4-36-1

expert in birth and death, ^snp3-4-36-2

a sage, blessed with sagacity. ^snp3-4-36-3

When such a person comes to the sacrifice, ^snp3-4-36-4

get rid of your scowl! ^snp3-4-37-1

Honor them with cupped palms, ^snp3-4-37-2

and venerate them with food and drink, ^snp3-4-37-3

and in this way your religious donation will succeed.” ^snp3-4-37-4

“The Buddha is worthy of the sacrificial cake, ^snp3-4-38-1

he is the supreme field of merit, ^snp3-4-38-2

recipient of gifts from the whole world; ^snp3-4-38-3

what’s given to the worthy one is very fruitful.” ^snp3-4-38-4

Then the brahmin Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā said to the Buddha, ^snp3-4-39-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! ^snp3-4-39-2

As if he were righting the overturned, or revealing the hidden, or pointing out the path to the lost, or lighting a lamp in the dark so people with clear eyes can see what’s there, worthy Gotama has made the teaching clear in many ways. ^snp3-4-39-3

I go for refuge to the worthy Gotama, to the teaching, and to the mendicant Saṅgha. ^snp3-4-39-4

May I receive the going forth, the ordination in the worthy Gotama’s presence?” ^snp3-4-39-5

And the brahmin Bhāradvāja of Sundarikā received the going forth, the ordination in the Buddha’s presence. ^snp3-4-39-6

And soon after, he became one of the perfected. ^snp3-4-39-7

### With Māgha (Anthology of Discourses 3.5) ^snp3-5-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-5-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Rājagaha, on the Vulture’s Peak Mountain. ^snp3-5-1-2

Then the student Māgha approached the Buddha and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp3-5-1-3

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, he sat down to one side, ^snp3-5-1-4

and said to the Buddha: ^snp3-5-1-5

“I’m a giver, worthy Gotama, a donor; I am bountiful and committed to charity. ^snp3-5-2-1

I seek wealth in a principled manner, ^snp3-5-2-2

and with that legitimate wealth I give to one person, to two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten, twenty, thirty, forty, fifty, a hundred people or even more. ^snp3-5-2-3

Giving and sacrificing like this, worthy Gotama, do I brim with much merit?” ^snp3-5-2-4

“Indeed you do, student. ^snp3-5-3-1

A giver or donor who is bountiful and committed to charity, ^snp3-5-3-2

who seeks wealth in a principled manner, ^snp3-5-3-3

and with that legitimate wealth gives to one person, or up to a hundred people or even more, brims with much merit.” ^snp3-5-3-4

Then Māgha addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp3-5-3-5

“I ask the bountiful Gotama,” ^snp3-5-4-1

said Māgha, ^snp3-5-4-2

“wearing an ochre robe, wandering homeless. ^snp3-5-4-3

Suppose a lay donor who is committed to charity ^snp3-5-4-4

makes a sacrifice seeking merit, looking for merit, ^snp3-5-4-5

giving food and drink to others here: ^snp3-5-4-6

how is their offering purified?” ^snp3-5-4-7

“Suppose a lay donor who is committed to charity,” ^snp3-5-5-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp3-5-5-2

“makes a sacrifice seeking merit, looking for merit, ^snp3-5-5-3

giving food and drink to others here: ^snp3-5-5-4

such a one would succeed due to those <j>who are worthy of donations.” ^snp3-5-5-5

“Suppose a lay donor who is committed to charity,” ^snp3-5-6-1

said Māgha, ^snp3-5-6-2

“makes a sacrifice seeking merit, looking for merit, ^snp3-5-6-3

giving food and drink to others here: ^snp3-5-6-4

explain to me who is worthy of donations.” ^snp3-5-6-5

“Those who wander the world unattached, ^snp3-5-7-1

consummate, self-controlled, owning nothing: ^snp3-5-7-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-7-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-7-4

Those who have cut off all fetters and bonds, ^snp3-5-8-1

tamed, liberated, untroubled, with no need for hope: ^snp3-5-8-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-8-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-8-4

Those who are released from all fetters, ^snp3-5-9-1

tamed, liberated, untroubled, with no need for hope: ^snp3-5-9-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-9-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-9-4

Having given up greed, hate, and delusion, ^snp3-5-10-1

with defilements ended, <j>the spiritual journey completed: ^snp3-5-10-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-10-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-10-4

Those in whom dwells no deceit or conceit, ^snp3-5-11-1

with defilements ended, <j>the spiritual journey completed: ^snp3-5-11-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-11-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-11-4

Those rid of greed, unselfish, with no need for hope, ^snp3-5-12-1

with defilements ended, <j>the spiritual journey completed: ^snp3-5-12-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-12-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-12-4

Those not fallen prey to cravings, ^snp3-5-13-1

who, having crossed the flood, live unselfishly: ^snp3-5-13-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-13-3

should bestow a timely offering as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-13-4

Those with no craving at all in the world ^snp3-5-14-1

for any state of existence in this life or the next: ^snp3-5-14-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-14-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-14-4

Those who have left sensuality behind, <j>wandering homeless, ^snp3-5-15-1

self-controlled, straight as a shuttle: ^snp3-5-15-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-15-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-15-4

Those freed of greed, with senses stilled, ^snp3-5-16-1

like the moon released from the eclipse: ^snp3-5-16-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-16-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-16-4

Those assuaged ones free of greed and anger, ^snp3-5-17-1

for whom there are no destinies, <j>being rid of them in this life: ^snp3-5-17-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-17-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-17-4

They’ve given up rebirth and death completely, ^snp3-5-18-1

and have gone beyond all indecision: ^snp3-5-18-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-18-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-18-4

Those who live as their own island, ^snp3-5-19-1

everywhere free, owning nothing: ^snp3-5-19-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-19-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-19-4

Those here who know this to be true: ^snp3-5-20-1

‘This is my last life, there are no future lives’: ^snp3-5-20-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-20-3

should bestow a timely oblation as sacrifice. ^snp3-5-20-4

A knowledge master, loving absorption, mindful, ^snp3-5-21-1

who has reached awakening and is a refuge for many: ^snp3-5-21-2

that is where a brahmin seeking merit ^snp3-5-21-3

should bestow a timely offering as sacrifice.” ^snp3-5-21-4

“Clearly my questions were not in vain!” ^snp3-5-22-1

said Māgha, ^snp3-5-22-2

“The Buddha has explained to me <j>who is worthy of donations. ^snp3-5-22-3

You are the one here who knows this to be true, ^snp3-5-22-4

for truly you understand this matter. ^snp3-5-22-5

Suppose a lay donor who is committed to charity ^snp3-5-23-1

makes a sacrifice seeking merit, looking for merit, ^snp3-5-23-2

giving food and drink to others here: ^snp3-5-23-3

explain to me how to accomplish the sacrifice.” ^snp3-5-23-4

“Sacrifice, and while doing so,” ^snp3-5-24-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp3-5-24-2

“be clear and confident in every way. ^snp3-5-24-3

Sacrifice is the ground standing upon which ^snp3-5-24-4

the sacrificer sheds their flaws. ^snp3-5-24-5

One free of greed, rid of anger, ^snp3-5-25-1

developing a heart of limitless love, ^snp3-5-25-2

spreads that limitlessness in every direction, ^snp3-5-25-3

ever diligent day and night.” ^snp3-5-25-4

“Who is purified, freed, awake? ^snp3-5-26-1

How can one go to the realm of divinity oneself? ^snp3-5-26-2

I do not know, so please tell me when asked, ^snp3-5-26-3

for the Buddha is the divinity I see in person today! ^snp3-5-26-4

To us you are truly the equal of divinity. ^snp3-5-26-5

Brilliant One, how is one reborn in the realm of divinity?” ^snp3-5-26-6

“One who accomplishes the sacrifice <j>with three modes,” ^snp3-5-27-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp3-5-27-2

“such a one would succeed <j>due to those who are worthy of donations. ^snp3-5-27-3

Sacrificing like this, one rightly committed to charity ^snp3-5-27-4

is reborn in the realm of divinity, I say.” ^snp3-5-27-5

When he had spoken, the student Māgha said to the Buddha, ^snp3-5-28-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! … ^snp3-5-28-2

From this day forth, may the worthy Gotama remember me as a lay follower who has gone for refuge for life.” ^snp3-5-28-3

### With Sabhiya (Anthology of Discourses 3.6) ^snp3-6-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-6-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Rājagaha, in the Bamboo Grove, the squirrels’ feeding ground. ^snp3-6-1-2

Now at that time the wanderer Sabhiya had been presented with several questions by a deity who was a former blood relation, saying: ^snp3-6-1-3

“Sabhiya, you should live the spiritual life with whatever ascetic or brahmin answers these questions.” ^snp3-6-1-4

Then Sabhiya, after learning those questions in the presence of that deity, approached those ascetics and brahmins who led an order and a community, and taught a community, who were well-known and famous religious founders, deemed holy by many people. That is, ^snp3-6-2-1

Pūraṇa Kassapa, the bamboo-staffed ascetic Gosāla, Ajita of the hair blanket, Pakudha Kaccāyana, Sañjaya Belaṭṭhiputta, and the Jain ascetic of the Ñātika clan. ^snp3-6-2-2

And he asked them those questions, ^snp3-6-2-3

but they were stumped by them. ^snp3-6-2-4

Displaying annoyance, hate, and bitterness, ^snp3-6-2-5

they questioned Sabhiya in return. ^snp3-6-2-6

Then Sabhiya thought, ^snp3-6-3-1

“Those famous ascetics and brahmins ^snp3-6-3-2

were stumped by my questions. ^snp3-6-3-4

Displaying annoyance, hate, and bitterness, ^snp3-6-3-5

they questioned me in return on that matter. ^snp3-6-3-6

Why don’t I return to a lesser life so I can enjoy sensual pleasures?” ^snp3-6-3-7

Then Sabhiya thought, ^snp3-6-4-1

“This ascetic Gotama also leads an order and a community, and tutors a community. He’s a well-known and famous religious founder, deemed holy by many people. ^snp3-6-4-2

Why don’t I ask him these questions?” ^snp3-6-4-3

Then he thought, ^snp3-6-5-1

“Even those ascetics and brahmins who are elderly and senior, who are advanced in years and have reached the final stage of life; who are senior, long standing, long gone forth; who lead an order and a community, and tutor a community; who are well-known and famous religious founders, deemed holy by many people—that is ^snp3-6-5-2

Pūraṇa Kassapa and the rest— ^snp3-6-5-3

were stumped by my questions. ^snp3-6-5-4

They displayed annoyance, hate, and bitterness, and even questioned me in return. ^snp3-6-5-5

How can the ascetic Gotama possibly answer my questions, ^snp3-6-5-6

since he is so young in age and newly gone forth?” ^snp3-6-5-7

Then he thought, ^snp3-6-6-1

“An ascetic should not be looked down upon or disparaged because they are young. ^snp3-6-6-2

Though young, the ascetic Gotama has great psychic power and might. Why don’t I ask him these questions?” ^snp3-6-6-3

Then Sabhiya set out for Rājagaha. ^snp3-6-7-1

Traveling stage by stage, he came to Rājagaha, the Bamboo Grove, the squirrels’ feeding ground. He went up to the Buddha and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp3-6-7-2

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, he sat down to one side, ^snp3-6-7-3

and addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp3-6-7-4

“I’ve come full of  doubts and uncertainties,” ^snp3-6-8-1

said Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-8-2

“wishing to ask some questions. ^snp3-6-8-3

Please solve them for me. ^snp3-6-8-4

Answer my questions in turn, <j>in accordance with the truth.” ^snp3-6-8-5

“You have come from afar, Sabhiya,” ^snp3-6-9-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-6-9-2

“wishing to ask some questions. ^snp3-6-9-3

I shall solve them for you, ^snp3-6-9-4

answering your questions in turn, <j>in accordance with the truth. ^snp3-6-9-5

Ask me your question, Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-10-1

whatever you want. ^snp3-6-10-2

I’ll solve each and every ^snp3-6-10-3

question you have.” ^snp3-6-10-4

Then Sabhiya thought, ^snp3-6-11-1

“Oh lord, how incredible, how amazing! ^snp3-6-11-2

Where those other ascetics and brahmins didn’t even give me a chance, the Buddha has invited me.” ^snp3-6-11-3

Uplifted and elated, full of rapture and happiness, he asked this question. ^snp3-6-11-4

“What must one attain to be called a mendicant?” ^snp3-6-12-1

said Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-12-2

“How is one ‘gentle’, how said to be ‘tamed’? ^snp3-6-12-3

How is one declared to be ‘awakened’? ^snp3-6-12-4

May the Buddha please answer my question.” ^snp3-6-12-5

“When by the path they have walked themselves,” ^snp3-6-13-1

said the Buddha to Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-13-2

“they reach extinguishment, with doubt overcome; ^snp3-6-13-3

giving up desire for existence or nonexistence, ^snp3-6-13-4

their journey complete, their rebirths ended: <j>that is a mendicant. ^snp3-6-13-5

Equanimous towards everything, mindful, ^snp3-6-14-1

they don’t harm anyone in the world. ^snp3-6-14-2

An ascetic who has crossed over, unclouded, ^snp3-6-14-3

who has no pretensions, is gentle. ^snp3-6-14-4

Their faculties have been developed ^snp3-6-15-1

inside and out in the whole world. ^snp3-6-15-2

Having pierced through this world and the next, ^snp3-6-15-3

tamed, they await their time. ^snp3-6-15-4

They have examined the eons in their entirety, ^snp3-6-16-1

and both sides of transmigration—<j>passing away and rebirth. ^snp3-6-16-2

Rid of dust, unblemished, purified: ^snp3-6-16-3

the one they call ‘awakened’ has attained <j>the end of rebirth.” ^snp3-6-16-4

And then, having approved and agreed with what the Buddha said, uplifted and elated, full of rapture and happiness, Sabhiya asked another question: ^snp3-6-17-1

“What must one attain to be called ‘brahmin’?” ^snp3-6-18-1

said Sabhiya. ^snp3-6-18-2

“Why is one an ‘ascetic’, and how a ‘bathed initiate’? ^snp3-6-18-3

How is one declared to be a ‘giant’? ^snp3-6-18-4

May the Buddha please answer my question.” ^snp3-6-18-5

“Having banished all bad things,” ^snp3-6-19-1

said the Buddha to Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-19-2

“immaculate, well-composed, steadfast, ^snp3-6-19-3

consummate, they’ve left transmigration behind: ^snp3-6-19-4

unattached, one such is called ‘brahmin’. ^snp3-6-19-5

An assuaged one who has given up good and evil, ^snp3-6-20-1

stainless, understanding this world and the next, ^snp3-6-20-2

gone beyond rebirth and death: ^snp3-6-20-3

one such is rightly called ‘ascetic’. ^snp3-6-20-4

Having washed off all bad things ^snp3-6-21-1

inside and out in the whole world, ^snp3-6-21-2

among gods and humans bound to creations, ^snp3-6-21-3

the one they call ‘washed’ does not return to creation. ^snp3-6-21-4

They do nothing monstrous at all in the world, ^snp3-6-22-1

discarding all yokes and bonds, ^snp3-6-22-2

everywhere not stuck, freed: ^snp3-6-22-3

one such is rightly called ‘giant’.” ^snp3-6-22-4

And then Sabhiya asked another question: ^snp3-6-23-1

“Who is a ‘field-victor’ according to the Buddhas?” ^snp3-6-24-1

said Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-24-2

“Why is one ‘skillful’, and how ‘a wise scholar’? ^snp3-6-24-3

How is one declared to be a ‘sage’? ^snp3-6-24-4

May the Buddha please answer my question.” ^snp3-6-24-5

“They are victors on the fields of deeds in their entirety,” ^snp3-6-25-1

said the Buddha to Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-25-2

“the fields of gods, humans, and divinities; ^snp3-6-25-3

released from the root bondage to all fields: ^snp3-6-25-4

one such is rightly called ‘field-victor’. ^snp3-6-25-5

They have examined the coffers in their entirety, ^snp3-6-26-1

the coffers of gods, humans, and divinities; ^snp3-6-26-2

released from the root bondage to all coffers: ^snp3-6-26-3

one such is rightly called ‘skillful’. ^snp3-6-26-4

They have examined whiteness ^snp3-6-27-1

both inside and out; understanding purity, ^snp3-6-27-2

they have left dark and bright behind: ^snp3-6-27-3

one such is rightly called ‘a wise scholar’. ^snp3-6-27-4

Understanding the nature of the bad and the good ^snp3-6-28-1

inside and out in the whole world; ^snp3-6-28-2

one worthy of honor by gods and humans, ^snp3-6-28-3

who has escaped from the net and the chain: that is a sage.” ^snp3-6-28-4

And then Sabhiya asked another question: ^snp3-6-29-1

“What must one attain <j>to be called ‘knowledge master’?” ^snp3-6-30-1

said Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-30-2

“Why is one ‘studied’, and how is one ‘heroic’? ^snp3-6-30-3

How to gain the name ‘thoroughbred’? ^snp3-6-30-4

May the Buddha please answer my question.” ^snp3-6-30-5

“They have examined knowledges in their entirety,” ^snp3-6-31-1

said the Buddha to Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-31-2

“those that are current among ascetics and brahmins; ^snp3-6-31-3

rid of greed for all feelings, ^snp3-6-31-4

having left all knowledges behind: <j>that is a knowledge master. ^snp3-6-31-5

Having studied proliferation and name & form ^snp3-6-32-1

inside and out—the root of disease; ^snp3-6-32-2

released from the root bondage to all disease: ^snp3-6-32-3

one such is rightly called ‘studied’. ^snp3-6-32-4

Refraining from all evil here, ^snp3-6-33-1

heroic, he escapes from the suffering of hell; ^snp3-6-33-2

he is heroic and energetic: ^snp3-6-33-3

one such is rightly called ‘hero’. ^snp3-6-33-4

Whoever’s bonds are cut, ^snp3-6-34-1

the root of clinging inside and out; ^snp3-6-34-2

released from the root bondage to all clinging: ^snp3-6-34-3

one such is rightly called ‘thoroughbred’.” ^snp3-6-34-4

And then Sabhiya asked another question: ^snp3-6-35-1

“What must one attain to be called ‘scholar’?” ^snp3-6-36-1

said Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-36-2

“Why is one ‘noble’, and how is one ‘well conducted’? ^snp3-6-36-3

How to gain the name ‘wanderer’? ^snp3-6-36-4

May the Buddha please answer my question.” ^snp3-6-36-5

“One who has learned every teaching,” ^snp3-6-37-1

said the Buddha to Sabhiya, ^snp3-6-37-2

“and has known for themselves what in the world <j>is blameworthy and blameless; ^snp3-6-37-3

a vanquisher, decided, liberated, ^snp3-6-37-4

untroubled everywhere: they call them ‘scholar’. ^snp3-6-37-5

Having cut off defilements and clingings, ^snp3-6-38-1

being wise, they enter no womb. ^snp3-6-38-2

They’ve cast aside the bog of the three perceptions, ^snp3-6-38-3

the one they call ‘noble’ does not return to creation. ^snp3-6-38-4

One here who is accomplished <j>and skillful in all forms of good conduct; ^snp3-6-39-1

always understanding the teaching, ^snp3-6-39-2

everywhere not stuck, freed in mind, ^snp3-6-39-3

who has no aversion: they are ‘well-conducted’. ^snp3-6-39-4

They wander full of wisdom, ^snp3-6-40-1

having banished any deed that results in suffering, ^snp3-6-40-2

deceit and conceit, as well as greed and anger, ^snp3-6-40-3

above, below, all round, between. ^snp3-6-40-4

They have made a limit on name and form; ^snp3-6-40-5

the one they call a ‘wanderer’ <j>has reached their destination.” ^snp3-6-40-6

And then, having approved and agreed with what the Buddha said, uplifted and elated, full of rapture and happiness, Sabhiya rose from his seat, arranged his robe over one shoulder, raised his cupped palms toward the Buddha, and extolled the Buddha in his presence with fitting verses: ^snp3-6-41-1

“O one of vast wisdom, <j>there are three and sixty opinions ^snp3-6-42-1

based on the doctrines of ascetics: ^snp3-6-42-2

they are expressions of perception, <j>based on perception. ^snp3-6-42-3

Having dispelled them all, <j>you passed over the dark flood. ^snp3-6-42-4

You have gone to the end, gone beyond suffering, ^snp3-6-43-1

you are perfected, a fully awakened Buddha; <j>I think you have ended defilements. ^snp3-6-43-2

Brilliant, intelligent, abounding in wisdom, ^snp3-6-43-3

ender of suffering—you brought me across! ^snp3-6-43-4

When you understood my uncertainty, ^snp3-6-44-1

you brought me beyond doubt—homage to you! ^snp3-6-44-2

A sage, accomplished in the ways of sagacity, ^snp3-6-44-3

you are gentle, not hardhearted, O Kinsman of the Sun. ^snp3-6-44-4

Any doubts that I once had, ^snp3-6-45-1

you have answered for me, O Clear-eyed One. ^snp3-6-45-2

Clearly you are a sage, an Awakened One, ^snp3-6-45-3

there are no hindrances in you. ^snp3-6-45-4

All your distress ^snp3-6-46-1

is blown away and mown down. ^snp3-6-46-2

Cooled, tamed, steadfast: ^snp3-6-46-3

truth is your strength. ^snp3-6-46-4

O giant among giants, O great hero, ^snp3-6-47-1

when you are speaking ^snp3-6-47-2

all the gods rejoice, ^snp3-6-47-3

including both Nārada and Pabbata. ^snp3-6-47-4

Homage to you, O thoroughbred! ^snp3-6-48-1

Homage to you, supreme among men! ^snp3-6-48-2

In the world with its gods, ^snp3-6-48-3

you have no rival. ^snp3-6-48-4

You are the Buddha, you are the Teacher, ^snp3-6-49-1

you are the sage who has overcome Māra; ^snp3-6-49-2

you have cut off the underlying tendencies, ^snp3-6-49-3

you’ve crossed over, and you bring humanity across. ^snp3-6-49-4

You have transcended attachments, ^snp3-6-50-1

your defilements are shattered; ^snp3-6-50-2

you are a lion, free of grasping, ^snp3-6-50-3

with fear and dread given up. ^snp3-6-50-4

Like a graceful lotus ^snp3-6-51-1

to which water does not stick, ^snp3-6-51-2

so both good and evil ^snp3-6-51-3

do not stick to you. ^snp3-6-51-4

Stretch out your feet, great hero: ^snp3-6-51-5

Sabhiya bows to the Teacher.” ^snp3-6-51-6

Then the wanderer Sabhiya bowed with his head at the Buddha’s feet and said, ^snp3-6-52-1

“Excellent, sir! Excellent! … ^snp3-6-52-2

I go for refuge to the Buddha, to the teaching, and to the mendicant Saṅgha. ^snp3-6-52-3

Sir, may I receive the going forth, the ordination in the Buddha’s presence?” ^snp3-6-52-4

“Sabhiya, if someone formerly ordained in another sect wishes to take the going forth, the ordination in this teaching and training, they must spend four months on probation. When four months have passed, if the mendicants are satisfied, they’ll give the going forth, the ordination into monkhood. ^snp3-6-53-1

However, I recognize individual differences in this matter.” ^snp3-6-53-2

“Sir, if four months probation are required in such a case, I’ll spend four years on probation. When four years have passed, if the mendicants are satisfied, let them give me the going forth, the ordination into monkhood.” ^snp3-6-54-1

And the wanderer Sabhiya received the going forth, the ordination in the Buddha’s presence. ^snp3-6-54-2

And Venerable Sabhiya became one of the perfected. ^snp3-6-54-3

### With Sela (Anthology of Discourses 3.7) ^snp3-7-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-7-1-1

At one time the Buddha was wandering together with a large Saṅgha of 1,250 mendicants in the land of the Aṅguttarāpans when he arrived at a town of theirs named Āpaṇa. ^snp3-7-1-2

The matted-hair ascetic Keṇiya heard: ^snp3-7-1-3

“It seems the ascetic Gotama—a Sakyan, gone forth from a Sakyan family—has arrived at Āpaṇa, together with a large Saṅgha of 1,250 mendicants. ^snp3-7-1-4

He has this good reputation: ^snp3-7-1-5

‘That Blessed One is perfected, a fully awakened Buddha, accomplished in knowledge and conduct, holy, knower of the world, supreme guide for those fit for training, teacher of gods and humans, awakened, blessed.’ ^snp3-7-1-6

He has realized with his own insight this world—with its gods, Māras, and divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, gods and humans—and he makes it known to others. ^snp3-7-1-7

He proclaims a teaching that’s good in the beginning, good in the middle, and good in the end, meaningful and well-phrased. And he reveals a spiritual practice that’s entirely full and pure. ^snp3-7-1-8

It’s good to see such perfected ones.” ^snp3-7-1-9

So Keṇiya approached the Buddha and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp3-7-2-1

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, he sat down to one side. ^snp3-7-2-2

The Buddha educated, encouraged, fired up, and inspired him with a Dhamma talk. ^snp3-7-2-3

Then he said to the Buddha, ^snp3-7-2-4

“Would Mister Gotama together with the mendicant Saṅgha please accept tomorrow’s meal from me?” ^snp3-7-2-5

When he said this, the Buddha said to him, ^snp3-7-2-6

“The Saṅgha is large, Keṇiya; there are 1,250 mendicants. And you are devoted to the brahmins.” ^snp3-7-2-7

For a second time, Keṇiya asked the Buddha to accept a meal offering. ^snp3-7-3-1

“Never mind that the Saṅgha is large, with 1,250 mendicants, and that I am devoted to the brahmins. ^snp3-7-3-2

Would Mister Gotama together with the mendicant Saṅgha please accept tomorrow’s meal from me?” ^snp3-7-3-3

And for a second time, the Buddha gave the same reply. ^snp3-7-3-4

For a third time, Keṇiya asked the Buddha to accept a meal offering. ^snp3-7-4-1

“Never mind that the Saṅgha is large, with 1,250 mendicants, and that I am devoted to the brahmins. ^snp3-7-4-2

Would Mister Gotama together with the mendicant Saṅgha please accept tomorrow’s meal from me?” ^snp3-7-4-3

The Buddha consented with silence. ^snp3-7-4-4

Then, knowing that the Buddha had consented, Keṇiya rose from his seat and went to his own hermitage. There he addressed his friends and colleagues, relatives and kin, ^snp3-7-4-5

“My friends and colleagues, relatives and kin: please listen! ^snp3-7-4-6

The ascetic Gotama together with the mendicant Saṅgha has been invited by me for tomorrow’s meal. ^snp3-7-4-7

Please help me out with the manual preparations.” ^snp3-7-4-8

“Yes, worthy sir,” they replied. Some dug ovens, some chopped wood, some washed dishes, some set out a water jar, and some spread out seats. ^snp3-7-4-9

Meanwhile, Keṇiya set up the pavilion himself. ^snp3-7-4-10

Now at that time the brahmin Sela was residing in Āpaṇa. He had mastered the three Vedas, together with their vocabularies and ritual performance, their phonology and word classification, and the testaments as fifth. He knew them word-by-word, and their grammar. He was well versed in cosmology and the marks of a great man. And he was teaching three hundred young students to recite the hymns. ^snp3-7-5-1

And at that time Keṇiya was devoted to Sela. ^snp3-7-6-1

Then Sela, while going for a walk escorted by the three hundred young students, approached Keṇiya’s hermitage. ^snp3-7-6-2

He saw the preparations going on, ^snp3-7-6-3

and said to Keṇiya, ^snp3-7-6-4

“Keṇiya, is your son or daughter being married? Or are you setting up a big sacrifice? Or has King Seniya Bimbisāra of Magadha been invited for tomorrow’s meal?” ^snp3-7-6-5

“There is no marriage, Sela, and the king is not coming. ^snp3-7-7-1

Rather, I am setting up a big sacrifice. ^snp3-7-7-2

The ascetic Gotama has arrived at Āpaṇa, together with a large Saṅgha of 1,250 mendicants. ^snp3-7-7-3

He has this good reputation: ^snp3-7-7-4

‘That Blessed One is perfected, a fully awakened Buddha, accomplished in knowledge and conduct, holy, knower of the world, supreme guide for those fit for training, teacher of gods and humans, awakened, blessed.’ ^snp3-7-7-5

He has been invited by me for tomorrow’s meal together with the mendicant Saṅgha.” ^snp3-7-7-6

“Worthy Keṇiya, did you say ‘the awakened one’?” ^snp3-7-7-7

“I said ‘the awakened one’.” ^snp3-7-7-8

“Worthy Keṇiya, did you say ‘the awakened one’?” ^snp3-7-7-9

“I said ‘the awakened one’.” ^snp3-7-7-10

Then Sela thought, ^snp3-7-8-1

“It’s hard to even find the word ‘awakened one’ in the world. ^snp3-7-8-2

The thirty-two marks of a great man have been handed down in our hymns. A great man who possesses these has only two possible destinies, no other. ^snp3-7-8-3

If he stays at home he becomes a king, a wheel-turning monarch, a just and principled king. His dominion extends to all four sides, he achieves stability in the country, and he possesses the seven treasures. ^snp3-7-8-4

He has the following seven treasures: ^snp3-7-8-5

the wheel, the elephant, the horse, the jewel, the woman, the householder, and the commander as the seventh treasure. ^snp3-7-8-6

He has over a thousand sons who are valiant and heroic, crushing the armies of his enemies. ^snp3-7-8-7

After conquering this land girt by sea, he reigns by principle, without rod or sword. ^snp3-7-8-8

But if he goes forth from the lay life to homelessness, he becomes a perfected one, a fully awakened Buddha, who has cast off the world’s veil. ^snp3-7-8-9

“But Keṇiya, where is the Blessed One at present, the perfected one, the fully awakened Buddha?” ^snp3-7-8-10

When he said this, Keṇiya pointed with his right arm and said, ^snp3-7-9-1

“There, worthy Sela, at that line of blue forest.” ^snp3-7-9-2

Then Sela, together with his young students, approached the Buddha. ^snp3-7-9-3

He said to his young students, ^snp3-7-9-4

“Come quietly, gentlemen, tread gently. ^snp3-7-9-5

For the Buddhas are intimidating, like a lion living alone. ^snp3-7-9-6

When I’m consulting with the ascetic Gotama, don’t interrupt. ^snp3-7-9-7

Wait until I’ve finished speaking.” ^snp3-7-9-8

Then Sela went up to the Buddha, and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp3-7-10-1

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, he sat down to one side. ^snp3-7-10-2

and scrutinized the Buddha’s body for the thirty-two marks of a great man. ^snp3-7-10-3

He saw all of them except for two, ^snp3-7-10-4

which he had doubts about: ^snp3-7-10-5

whether the private parts were sheathed in a foreskin, and the largeness of the tongue. ^snp3-7-10-6

Then it occurred to the Buddha, ^snp3-7-11-1

“Sela sees all the marks except for two, ^snp3-7-11-2

which he has doubts about: ^snp3-7-11-3

whether the private parts are sheathed in a foreskin, and the largeness of the tongue.” ^snp3-7-11-4

The Buddha used his psychic power to will that Sela would see his private parts sheathed in a foreskin. ^snp3-7-11-5

And he stuck out his tongue and stroked back and forth on his ear holes and nostrils, and covered his entire forehead with his tongue. ^snp3-7-11-6

Then Sela thought, ^snp3-7-12-1

“The ascetic Gotama possesses the thirty-two marks completely, lacking none. ^snp3-7-12-2

But I don’t know whether or not he is an awakened one. ^snp3-7-12-3

I have heard that brahmins of the past who were elderly and senior, the tutors of tutors, said, ^snp3-7-12-4

‘Those who are perfected ones, fully awakened Buddhas reveal themselves when praised.’ ^snp3-7-12-5

Why don’t I extoll him in his presence with fitting verses?” ^snp3-7-12-6

Then Sela extolled the Buddha in his presence with fitting verses: ^snp3-7-12-7

“O Blessed One, your body’s perfect, ^snp3-7-13-1

you’re radiant, handsome, lovely to behold; ^snp3-7-13-2

golden colored, ^snp3-7-13-3

with teeth so white; you’re strong. ^snp3-7-13-4

The characteristics ^snp3-7-14-1

of a handsome man, ^snp3-7-14-2

the marks of a great man, ^snp3-7-14-3

are all found on your body. ^snp3-7-14-4

Your eyes are clear, your face is fair, ^snp3-7-15-1

you’re formidable, upright, majestic. ^snp3-7-15-2

In the midst of the Saṅgha of ascetics, ^snp3-7-15-3

you shine like the sun. ^snp3-7-15-4

You’re a mendicant fine to see, ^snp3-7-16-1

with skin that shines like lustrous gold. ^snp3-7-16-2

But with such excellent appearance, ^snp3-7-16-3

what do you want with the ascetic life? ^snp3-7-16-4

You’re fit to be a king, ^snp3-7-17-1

a wheel-turning monarch, chief of charioteers, ^snp3-7-17-2

victorious in the four quarters, ^snp3-7-17-3

lord of the Black Plum Tree Land. ^snp3-7-17-4

Aristocrats, nobles, and kings ^snp3-7-18-1

ought follow your rule. ^snp3-7-18-2

Gotama, may you reign ^snp3-7-18-3

as king of kings, lord of mankind!” ^snp3-7-18-4

“I am a king, Sela”, ^snp3-7-19-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-7-19-2

“the supreme king of the teaching. ^snp3-7-19-3

By the teaching I roll forth the wheel ^snp3-7-19-4

which cannot be rolled back.” ^snp3-7-19-5

“You claim to be awakened,” ^snp3-7-20-1

said Sela the brahmin, ^snp3-7-20-2

“the supreme king of the teaching. ^snp3-7-20-3

‘I roll forth the teaching’: ^snp3-7-20-4

so you say, Gotama. ^snp3-7-20-5

Then who is your general, ^snp3-7-21-1

the disciple who follows the Teacher’s way? ^snp3-7-21-2

Who keeps rolling the wheel ^snp3-7-21-3

of the teaching you rolled forth?” ^snp3-7-21-4

“By me the wheel was rolled forth,” ^snp3-7-22-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-7-22-2

“the supreme wheel of the teaching. ^snp3-7-22-3

Sāriputta, taking after the Realized One, ^snp3-7-22-4

keeps it rolling on. ^snp3-7-22-5

I have known what should be known, ^snp3-7-23-1

and developed what should be developed, ^snp3-7-23-2

and given up what should be given up: ^snp3-7-23-3

and so, brahmin, I am a Buddha. ^snp3-7-23-4

Dispel your doubt in me— ^snp3-7-24-1

make up your mind, brahmin! ^snp3-7-24-2

The sight of a Buddha ^snp3-7-24-3

is hard to find again. ^snp3-7-24-4

I am a Buddha, brahmin, ^snp3-7-25-1

the supreme surgeon, ^snp3-7-25-2

one of those whose appearance in the world ^snp3-7-25-3

is hard to find again. ^snp3-7-25-4

A manifestation of divinity, unequaled, ^snp3-7-26-1

crusher of Māra’s army; ^snp3-7-26-2

having subdued all my opponents, ^snp3-7-26-3

I rejoice, fearing nothing from any quarter.” ^snp3-7-26-4

“Pay heed, good fellows, to what ^snp3-7-27-1

is spoken by the Clear-eyed One. ^snp3-7-27-2

The surgeon, the great hero, ^snp3-7-27-3

roars like a lion in the jungle. ^snp3-7-27-4

A manifestation of divinity, unequaled, ^snp3-7-28-1

crusher of Māra’s army; ^snp3-7-28-2

who would not be inspired by him, ^snp3-7-28-3

even one born in a dark class? ^snp3-7-28-4

Those who wish may follow me; ^snp3-7-29-1

those who don’t may go. ^snp3-7-29-2

Right here, I’ll go forth in his presence, ^snp3-7-29-3

the one of such outstanding wisdom.” ^snp3-7-29-4

“Sir, if you endorse ^snp3-7-30-1

the teaching of the Buddha, ^snp3-7-30-2

we’ll also go forth in his presence, ^snp3-7-30-3

the one of such outstanding wisdom.” ^snp3-7-30-4

“These three hundred brahmins ^snp3-7-31-1

with cupped palms held up, ask: ^snp3-7-31-2

‘May we lead the spiritual life ^snp3-7-31-3

in your presence, Blessed One?’” ^snp3-7-31-4

“The spiritual life is well explained,” ^snp3-7-32-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-7-32-2

“apparent in the present life, immediately effective. ^snp3-7-32-3

Here the going forth isn’t in vain ^snp3-7-32-4

for one who trains with diligence.” ^snp3-7-32-5

And the brahmin Sela together with his assembly received the going forth, the ordination in the Buddha’s presence. ^snp3-7-33-1

And when the night had passed Keṇiya had delicious fresh and cooked foods prepared in his own hermitage. Then he had the Buddha informed of the time, saying, ^snp3-7-33-2

“It’s time, worthy Gotama, the meal is ready.” ^snp3-7-33-3

Then the Buddha robed up in the morning and, taking his bowl and robe, went to Keṇiya’s hermitage, where he sat on the seat spread out, together with the Saṅgha of mendicants. ^snp3-7-33-4

Then Keṇiya served and satisfied the mendicant Saṅgha headed by the Buddha with his own hands with delicious fresh and cooked foods. ^snp3-7-34-1

When the Buddha had eaten and washed his hand and bowl, Keṇiya took a low seat and sat to one side. ^snp3-7-34-2

The Buddha expressed his appreciation with these verses: ^snp3-7-34-3

“The foremost of sacrifices <j>is the offering to the sacred flame; ^snp3-7-35-1

the Sāvitrī is the foremost of prayer; ^snp3-7-35-2

of humans, the king is the foremost; ^snp3-7-35-3

the ocean’s the foremost of rivers; ^snp3-7-35-4

the foremost of stars is the moon; ^snp3-7-36-1

the sun is the foremost of blazes; ^snp3-7-36-2

for those who sacrifice seeking merit, ^snp3-7-36-3

the Saṅgha is the foremost.” ^snp3-7-36-4

When the Buddha had expressed his appreciation to Keṇiya the matted-hair ascetic with these verses, he rose from his seat and left. ^snp3-7-37-1

Then Venerable Sela and his assembly, living alone, withdrawn, diligent, keen, and resolute, soon realized the supreme end of the spiritual path in this very life. They lived having achieved with their own insight the goal for which gentlemen rightly go forth from the lay life to homelessness. ^snp3-7-37-2

And Venerable Sela together with his assembly became perfected. ^snp3-7-37-3

Then Sela with his assembly went to see the Buddha. He arranged his robe over one shoulder, raised his cupped palms toward the Buddha, and said: ^snp3-7-38-1

“This is the eighth day since ^snp3-7-39-1

we went for refuge, O Clear-eyed One. ^snp3-7-39-2

In these seven days, Blessed One, ^snp3-7-39-3

we’ve become tamed in your teaching. ^snp3-7-39-4

You are the Buddha, you are the Teacher, ^snp3-7-40-1

you are the sage who has overcome Māra; ^snp3-7-40-2

you have cut off the underlying tendencies, ^snp3-7-40-3

you’ve crossed over, and you bring humanity across. ^snp3-7-40-4

You have transcended attachments, ^snp3-7-41-1

your defilements are shattered; ^snp3-7-41-2

you are a lion, free of grasping, ^snp3-7-41-3

with fear and dread given up. ^snp3-7-41-4

These three hundred mendicants ^snp3-7-42-1

stand with cupped palms raised. ^snp3-7-42-2

Stretch out your feet, great hero: ^snp3-7-42-3

let these giants bow to the Teacher.” ^snp3-7-42-4

### The Dart (Anthology of Discourses 3.8) ^snp3-8-0

Unforeseen and unknown ^snp3-8-1-1

is the extent of this mortal life— ^snp3-8-1-2

hard and short ^snp3-8-1-3

and bound to pain. ^snp3-8-1-4

There is no way that ^snp3-8-2-1

those born will not die. ^snp3-8-2-2

On reaching old age death follows: ^snp3-8-2-3

such is the nature of living creatures. ^snp3-8-2-4

As ripe fruit ^snp3-8-3-1

are always in danger of falling, ^snp3-8-3-2

so mortals once born ^snp3-8-3-3

are always in danger of death. ^snp3-8-3-4

As clay pots ^snp3-8-4-1

made by a potter ^snp3-8-4-2

all end up being broken, ^snp3-8-4-3

so is the life of mortals. ^snp3-8-4-4

Young and old, ^snp3-8-5-1

foolish and wise— ^snp3-8-5-2

all go under the sway of death; ^snp3-8-5-3

all are destined to die. ^snp3-8-5-4

When those overcome by death ^snp3-8-6-1

leave this world for the next, ^snp3-8-6-2

a father cannot protect his son, ^snp3-8-6-3

nor relatives their kin. ^snp3-8-6-4

See how, while relatives look on, ^snp3-8-7-1

wailing profusely, ^snp3-8-7-2

mortals are led away one by one, ^snp3-8-7-3

like a cow to the slaughter. ^snp3-8-7-4

And so the world is stricken ^snp3-8-8-1

by old age and by death. ^snp3-8-8-2

That is why the attentive do not grieve, ^snp3-8-8-3

for they understand the way of the world. ^snp3-8-8-4

For one whose path you do not know— ^snp3-8-9-1

not whence they came nor where they went— ^snp3-8-9-2

you lament in vain, ^snp3-8-9-3

seeing neither end. ^snp3-8-9-4

If a bewildered person, ^snp3-8-10-1

lamenting and self-harming, ^snp3-8-10-2

could extract any good from that, ^snp3-8-10-3

then those who see clearly would do the same. ^snp3-8-10-4

For not by weeping and wailing ^snp3-8-11-1

will you find peace of heart. ^snp3-8-11-2

It just gives rise to more suffering, ^snp3-8-11-3

and distresses your body. ^snp3-8-11-4

Growing thin and pale, ^snp3-8-12-1

you hurt yourself. ^snp3-8-12-2

It does nothing to help the dead: ^snp3-8-12-3

your lamentation is in vain. ^snp3-8-12-4

Unless a personage gives up grief, ^snp3-8-13-1

they fall into suffering all the more. ^snp3-8-13-2

Bewailing those whose time has come, ^snp3-8-13-3

you fall under the sway of grief. ^snp3-8-13-4

See, too, other folk departing, ^snp3-8-14-1

passing on according to their deeds; ^snp3-8-14-2

fallen under the sway of death, ^snp3-8-14-3

beings flounder while still here. ^snp3-8-14-4

For whatever you imagine it is, ^snp3-8-15-1

it turns out to be something else. ^snp3-8-15-2

Such is separation: ^snp3-8-15-3

see the way of the world! ^snp3-8-15-4

Even if a human lives ^snp3-8-16-1

a hundred years or more, ^snp3-8-16-2

they are parted from their family circle, ^snp3-8-16-3

they leave this life behind. ^snp3-8-16-4

Therefore, having learned from the Perfected One, ^snp3-8-17-1

dispel lamentation. ^snp3-8-17-2

Seeing the dead and departed, think: ^snp3-8-17-3

“I cannot escape this.” ^snp3-8-17-4

As one would extinguish ^snp3-8-18-1

a blazing refuge with water, ^snp3-8-18-2

so too an attentive one—a wise, ^snp3-8-18-3

astute, and skilled person— ^snp3-8-18-4

would swiftly blow away grief that comes up, ^snp3-8-18-5

like the wind a tuft of cotton. ^snp3-8-18-6

One who seeks their own happiness ^snp3-8-19-1

would pluck out the dart from themselves— ^snp3-8-19-2

the wailing and moaning, ^snp3-8-19-3

and sadness inside. ^snp3-8-19-4

With dart plucked out, unattached, ^snp3-8-20-1

having found peace of mind, ^snp3-8-20-2

overcoming all sorrow, ^snp3-8-20-3

one is sorrowless and quenched. ^snp3-8-20-4

### With Vāseṭṭha (Anthology of Discourses 3.9) ^snp3-9-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-9-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying in a forest near Icchānaṅgala. ^snp3-9-1-2

Now at that time several very well-known well-to-do brahmins were residing in Icchānaṅgala. They included ^snp3-9-1-3

the brahmins Caṅkī, Tārukkha, Pokkharasāti, Jānussoṇi, Todeyya, and others. ^snp3-9-1-4

Then as the students Vāseṭṭha and Bhāradvāja were going for a walk they began to discuss the question: ^snp3-9-1-5

“How do you become a brahmin?” ^snp3-9-1-6

Bhāradvāja said this: ^snp3-9-2-1

“When you’re well born on both your mother’s and father’s side, stemming from a clean vessel, with irrefutable and impeccable genealogy back to the seventh paternal generation— ^snp3-9-2-2

then you’re a brahmin.” ^snp3-9-2-3

Vāseṭṭha said this: ^snp3-9-3-1

“When you’re ethical and accomplished in doing your duties— ^snp3-9-3-2

then you’re a brahmin.” ^snp3-9-3-3

But neither was able to persuade the other. ^snp3-9-3-4

So Vāseṭṭha said to Bhāradvāja, ^snp3-9-4-1

“Worthy Bhāradvāja, the ascetic Gotama—a Sakyan, gone forth from a Sakyan family—is staying in a forest near Icchānaṅgala. ^snp3-9-4-2

He has this good reputation: ^snp3-9-4-3

‘That Blessed One is perfected, a fully awakened Buddha, accomplished in knowledge and conduct, holy, knower of the world, supreme guide for those fit for training, teacher of gods and humans, awakened, blessed.’ ^snp3-9-4-4

Come, let’s go to see him and ask him about this matter. ^snp3-9-4-5

As he answers, so we’ll remember it.” ^snp3-9-4-6

“Yes, worthy sir,” replied Bhāradvāja. ^snp3-9-4-7

So they went to the Buddha and exchanged greetings with him. ^snp3-9-5-1

When the greetings and polite conversation were over, they sat down to one side, ^snp3-9-5-2

and Vāseṭṭha addressed the Buddha in verse: ^snp3-9-5-3

“We’re both authorized masters ^snp3-9-6-1

of the three Vedas. ^snp3-9-6-2

I’m a student of Pokkharasāti, ^snp3-9-6-3

and he of Tārukkha. ^snp3-9-6-4

We’re fully qualified ^snp3-9-7-1

in all the Vedic experts teach. ^snp3-9-7-2

As philologists and grammarians, ^snp3-9-7-3

we match our tutors in recitation. ^snp3-9-7-4

We have a dispute ^snp3-9-8-1

regarding genealogy. ^snp3-9-8-2

For Bhāradvāja says that ^snp3-9-8-3

one is a brahmin due to birth, ^snp3-9-8-4
