but I declare it’s because of one’s deeds. ^snp3-9-8-5

Oh Clear-eyed One, know this as our debate. ^snp3-9-8-6

Since neither of us was able ^snp3-9-9-1

to convince the other, ^snp3-9-9-2

we’ve come to ask you, sir, ^snp3-9-9-3

renowned as the awakened one. ^snp3-9-9-4

As people honor with cupped palms ^snp3-9-10-1

the moon on the cusp of waxing, ^snp3-9-10-2

bowing, they revere ^snp3-9-10-3

Gotama in the world. ^snp3-9-10-4

We ask this of Gotama, ^snp3-9-11-1

the Eye arisen in the world: ^snp3-9-11-2

is one a brahmin due to birth, ^snp3-9-11-3

or else because of deeds? ^snp3-9-11-4

We don’t know, please tell us, ^snp3-9-11-5

so we can recognize a brahmin.” ^snp3-9-11-6

“I shall explain to you,” ^snp3-9-12-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-9-12-2

“accurately and in sequence, ^snp3-9-12-3

the taxonomy of living creatures, ^snp3-9-12-4

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-12-5

Know the grass and trees, ^snp3-9-13-1

though they lack self-awareness. ^snp3-9-13-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-13-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-13-4

Next there are bugs that crawl or fly, ^snp3-9-14-1

and so on, to ants and termites. ^snp3-9-14-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-14-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-14-4

Know the quadrupeds, too, ^snp3-9-15-1

both small and large. ^snp3-9-15-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-15-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-15-4

Know, too, the long-backed snakes, ^snp3-9-16-1

crawling on their bellies. ^snp3-9-16-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-16-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-16-4

Next know the fish, ^snp3-9-17-1

whose habitat is the water. ^snp3-9-17-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-17-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-17-4

Next know the birds, ^snp3-9-18-1

winged chariots in flight. ^snp3-9-18-2

They’re defined by birth, ^snp3-9-18-3

for species are indeed diverse. ^snp3-9-18-4

While the differences between these species ^snp3-9-19-1

are defined by birth, ^snp3-9-19-2

the differences between humans ^snp3-9-19-3

are not defined by birth. ^snp3-9-19-4

Not by hair nor by head, ^snp3-9-20-1

not by ear nor by eye, ^snp3-9-20-2

not by mouth nor by nose, ^snp3-9-20-3

not by lips nor by eyebrow, ^snp3-9-20-4

not by shoulder nor by neck, ^snp3-9-21-1

not by belly nor by back, ^snp3-9-21-2

not by buttocks nor by breast, ^snp3-9-21-3

not by groin nor by genitals, ^snp3-9-21-4

not by hands nor by feet, ^snp3-9-22-1

not by fingers nor by nails, ^snp3-9-22-2

not by knees nor by thighs, ^snp3-9-22-3

not by color nor by voice: ^snp3-9-22-4

none of these are defined by birth ^snp3-9-22-5

as it is for other species. ^snp3-9-22-6

In specific human bodies ^snp3-9-23-1

you can’t find such distinctions. ^snp3-9-23-2

The distinctions among humans ^snp3-9-23-3

are spoken of by convention. ^snp3-9-23-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-24-1

who lives off keeping cattle: ^snp3-9-24-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-24-3

as a farmer, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-24-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-25-1

who lives off various professions: ^snp3-9-25-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-25-3

as a professional, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-25-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-26-1

who lives off trade: ^snp3-9-26-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-26-3

as a trader, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-26-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-27-1

who lives off serving others: ^snp3-9-27-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-27-3

as a servant, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-27-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-28-1

who lives off stealing: ^snp3-9-28-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-28-3

as a bandit, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-28-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-29-1

who lives off archery: ^snp3-9-29-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-29-3

as a warrior, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-29-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-30-1

who lives off priesthood: ^snp3-9-30-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-30-3

as a sacrificer, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-30-4

Anyone among humans ^snp3-9-31-1

who taxes village and nation, ^snp3-9-31-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-31-3

as a ruler, not a brahmin. ^snp3-9-31-4

I don’t call someone a brahmin ^snp3-9-32-1

after the mother’s womb they’re born from. ^snp3-9-32-2

If they still have attachments, ^snp3-9-32-3

they’re just someone who says ‘worthy’. ^snp3-9-32-4

Having nothing, taking nothing: ^snp3-9-32-5

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-32-6

Having cut off all fetters ^snp3-9-33-1

they have no anxiety; ^snp3-9-33-2

they’ve slipped their chains and are detached: ^snp3-9-33-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-33-4

They’ve cut the strap and harness, ^snp3-9-34-1

the halter and bridle too; ^snp3-9-34-2

with cross-bar lifted, they’re awakened: ^snp3-9-34-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-34-4

Abuse, killing, caging: ^snp3-9-35-1

they endure these without anger. ^snp3-9-35-2

Patience is their powerful army: ^snp3-9-35-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-35-4

Not irritable or pretentious, ^snp3-9-36-1

dutiful in precepts and observances, ^snp3-9-36-2

tamed, bearing their final body: ^snp3-9-36-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-36-4

Like water from a lotus leaf, ^snp3-9-37-1

like a mustard seed off a pin-point, ^snp3-9-37-2

sensual pleasures slip off them: ^snp3-9-37-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-37-4

They understand for themselves ^snp3-9-38-1

the end of suffering in this life; ^snp3-9-38-2

with burden put down, detached: ^snp3-9-38-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-38-4

Deep in wisdom, intelligent, ^snp3-9-39-1

expert in the path and what is not the path; ^snp3-9-39-2

arrived at the highest goal: ^snp3-9-39-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-39-4

Mixing with neither ^snp3-9-40-1

householders nor the homeless; ^snp3-9-40-2

a migrant with no bastion, few in wishes: ^snp3-9-40-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-40-4

They’ve laid aside violence ^snp3-9-41-1

against creatures firm and frail; ^snp3-9-41-2

not killing or making others kill: ^snp3-9-41-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-41-4

Not fighting among those who fight, ^snp3-9-42-1

quenched among those who have taken up arms, ^snp3-9-42-2

not grasping among those who grasp: ^snp3-9-42-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-42-4

They’ve discarded greed and hate, ^snp3-9-43-1

along with conceit and contempt, ^snp3-9-43-2

like a mustard seed off the point of a pin: ^snp3-9-43-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-43-4

The words they utter ^snp3-9-44-1

are polished, informative, and true, ^snp3-9-44-2

and don’t offend anyone: ^snp3-9-44-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-44-4

They don’t steal anything in the world, ^snp3-9-45-1

long or short, ^snp3-9-45-2

fine or coarse, beautiful or ugly: ^snp3-9-45-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-45-4

They have no hope ^snp3-9-46-1

for this world or the next; ^snp3-9-46-2

with no need for hope, detached: ^snp3-9-46-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-46-4

They have no clinging, ^snp3-9-47-1

knowledge has freed them of indecision, ^snp3-9-47-2

they’ve arrived at the objective, freedom from death: ^snp3-9-47-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-47-4

They’ve escaped clinging ^snp3-9-48-1

to both good and bad deeds; ^snp3-9-48-2

sorrowless, stainless, pure: ^snp3-9-48-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-48-4

Pure as the spotless moon, ^snp3-9-49-1

clear and undisturbed, ^snp3-9-49-2

they’ve ended relish for rebirth: ^snp3-9-49-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-49-4

They’ve got past this grueling swamp ^snp3-9-50-1

of delusion, transmigration. ^snp3-9-50-2

Meditating in stillness, free of indecision, ^snp3-9-50-3

they have crossed over to the far shore. ^snp3-9-50-4

They’re quenched by not grasping: ^snp3-9-50-5

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-50-6

They’ve given up sensual stimulations, ^snp3-9-51-1

and have gone forth from lay life; ^snp3-9-51-2

they’ve ended rebirth in the sensual realm: ^snp3-9-51-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-51-4

They’ve given up craving, ^snp3-9-52-1

and have gone forth from lay life; ^snp3-9-52-2

they’ve ended craving to be reborn: ^snp3-9-52-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-52-4

They’ve thrown off the human yoke, ^snp3-9-53-1

and slipped out of the heavenly yoke; ^snp3-9-53-2

unyoked from all yokes: ^snp3-9-53-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-53-4

Giving up desire and discontent, ^snp3-9-54-1

they’re cooled and free of attachments; ^snp3-9-54-2

a hero, master of the whole world: ^snp3-9-54-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-54-4

They know the passing away ^snp3-9-55-1

and rebirth of all beings; ^snp3-9-55-2

unattached, holy, awakened: ^snp3-9-55-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-55-4

Gods, centaurs, and humans ^snp3-9-56-1

don’t know their destiny; ^snp3-9-56-2

the perfected ones with defilements ended: ^snp3-9-56-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-56-4

They have nothing before or after, ^snp3-9-57-1

or even in between. ^snp3-9-57-2

Having nothing, taking nothing: ^snp3-9-57-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-57-4

Captain of the herd, excellent hero, ^snp3-9-58-1

great seer and victor; ^snp3-9-58-2

unstirred, washed, awakened: ^snp3-9-58-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-58-4

They who know their past lives, ^snp3-9-59-1

see heaven and places of loss, ^snp3-9-59-2

and have attained the ending of rebirth, ^snp3-9-59-3

that’s who I declare a brahmin. ^snp3-9-59-4

For name and clan are formulated ^snp3-9-60-1

as mere convention in the world. ^snp3-9-60-2

Produced by mutual agreement, ^snp3-9-60-3

they’re formulated in each case. ^snp3-9-60-4

For a long time this misconception ^snp3-9-61-1

has prejudiced those who don’t understand. ^snp3-9-61-2

Ignorant, they declare ^snp3-9-61-3

that one is a brahmin by birth. ^snp3-9-61-4

You’re not a brahmin by birth, ^snp3-9-62-1

nor by birth a non-brahmin. ^snp3-9-62-2

You’re a brahmin by your deeds, ^snp3-9-62-3

and by deeds a non-brahmin. ^snp3-9-62-4

You’re a farmer by your deeds, ^snp3-9-63-1

by deeds you’re a professional; ^snp3-9-63-2

you’re a trader by your deeds, ^snp3-9-63-3

by deeds are you a servant; ^snp3-9-63-4

you’re a bandit by your deeds, ^snp3-9-64-1

by deeds you’re a warrior; ^snp3-9-64-2

you’re a sacrificer by your deeds, ^snp3-9-64-3

by deeds you’re a ruler. ^snp3-9-64-4

In this way the astute regard deeds ^snp3-9-65-1

in accord with truth. ^snp3-9-65-2

Seeing dependent origination, ^snp3-9-65-3

they’re expert in deeds and their results. ^snp3-9-65-4

Deeds make the world go on, ^snp3-9-66-1

deeds make people go on; ^snp3-9-66-2

sentient beings are bound by deeds, ^snp3-9-66-3

like a moving chariot’s linchpin. ^snp3-9-66-4

By fervor and spiritual practice, ^snp3-9-67-1

by restraint and by self-control: ^snp3-9-67-2

that’s how to become a brahmin, ^snp3-9-67-3

this is the supreme brahmin. ^snp3-9-67-4

Accomplished in the three knowledges, ^snp3-9-68-1

peaceful, with rebirth ended, ^snp3-9-68-2

know them, Vāseṭṭha, ^snp3-9-68-3

as the Divinity and Sakka to the wise.” ^snp3-9-68-4

When he had spoken, Vāseṭṭha and Bhāradvāja said to him, ^snp3-9-69-1

“Excellent, worthy Gotama! Excellent! … ^snp3-9-69-2

From this day forth, may the worthy Gotama remember us as lay followers who have gone for refuge for life.” ^snp3-9-69-3

### With Kokālika (Anthology of Discourses 3.10) ^snp3-10-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-10-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in Jeta’s Grove, Anāthapiṇḍika’s monastery. ^snp3-10-1-2

Then the mendicant Kokālika went up to the Buddha, bowed, sat down to one side, and said to him, ^snp3-10-1-3

“Sir, Sāriputta and Moggallāna have corrupt wishes. They’ve fallen under the sway of corrupt wishes.” ^snp3-10-1-4

When this was said, the Buddha said to Kokālika, ^snp3-10-2-1

“Don’t say that, Kokālika! Don’t say that, Kokālika! ^snp3-10-2-2

Have confidence in Sāriputta and Moggallāna, ^snp3-10-2-3

they’re good monks.” ^snp3-10-2-4

For a second time … ^snp3-10-3-1

For a third time Kokālika said to the Buddha, ^snp3-10-3-2

“Despite my faith and trust in the Buddha, Sāriputta and Moggallāna have corrupt wishes. They’ve fallen under the sway of corrupt wishes.” ^snp3-10-3-3

For a third time, the Buddha said to Kokālika, ^snp3-10-3-4

“Don’t say that, Kokālika! Don’t say that, Kokālika! ^snp3-10-3-5

Have confidence in Sāriputta and Moggallāna, ^snp3-10-3-6

they’re good monks.” ^snp3-10-3-7

Then Kokālika rose from his seat, bowed, and respectfully circled the Buddha, keeping him on his right, before leaving. ^snp3-10-4-1

Not long after he left his body erupted with boils the size of mustard seeds. ^snp3-10-4-2

The boils grew to the size of mung beans, then chickpeas, then jujube seeds, then jujubes, then myrobalans, then unripe wood-apples, then ripe wood-apples. Finally they burst open, and pus and blood oozed out. ^snp3-10-4-3

Then the mendicant Kokālika died of that illness. ^snp3-10-4-4

He was reborn in the Pink Lotus hell because of his resentment for Sāriputta and Moggallāna. ^snp3-10-4-5

Then, late at night, the glorious divinity Sahampati, lighting up the entire Jeta’s Grove, went up to the Buddha, bowed, stood to one side, and said to him, ^snp3-10-5-1

“Sir, the mendicant Kokālika has passed away. ^snp3-10-5-2

He was reborn in the Pink Lotus hell because of his resentment for Sāriputta and Moggallāna.” ^snp3-10-5-3

That’s what the divinity Sahampati said. ^snp3-10-5-4

Then he bowed and respectfully circled the Buddha, keeping him on his right side, before vanishing right there. ^snp3-10-5-5

Then, when the night had passed, the Buddha told the mendicants all that had happened. ^snp3-10-6-1

When he said this, one of the mendicants said to the Buddha, ^snp3-10-7-1

“Sir, how long is the lifespan in the Pink Lotus hell?” ^snp3-10-7-2

“It’s long, mendicant. ^snp3-10-7-3

It’s not easy to calculate how many years, how many hundreds or thousands or hundreds of thousands of years it lasts.” ^snp3-10-7-4

“But sir, is it possible to give a simile?” ^snp3-10-7-5

“It’s possible,” said the Buddha. ^snp3-10-7-6

“Suppose there was a Kosalan load of twenty packs of sesame seed. And at the end of every hundred years someone would remove a single seed from it. ^snp3-10-8-1

By this means the Kosalan load of twenty packs of sesame seed would run out faster than a single lifetime in the Abbuda hell. ^snp3-10-8-2

Now, twenty lifetimes in the Abbuda hell equal one lifetime in the Nirabbuda hell. ^snp3-10-8-3

Twenty lifetimes in the Nirabbuda hell equal one lifetime in the Ababa hell. ^snp3-10-8-4

Twenty lifetimes in the Ababa hell equal one lifetime in the Aṭaṭa hell. ^snp3-10-8-5

Twenty lifetimes in the Aṭaṭa hell equal one lifetime in the Ahaha hell. ^snp3-10-8-6

Twenty lifetimes in the Ahaha hell equal one lifetime in the Yellow Lotus hell. ^snp3-10-8-7

Twenty lifetimes in the Yellow Lotus hell equal one lifetime in the Fragrant Water Lily hell. ^snp3-10-8-8

Twenty lifetimes in the Fragrant Water Lily hell equal one lifetime in the Blue Water Lily hell. ^snp3-10-8-9

Twenty lifetimes in the Blue Water Lily hell equal one lifetime in the White Lotus hell. ^snp3-10-8-10

Twenty lifetimes in the White Lotus hell equal one lifetime in the Pink Lotus hell. ^snp3-10-8-11

The mendicant Kokālika has been reborn in the Pink Lotus hell because of his resentment for Sāriputta and Moggallāna.” ^snp3-10-8-12

That is what the Buddha said. ^snp3-10-8-13

Then the Holy One, the Teacher, went on to say: ^snp3-10-8-14

“A person is born ^snp3-10-9-1

with an axe in their mouth. ^snp3-10-9-2

A fool cuts themselves with it ^snp3-10-9-3

when they say bad words. ^snp3-10-9-4

When you praise someone worthy of criticism, ^snp3-10-10-1

or criticize someone worthy of praise, ^snp3-10-10-2

you choose a losing hand with your own mouth: ^snp3-10-10-3

you’ll never find happiness that way. ^snp3-10-10-4

A losing hand at dice is a trivial thing, ^snp3-10-11-1

if all you lose is your money ^snp3-10-11-2

and all you own, even yourself. ^snp3-10-11-3

What’s a really terrible hand ^snp3-10-11-4

is to hate the holy ones. ^snp3-10-11-5

For a hundred thousand times a hundred million, ^snp3-10-12-1

times five hundred and thirty-six times <j>a thousand times ten million years ^snp3-10-12-2

a slanderer of noble ones goes to hell, ^snp3-10-12-3

having aimed bad words and thoughts at them. ^snp3-10-12-4

A liar goes to hell, ^snp3-10-13-1

as does one who denies what they did. ^snp3-10-13-2

Both are equal in the hereafter, ^snp3-10-13-3

those men of base deeds. ^snp3-10-13-4

Whoever wrongs a man who has done no wrong, ^snp3-10-14-1

a pure man who has not a blemish, ^snp3-10-14-2

the evil backfires on the fool, ^snp3-10-14-3

like fine dust thrown upwind. ^snp3-10-14-4

One addicted to the way of greed, ^snp3-10-15-1

abuses others with their speech, ^snp3-10-15-2

faithless, miserly, uncharitable, ^snp3-10-15-3

stingy, addicted to backbiting. ^snp3-10-15-4

Foul-mouthed, divisive, ignoble, ^snp3-10-16-1

a baby-killer, wicked, wrongdoer, ^snp3-10-16-2

worst of men, cursed, base-born— ^snp3-10-16-3

quiet now, for you are bound for hell. ^snp3-10-16-4

You stir up dust, causing harm, ^snp3-10-17-1

when you, sinner, malign the good. ^snp3-10-17-2

Having done many bad deeds, ^snp3-10-17-3

you’ll go to the pit for a long time. ^snp3-10-17-4

For no-one’s deeds are ever lost, ^snp3-10-18-1

they return to their owner. ^snp3-10-18-2

In the next life that stupid sinner ^snp3-10-18-3

sees suffering in themselves. ^snp3-10-18-4

They approach the place of impalement, ^snp3-10-19-1

with its iron spikes, sharp blades, and iron stakes. ^snp3-10-19-2

Then there is the food, which appropriately, ^snp3-10-19-3

is like a red-hot iron ball. ^snp3-10-19-4

For the speakers speak not sweetly, ^snp3-10-20-1

they don’t hurry there, or find shelter. ^snp3-10-20-2

They lie upon a spread of coals, ^snp3-10-20-3

they enter a blazing mass of fire. ^snp3-10-20-4

Wrapping them in a net, ^snp3-10-21-1

they strike them there with iron hammers. ^snp3-10-21-2

They come to blinding darkness, ^snp3-10-21-3

which spreads about them like a fog. ^snp3-10-21-4

Next they enter a copper pot, ^snp3-10-22-1

a blazing mass of fire. ^snp3-10-22-2

There they roast for a long time, ^snp3-10-22-3

writhing in the masses of fire. ^snp3-10-22-4

Then the sinner roasts there ^snp3-10-23-1

in a mixture of pus and blood. ^snp3-10-23-2

No matter where they settle, ^snp3-10-23-3

everything they touch there hurts them. ^snp3-10-23-4

The sinner roasts in ^snp3-10-24-1

worm-infested water. ^snp3-10-24-2

There’s not even a shore to go to, ^snp3-10-24-3

for all around are the same kind of pots. ^snp3-10-24-4

They enter the Sword-Leaf Forest, ^snp3-10-25-1

so sharp they cut their body to pieces. ^snp3-10-25-2

Having grabbed the tongue with a hook, ^snp3-10-25-3

they stab it, slashing back and forth. ^snp3-10-25-4

Then they approach the impassable Vetaraṇī River, ^snp3-10-26-1

with its sharp blades, its razor blades. ^snp3-10-26-2

Idiots fall into it, ^snp3-10-26-3

the wicked who have done wicked deeds. ^snp3-10-26-4

There dogs all brown and spotted, ^snp3-10-27-1

and raven flocks, and greedy jackals ^snp3-10-27-2

devour them as they wail, ^snp3-10-27-3

while hawks and crows attack them. ^snp3-10-27-4

Hard, alas, is the life here ^snp3-10-28-1

that sinners endure. ^snp3-10-28-2

That’s why for the rest of this life ^snp3-10-28-3

a person ought do their duty without fail. ^snp3-10-28-4

Experts have counted the loads of sesame ^snp3-10-29-1

as compared to the Pink Lotus Hell. ^snp3-10-29-2

For there are five times ten thousand times ten million, ^snp3-10-29-3

plus another twelve hundred times ten million years— ^snp3-10-29-4

as painful as life is said to be in hell, ^snp3-10-30-1

that’s how long one must dwell there. ^snp3-10-30-2

That’s why, for those who are pure, well-behaved, <j>full of good qualities, ^snp3-10-30-3

one should always guard one’s speech and mind.” ^snp3-10-30-4

### About Nālaka (Anthology of Discourses 3.11) ^snp3-11-0

The seer Asita in his daily meditation ^snp3-11-1-1

saw the bright-clad gods of the host of Thirty ^snp3-11-1-2

and their lord Sakka joyfully celebrating, ^snp3-11-1-3

waving streamers in exuberant exaltation. ^snp3-11-1-4

Seeing the gods rejoicing, elated, ^snp3-11-2-1

he paid respects and said this there: ^snp3-11-2-2

“Why is the community of gods <j>in such excellent spirits? ^snp3-11-2-3

Why take up streamers and whirl them about? ^snp3-11-2-4

Even in the war with the titans, ^snp3-11-3-1

when gods were victorious and titans defeated, ^snp3-11-3-2

there was no such excitement. ^snp3-11-3-3

What marvel have the celestials seen <j>that they so rejoice? ^snp3-11-3-4

Shouting and singing and playing music, ^snp3-11-4-1

they clap their hands and dance. ^snp3-11-4-2

I ask you, dwellers on Mount Meru’s peak, ^snp3-11-4-3

quickly dispel my doubt, good fellows!” ^snp3-11-4-4

“The being intent on awakening, a peerless gem, ^snp3-11-5-1

has been born in the human realm <j>for the sake of welfare and happiness, ^snp3-11-5-2

in Lumbinī, a village in the Sakyan land. ^snp3-11-5-3

That’s why we’re so happy, in such excellent spirits. ^snp3-11-5-4

He is supreme among all beings, the peak individual, ^snp3-11-6-1

captain of men, supreme among all creatures. ^snp3-11-6-2

He will roll forth the wheel in the grove of the seers, ^snp3-11-6-3

roaring like a mighty lion, lord of beasts.” ^snp3-11-6-4

Hearing this, he swiftly descended ^snp3-11-7-1

and right away approached Suddhodana’s home. ^snp3-11-7-2

Seated there he said this to the Sakyans, ^snp3-11-7-3

“Where is the boy? I too wish to see him!” ^snp3-11-7-4

Then the Sakyans showed their son <j>to the one named Asita— ^snp3-11-8-1

the boy shone like burning gold ^snp3-11-8-2

well-wrought in the forge; ^snp3-11-8-3

dazzling with glory, of peerless beauty. ^snp3-11-8-4

The boy beamed like crested flame, ^snp3-11-9-1

pure as the moon, lord of stars traversing the sky, ^snp3-11-9-2

blazing like the sun free of stormclouds after the rains; ^snp3-11-9-3

seeing him, he was joyful, brimming with happiness. ^snp3-11-9-4

The celestials held up a parasol in the sky, ^snp3-11-10-1

many-ribbed and thousand-circled; ^snp3-11-10-2

and golden-handled chowries waved— ^snp3-11-10-3

but none could see who held <j>the chowries or the parasols. ^snp3-11-10-4

When the dreadlocked seer called “Dark Splendor” ^snp3-11-11-1

had seen the boy like a gold ingot on a cream rug ^snp3-11-11-2

with a white parasol held over his head, ^snp3-11-11-3

he received him, elated and happy. ^snp3-11-11-4

Having received the Sakyan bull, ^snp3-11-12-1

the seeker, master of marks and hymns, ^snp3-11-12-2

lifted up his voice with confident heart: ^snp3-11-12-3

“He is supreme, the best of men!” ^snp3-11-12-4

But then, remembering he would depart this world, ^snp3-11-13-1

his spirits fell and his tears flowed. ^snp3-11-13-2

Seeing the weeping seer, the Sakyans said, ^snp3-11-13-3

“Surely there will be no threat to the boy?” ^snp3-11-13-4

Seeing the crestfallen Sakyans, the seer said, ^snp3-11-14-1

“I do not forsee harm befall the boy, ^snp3-11-14-2

and there will be no threat to him, ^snp3-11-14-3

not in the least; set your minds at ease. ^snp3-11-14-4

This boy shall reach the highest awakening. ^snp3-11-15-1

As one of perfectly purified vision, <j>compassionate for the welfare of the many, ^snp3-11-15-2

he shall roll forth the wheel of the teaching; ^snp3-11-15-3

his spiritual path will become widespread. ^snp3-11-15-4

But I have not long left in this life, ^snp3-11-16-1

I shall die before then. ^snp3-11-16-2

I will never hear the teaching of the one <j>who bore the unequaled burden. ^snp3-11-16-3

That’s why I’m so upset and distraught—<j>it’s a disaster for me!” ^snp3-11-16-4

Having brought abundant happiness to the Sakyans, ^snp3-11-17-1

the spiritual seeker left the royal compound. ^snp3-11-17-2

He had a nephew; and out of sympathy ^snp3-11-17-3

he encouraged him in the teaching <j>of the one who bore the unequaled burden. ^snp3-11-17-4

“When you hear the voice of another saying <j>‘Buddha’— ^snp3-11-18-1

one who has attained awakening <j>and who reveals the foremost teaching— ^snp3-11-18-2

go there and ask about his breakthrough; ^snp3-11-18-3

lead the spiritual life under that Blessed One.” ^snp3-11-18-4

Now, that Nālaka had a store of accumulated merit; ^snp3-11-19-1

so when instructed by one of such kindly intent, ^snp3-11-19-2

with perfectly purified vision of the future, ^snp3-11-19-3

he waited anticipating the Victor, <j>guarding his senses. ^snp3-11-19-4

When he heard of the Victor rolling forth <j>the excellent wheel he went to him, ^snp3-11-20-1

and seeing the boss seer, he became confident. ^snp3-11-20-2

The time of Asita’s instruction had arrived; ^snp3-11-20-3

so he asked the excellent sage <j>about the highest sagacity. ^snp3-11-20-4

The introductory verses are finished. ^snp3-11-20-5

“I now know that Asita’s words ^snp3-11-21-1

have turned out to be true. ^snp3-11-21-2

I ask you this, Gotama, ^snp3-11-21-3

who has gone beyond all things: ^snp3-11-21-4

For one who has entered the homeless life, ^snp3-11-22-1

seeking food on alms round, ^snp3-11-22-2

when questioned, O sage, please tell me ^snp3-11-22-3

of sagacity, the ultimate state.” ^snp3-11-22-4

“I shall school you in sagacity,” ^snp3-11-23-1

said the Buddha, ^snp3-11-23-2

“so difficult and challenging. ^snp3-11-23-3

Come, I shall tell you all about it. ^snp3-11-23-4

Brace yourself; stay strong! ^snp3-11-23-5

In the village, keep the same attitude ^snp3-11-24-1

no matter if reviled or praised. ^snp3-11-24-2

Guard against ill-tempered thoughts; ^snp3-11-24-3

wander peaceful and humble. ^snp3-11-24-4

Many different things come up, ^snp3-11-25-1

like tongues of fire in a forest. ^snp3-11-25-2

Women try to seduce a sage— ^snp3-11-25-3

let them not seduce you! ^snp3-11-25-4

Refraining from sex, ^snp3-11-26-1

having left behind sensual pleasures high and low, ^snp3-11-26-2

don’t be hostile or attached ^snp3-11-26-3

to living creatures firm or frail. ^snp3-11-26-4

‘As am I, so are they; ^snp3-11-27-1

as are they, so am I’— ^snp3-11-27-2

Treating others like oneself, ^snp3-11-27-3

neither kill nor incite to kill. ^snp3-11-27-4

Leaving behind desire and greed ^snp3-11-28-1

for what ordinary people are attached to, ^snp3-11-28-2

one whose eye is clear would set out to practice, ^snp3-11-28-3

they’d cross over this abyss. ^snp3-11-28-4

With empty stomach, taking limited food, ^snp3-11-29-1

few in wishes, not greedy; ^snp3-11-29-2

truly hungerless regarding all desires, ^snp3-11-29-3

desireless, one is quenched. ^snp3-11-29-4

Having wandered for alms, ^snp3-11-30-1

they’d take themselves into the forest; ^snp3-11-30-2

and nearing the foot of a tree, ^snp3-11-30-3

the sage would take their seat. ^snp3-11-30-4

That attentive one intent on absorption, ^snp3-11-31-1

would delight within the forest. ^snp3-11-31-2

They’d practice absorption at the foot of a tree, ^snp3-11-31-3

filling themselves with bliss. ^snp3-11-31-4

Then, at the end of the night, ^snp3-11-32-1

they’d take themselves into a village. ^snp3-11-32-2

They’d not welcome being called, ^snp3-11-32-3

nor offerings brought from the village. ^snp3-11-32-4

A sage who has come to a village ^snp3-11-33-1

would not walk hastily among the families. ^snp3-11-33-2

They’d not discuss their search for food, ^snp3-11-33-3

nor would they speak suggestively. ^snp3-11-33-4

‘I got something, that’s good. ^snp3-11-34-1

I got nothing, that’s fine.’ ^snp3-11-34-2

Unaffected in both cases, ^snp3-11-34-3

they return right to the tree. ^snp3-11-34-4

Wandering with bowl in hand, ^snp3-11-35-1

not dumb, but thought to be dumb, ^snp3-11-35-2

they wouldn’t scorn a tiny gift, ^snp3-11-35-3

nor look down upon the giver. ^snp3-11-35-4

For the practice has many aspects, ^snp3-11-36-1

as explained by the Ascetic. ^snp3-11-36-2

They do not go to the far shore twice, ^snp3-11-36-3

nor having gone once do they fall away. ^snp3-11-36-4

When a mendicant has no creeping, ^snp3-11-37-1

and has cut the stream of craving, ^snp3-11-37-2

and given up all the various duties, ^snp3-11-37-3

no fever is found in them. ^snp3-11-37-4

I shall school you in sagacity. ^snp3-11-38-1

Practice as if you were licking a razor’s edge. ^snp3-11-38-2

With tongue pressed against the roof of your mouth, ^snp3-11-38-3

be restrained regarding your stomach. ^snp3-11-38-4

Don’t be sluggish in mind, ^snp3-11-39-1

nor think overly much. ^snp3-11-39-2

Be free of putrefaction and unattached, ^snp3-11-39-3

committed to the spiritual life. ^snp3-11-39-4

Train in a lonely seat, ^snp3-11-40-1

attending closely to ascetics; ^snp3-11-40-2

solitude is sagacity, they say. ^snp3-11-40-3

If you welcome solitude, ^snp3-11-40-4

you’ll light up the ten directions. ^snp3-11-40-5

Having heard the words of the attentive, ^snp3-11-41-1

the meditators who’ve given up sensual desires, ^snp3-11-41-2

a follower of mine would develop ^snp3-11-41-3

conscience and faith all the more. ^snp3-11-41-4

Understand this by the way streams move ^snp3-11-42-1

in clefts and crevices: ^snp3-11-42-2

the little creeks flow on resounding, ^snp3-11-42-3

while silent flow the great rivers. ^snp3-11-42-4

What is lacking, resounds; ^snp3-11-43-1

what is full is at peace. ^snp3-11-43-2

The fool is like a half-full pot; ^snp3-11-43-3

the wise like a brimfull lake. ^snp3-11-43-4

When the Ascetic speaks much ^snp3-11-44-1

it is relevant and meaningful: ^snp3-11-44-2

knowing, he teaches the Dhamma; ^snp3-11-44-3

knowing, he speaks much. ^snp3-11-44-4

But one who, knowing, is self-controlled, ^snp3-11-45-1

knowing, does not speak much; ^snp3-11-45-2

that sage is worthy of sagacity, ^snp3-11-45-3

that sage has achieved sagacity.” ^snp3-11-45-4

### Contemplating Pairs (Anthology of Discourses 3.12) ^snp3-12-0

So I have heard. ^snp3-12-1-1

At one time the Buddha was staying near Sāvatthī in the stilt longhouse of Migāra’s mother in the Eastern Monastery. ^snp3-12-1-2

Now, at that time it was the sabbath—the full moon on the fifteenth day—and the Buddha was sitting in the open surrounded by the Saṅgha of monks. ^snp3-12-1-3

Then the Buddha looked around the Saṅgha of mendicants, who were so very silent. He addressed them: ^snp3-12-1-4

“Suppose, mendicants, they questioned you thus: ‘There are skillful teachings that are noble, emancipating, and lead to awakening. What is the real reason for listening to such teachings?’ You should answer: ^snp3-12-2-1

‘Only so as to truly know the pairs of teachings.’ ^snp3-12-2-2

And what pairs do they speak of? ^snp3-12-2-3

‘This is suffering; this is the origin of suffering’: this is the first contemplation. ^snp3-12-3-1

‘This is the cessation of suffering; this is the practice that leads to the cessation of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-3-2

When a mendicant meditates rightly contemplating a pair of teachings in this way—diligent, keen, and resolute—they can expect one of two results: ^snp3-12-3-3

enlightenment in this very life, or if there’s residue left behind, non-return.” ^snp3-12-3-4

That is what the Buddha said. ^snp3-12-4-1

Then the Holy One, the Teacher, went on to say: ^snp3-12-4-2

“There are those who don’t understand suffering ^snp3-12-5-1

and suffering’s cause, ^snp3-12-5-2

and where all suffering ^snp3-12-5-3

ceases no residue left behind. ^snp3-12-5-4

And they do not know the path ^snp3-12-5-5

that leads to the stilling of suffering. ^snp3-12-5-6

They lack the heart’s release, ^snp3-12-6-1

as well as the release by wisdom. ^snp3-12-6-2

Unable to make an end, ^snp3-12-6-3

they continue to be reborn and grow old. ^snp3-12-6-4

But there are those who understand suffering ^snp3-12-7-1

and suffering’s cause, ^snp3-12-7-2

and where all suffering ^snp3-12-7-3

ceases with no residue left behind. ^snp3-12-7-4

And they understand the path ^snp3-12-7-5

that leads to the stilling of suffering. ^snp3-12-7-6

They’re endowed with the heart’s release, ^snp3-12-8-1

as well as the release by wisdom. ^snp3-12-8-2

Able to make an end, ^snp3-12-8-3

they don’t continue to be reborn and grow old.” ^snp3-12-8-4

“Suppose, mendicants, they questioned you thus: ‘Could there be another way to contemplate the pairs?’ ^snp3-12-9-1

You should say, ‘There could.’ ^snp3-12-9-2

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-9-3

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by attachment’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-9-4

‘When attachment has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-9-5

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-9-6

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-9-7

“Attachment is the source of suffering ^snp3-12-10-1

in all its countless forms in the world. ^snp3-12-10-2

When an ignorant person builds up attachments, ^snp3-12-10-3

that dullard returns to suffering again and again. ^snp3-12-10-4

So let one who understands <j>not build up attachments, ^snp3-12-10-5

contemplating the inception of suffering and rebirth.” ^snp3-12-10-6

“Suppose, mendicants, they questioned you thus: ‘Could there be another way to contemplate the pairs?’ ^snp3-12-11-1

You should say, ‘There could.’ ^snp3-12-11-2

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-11-3

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by ignorance’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-11-4

‘When ignorance has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-11-5

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-11-6

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-11-7

“Those who journey again and again, ^snp3-12-12-1

transmigrating through birth and death; ^snp3-12-12-2

they go from this state to another, ^snp3-12-12-3

that destination due only to ignorance. ^snp3-12-12-4

For ignorance is the great delusion ^snp3-12-13-1

because of which we have long transmigrated. ^snp3-12-13-2

Those beings who have knowledge ^snp3-12-13-3

do not proceed to a future life.” ^snp3-12-13-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-14-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-14-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by choices’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-14-3

‘When choices has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-14-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-14-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-14-6

“All the suffering that originates ^snp3-12-15-1

is caused by choices. ^snp3-12-15-2

With the cessation of choices, ^snp3-12-15-3

there is no origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-15-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-16-1

that suffering is caused by choices; ^snp3-12-16-2

through the stilling of all choices, ^snp3-12-16-3

and the stopping of perceptions, ^snp3-12-16-4

this is the way suffering ends ^snp3-12-16-5

when one has known this as it is. ^snp3-12-16-6

Rightly seeing, knowledge masters, ^snp3-12-17-1

the astute, understanding rightly, ^snp3-12-17-2

having thrown off Māra’s yoke, ^snp3-12-17-3

do not proceed to a future life.” ^snp3-12-17-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-18-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-18-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by consciousness’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-18-3

‘When consciousness has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-18-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-18-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-18-6

“All the suffering that originates ^snp3-12-19-1

is caused by consciousness. ^snp3-12-19-2

With the cessation of consciousness, ^snp3-12-19-3

there is no origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-19-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-20-1

that suffering is caused by consciousness, ^snp3-12-20-2

with the stilling of consciousness a mendicant ^snp3-12-20-3

is hungerless, quenched.” ^snp3-12-20-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-21-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-21-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by contact’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-21-3

‘When contact has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-21-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-21-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-21-6

“Those mired in contact, ^snp3-12-22-1

swept down the stream of lives, ^snp3-12-22-2

practicing the wrong way, ^snp3-12-22-3

are far from the ending of fetters. ^snp3-12-22-4

But those who completely understand contact, ^snp3-12-23-1

who, understanding, delight in peace; ^snp3-12-23-2

by comprehending contact ^snp3-12-23-3

they are hungerless, quenched.” ^snp3-12-23-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-24-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-24-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by feeling’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-24-3

‘When feeling has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-24-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-24-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-24-6

“Whether it’s pleasant or painful, ^snp3-12-25-1

as well as what’s neutral, ^snp3-12-25-2

whatever there is that is felt ^snp3-12-25-3

internally and externally: ^snp3-12-25-4

having known, ‘this is suffering, ^snp3-12-26-1

deceptive, breaking apart’, ^snp3-12-26-2

seeing feelings vanish with every contact, ^snp3-12-26-3

that’s how they understand them. ^snp3-12-26-4

With the ending of feelings, a mendicant ^snp3-12-26-5

is hungerless, quenched.” ^snp3-12-26-6

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-27-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-27-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by craving’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-27-3

‘When craving has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-27-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-27-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-27-6

“Craving is a person’s partner ^snp3-12-28-1

as they transmigrate on this long journey. ^snp3-12-28-2

They go from this state to another, ^snp3-12-28-3

but don’t escape transmigration. ^snp3-12-28-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-29-1

that craving is the cause of suffering— ^snp3-12-29-2

rid of craving, free of grasping, ^snp3-12-29-3

a mendicant would wander mindful.” ^snp3-12-29-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-30-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-30-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by grasping’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-30-3

‘When grasping has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-30-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-30-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-30-6

“Grasping is the cause of continued existence; ^snp3-12-31-1

one who exists falls into suffering. ^snp3-12-31-2

Death comes to those who are born— ^snp3-12-31-3

this is the origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-31-4

That’s why with the end of grasping, ^snp3-12-32-1

the astute, understanding rightly, ^snp3-12-32-2

having directly known the end of rebirth, ^snp3-12-32-3

do not proceed to a future life.” ^snp3-12-32-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-33-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-33-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by instigating activity’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-33-3

‘When instigation has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-33-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-33-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-33-6

“All the suffering that originates ^snp3-12-34-1

is caused by instigating activity. ^snp3-12-34-2

With the cessation of instigation, ^snp3-12-34-3

there is no origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-34-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-35-1

that suffering is caused by instigating activity, ^snp3-12-35-2

having given up all instigation, ^snp3-12-35-3

one is freed with respects to instigation. ^snp3-12-35-4

For the mendicant with peaceful mind, ^snp3-12-36-1

who has cut off craving for continued existence, ^snp3-12-36-2

transmigration through births is finished; ^snp3-12-36-3

there’ll be no more future lives for them.” ^snp3-12-36-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-37-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-37-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by sustenance’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-37-3

‘When sustenance has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-37-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-37-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-37-6

“All the suffering that originates ^snp3-12-38-1

is caused by sustenance. ^snp3-12-38-2

With the cessation of sustenance, ^snp3-12-38-3

there is no origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-38-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-39-1

that suffering is caused by sustenance, ^snp3-12-39-2

completely understanding all sustenance, ^snp3-12-39-3

one is independent of all sustenance. ^snp3-12-39-4

Having rightly understood the state of health, ^snp3-12-40-1

through the ending of defilements, ^snp3-12-40-2

using after reflection, firm in principle, ^snp3-12-40-3

a knowledge master cannot be reckoned.” ^snp3-12-40-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-41-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-41-2

‘All the suffering that originates is caused by perturbation’: this is one contemplation. ^snp3-12-41-3

‘When perturbation has faded away and ceased with no residue left behind, there is no origination of suffering’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-41-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-41-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-41-6

“All the suffering that originates ^snp3-12-42-1

is caused by perturbation. ^snp3-12-42-2

With the cessation of perturbation, ^snp3-12-42-3

there is no origination of suffering. ^snp3-12-42-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-43-1

that suffering is caused by perturbation, ^snp3-12-43-2

that’s why, having relinquished perturbation, ^snp3-12-43-3

and stopped making karmic choices, ^snp3-12-43-4

imperturbable, free of grasping, ^snp3-12-43-5

a mendicant would wander mindful.” ^snp3-12-43-6

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-44-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-44-2

‘For the dependent there is agitation’: this is the first contemplation. ^snp3-12-44-3

‘For the independent there’s no agitation’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-44-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-44-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-44-6

“For the independent there’s no agitation. ^snp3-12-45-1

The dependent, grasping, ^snp3-12-45-2

goes from this state to another, ^snp3-12-45-3

without escaping transmigration. ^snp3-12-45-4

Knowing this danger, ^snp3-12-46-1

the great fear in dependencies, ^snp3-12-46-2

independent, free of grasping, ^snp3-12-46-3

a mendicant would wander mindful.” ^snp3-12-46-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-47-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-47-2

‘Formless states are more peaceful than states of form’: this is the first contemplation. ^snp3-12-47-3

‘Cessation is more peaceful than formless states’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-47-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-47-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-47-6

“There are beings in the realm of luminous form, ^snp3-12-48-1

and others stuck in the formless. ^snp3-12-48-2

Not understanding cessation, ^snp3-12-48-3

they return in future lives. ^snp3-12-48-4

But the people who completely understand form, ^snp3-12-49-1

not stuck in the formless, ^snp3-12-49-2

released in cessation— ^snp3-12-49-3

they are conquerors of death.” ^snp3-12-49-4

“‘Could there be another way?’ … ^snp3-12-50-1

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-50-2

‘What this world—with its gods, Māras, and Divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, its gods and humans—focuses on as true, the noble ones have clearly seen with right wisdom to be actually false’: this is the first contemplation. ^snp3-12-50-3

‘What this world focuses on as false, the noble ones have clearly seen with right wisdom to be actually true’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-50-4

When a mendicant meditates in this way they can expect enlightenment or non-return.” ^snp3-12-50-5

Then the Teacher went on to say: ^snp3-12-50-6

“See how the world with its gods ^snp3-12-51-1

imagines not-self to be self; ^snp3-12-51-2

habituated to name and form, ^snp3-12-51-3

imagining this is truth. ^snp3-12-51-4

For whatever you imagine it is, ^snp3-12-52-1

it turns out to be something else. ^snp3-12-52-2

And that is what is false in it, ^snp3-12-52-3

for the ephemeral is deceptive by nature. ^snp3-12-52-4

Extinguishment has an undeceptive nature, ^snp3-12-53-1

the noble ones know it as truth. ^snp3-12-53-2

Having comprehended the truth, ^snp3-12-53-3

they are hungerless, quenched.” ^snp3-12-53-4

“Suppose, mendicants, they questioned you thus: ‘Could there be another way to contemplate the pairs?’ ^snp3-12-54-1

You should say, ‘There could.’ ^snp3-12-54-2

And how could there be? ^snp3-12-54-3

‘What this world—with its gods, Māras, and Divinities, this population with its ascetics and brahmins, its gods and humans—focuses on as happiness, the noble ones have clearly seen with right wisdom to be actually suffering’: this is the first contemplation. ^snp3-12-54-4

‘What this world focuses on as suffering, the noble ones have clearly seen with right wisdom to be actually happiness’: this is the second contemplation. ^snp3-12-54-5

When a mendicant meditates rightly contemplating a pair of teachings in this way—diligent, keen, and resolute—they can expect one of two results: ^snp3-12-54-6

enlightenment in this very life, or if there’s residue left behind, non-return.” ^snp3-12-54-7

That is what the Buddha said. ^snp3-12-54-8

Then the Holy One, the Teacher, went on to say: ^snp3-12-54-9

“Sights, sounds, tastes, smells, ^snp3-12-55-1

touches, and ideas, the lot of them— ^snp3-12-55-2

they’re likable, desirable, and pleasurable ^snp3-12-55-3

as long as you can say that they exist. ^snp3-12-55-4

For all the world with its gods, ^snp3-12-56-1

this is what they deem happiness. ^snp3-12-56-2

And where they cease ^snp3-12-56-3

is deemed as suffering for them. ^snp3-12-56-4

The noble ones have seen as happiness ^snp3-12-57-1

the ceasing of substantial reality. ^snp3-12-57-2

This insight by those who see ^snp3-12-57-3

contradicts the whole world. ^snp3-12-57-4

What others say is happiness ^snp3-12-58-1

the noble ones say is suffering. ^snp3-12-58-2

What others say is suffering ^snp3-12-58-3

the noble ones know as happiness. ^snp3-12-58-4

See, this teaching is hard to understand, ^snp3-12-59-1

it confuses the ignorant. ^snp3-12-59-2

There is darkness for the shrouded; ^snp3-12-59-3

blackness for those who don’t see. ^snp3-12-59-4

But the good are open; ^snp3-12-60-1

like light for those who see. ^snp3-12-60-2

Though close, they do not understand, ^snp3-12-60-3

those fools inexpert in the teaching. ^snp3-12-60-4

They’re mired in desire for continued existence, ^snp3-12-61-1

flowing along the stream of lives, ^snp3-12-61-2

mired in Māra’s dominion: ^snp3-12-61-3

this teaching isn’t easy for them to understand. ^snp3-12-61-4

Who, apart from the noble ones, ^snp3-12-62-1

is qualified to understand this state? ^snp3-12-62-2

Having rightly understood this state, ^snp3-12-62-3

the undefiled are fully extinguished.” ^snp3-12-62-4

That is what the Buddha said. ^snp3-12-63-1

Satisfied, the mendicants approved what the Buddha said. ^snp3-12-63-2

And while this discourse was being spoken, the minds of sixty mendicants were freed from defilements by not grasping. ^snp3-12-63-3

## Vagga 4 ^vagga4-0

### Sensual Pleasures (Anthology of Discourses 4.1) ^snp4-1-0

If a mortal desires sensual pleasure ^snp4-1-1-1

and their desire succeeds, ^snp4-1-1-2

they definitely become elated, ^snp4-1-1-3

having got what they want. ^snp4-1-1-4

But for that personage in the throes of pleasure, ^snp4-1-2-1

aroused by desire, ^snp4-1-2-2

when those pleasures fade, ^snp4-1-2-3

it inflicts them like an arrow strike. ^snp4-1-2-4

One who, being mindful, ^snp4-1-3-1

avoids sensual pleasures ^snp4-1-3-2

like side-stepping a snake’s head, ^snp4-1-3-3

transcends attachment to the world. ^snp4-1-3-4

There are many objects of sensual desire: ^snp4-1-4-1

fields, lands, and gold; cattle and horses; ^snp4-1-4-2

slaves and servants; women and relatives. ^snp4-1-4-3

When a man lusts over these, ^snp4-1-4-4

the weak overpower him ^snp4-1-5-1

and adversities crush him. ^snp4-1-5-2

Suffering follows him ^snp4-1-5-3

like water in a leaky boat. ^snp4-1-5-4

That’s why a personage, ever mindful, ^snp4-1-6-1

should avoid sensual pleasures. ^snp4-1-6-2

Give them up and cross the flood, ^snp4-1-6-3

as a bailed-out boat reaches the far shore. ^snp4-1-6-4

### Eight on the Cave (Anthology of Discourses 4.2) ^snp4-2-0

Trapped in a cave, thickly overspread, ^snp4-2-1-1

sunk in delusion they stay. ^snp4-2-1-2

That sort is far from seclusion, ^snp4-2-1-3

for sensual pleasures in the world <j>are not easy to give up. ^snp4-2-1-4

The chains of desire, the bonds of life’s pleasures ^snp4-2-2-1

are hard to escape, for one cannot free another. ^snp4-2-2-2

Looking to the past or the future, ^snp4-2-2-3

they pray for these pleasures or former ones. ^snp4-2-2-4

Greedy, fixated, infatuated by sensual pleasures, ^snp4-2-3-1

they are incorrigible, habitually immoral. ^snp4-2-3-2

When led to suffering they lament, ^snp4-2-3-3

“What will become of us <j>when we pass away from here?” ^snp4-2-3-4

That’s why a personage should train in this life: ^snp4-2-4-1

should you know that anything in the world is wrong, ^snp4-2-4-2

don’t act wrongly on account of that; ^snp4-2-4-3

for the attentive say this life is short. ^snp4-2-4-4

I see the world’s population floundering, ^snp4-2-5-1

given to craving for future lives. ^snp4-2-5-2

Base men wail in the jaws of death, ^snp4-2-5-3

not rid of craving for life after life. ^snp4-2-5-4

See them flounder over belongings, ^snp4-2-6-1

like fish in puddles of a dried-up stream. ^snp4-2-6-2

Seeing this, live unselfishly, ^snp4-2-6-3

forming no attachment to future lives. ^snp4-2-6-4

Rid of desire for both ends, ^snp4-2-7-1

having completely understood contact, free of greed, ^snp4-2-7-2

doing nothing for which they’d blame themselves, ^snp4-2-7-3

the attentive don’t cling to the seen and the heard. ^snp4-2-7-4

Having completely understood perception <j>and having crossed the flood, ^snp4-2-8-1

the sage, not clinging to possessions, ^snp4-2-8-2

with dart plucked out, living diligently, ^snp4-2-8-3

does not hope for this world or the next. ^snp4-2-8-4

### Eight on Malice (Anthology of Discourses 4.3) ^snp4-3-0

Some speak with malicious intent, ^snp4-3-1-1

while others speak set on truth. ^snp4-3-1-2

When disputes come up a sage does not get involved, ^snp4-3-1-3

which is why they’ve no barrenness at all. ^snp4-3-1-4

How can you transcend your own view ^snp4-3-2-1

when you’re led by preference, dogmatic in belief? ^snp4-3-2-2

Inventing your own undertakings, ^snp4-3-2-3

you’d speak according to your notion. ^snp4-3-2-4

Some personages, unasked, tell others ^snp4-3-3-1

of their own precepts and vows. ^snp4-3-3-2

They have an ignoble nature, <j>say those who are skilled, ^snp4-3-3-3

since they speak on themselves of their own accord. ^snp4-3-3-4

A mendicant, peaceful, quenched, ^snp4-3-4-1

never boasts “thus am I” of their precepts. ^snp4-3-4-2

They have a noble nature, say those who are skilled, ^snp4-3-4-3

who have no pretensions about anything in the world. ^snp4-3-4-4

For one who formulates and creates teachings, ^snp4-3-5-1

and promotes them despite their defects, ^snp4-3-5-2

if they see an advantage for themselves, ^snp4-3-5-3

they become dependent on that, <j>relying on unstable peace. ^snp4-3-5-4

It’s not easy to get over dogmatic views ^snp4-3-6-1

adopted after judging among the teachings. ^snp4-3-6-2

That’s why, among all these dogmas, a person ^snp4-3-6-3

ditches one teaching and takes up another. ^snp4-3-6-4

The cleansed one has no formulated view ^snp4-3-7-1

at all in the world about the different realms. ^snp4-3-7-2

Having given up illusion and conceit, ^snp4-3-7-3

by what path would they go? They are not involved. ^snp4-3-7-4

For one who gets involved is embroiled in disputes about teachings— ^snp4-3-8-1

but how to dispute with the uninvolved? <j>About what? ^snp4-3-8-2

For picking up and putting down <j>is not what they do; ^snp4-3-8-3

they have shaken off all views in this very life. ^snp4-3-8-4

### Eight on the Pure (Anthology of Discourses 4.4) ^snp4-4-0

“I see someone pure, perfectly healthy; ^snp4-4-1-1

it is vision that grants a person purity.” ^snp4-4-1-2

Recalling this notion of the ultimate, ^snp4-4-1-3

they believe in the notion <j>that there is one who observes purity. ^snp4-4-1-4

If a person were granted purity through what is seen, ^snp4-4-2-1

or if by a notion they could give up suffering, ^snp4-4-2-2

then one with attachments is purified by another: ^snp4-4-2-3

their view betrays them as one who asserts thus. ^snp4-4-2-4

The brahmin speaks not of purity from another ^snp4-4-3-1

in terms of what has been seen, heard, or thought; <j>or by precepts or vows. ^snp4-4-3-2

They are unsullied in the midst of good and evil, ^snp4-4-3-3

letting go what was picked up, <j>without creating anything new here. ^snp4-4-3-4

Having let go the last they lay hold of the next; ^snp4-4-4-1

following impulse, they don’t pass the chain. ^snp4-4-4-2

They grab on and let loose like a monkey ^snp4-4-4-3

grabbing and releasing a branch. ^snp4-4-4-4

Having undertaken their own vows, a personage ^snp4-4-5-1

visits various teachers, <j>being attached to perception. ^snp4-4-5-2

One who knows, having comprehended the truth <j>through the knowledges, ^snp4-4-5-3

does not visit various teachers, being of vast wisdom. ^snp4-4-5-4

They are remote from all things ^snp4-4-6-1

seen, heard, or thought. ^snp4-4-6-2

Seeing them living openly, ^snp4-4-6-3

how could anyone in this world judge them? ^snp4-4-6-4

They don’t make things up or promote them, ^snp4-4-7-1

or speak of the uttermost purity. ^snp4-4-7-2

After untying the tight knot of grasping ^snp4-4-7-3

they long for nothing in the world. ^snp4-4-7-4

The brahmin has stepped over the perimeter; ^snp4-4-8-1

knowing and seeing, they adopt nothing. ^snp4-4-8-2

Neither in love with passion <j>nor besotted by dispassion, ^snp4-4-8-3

there is nothing here they adopt as the ultimate. ^snp4-4-8-4

### Eight on the Ultimate (Anthology of Discourses 4.5) ^snp4-5-0

If, maintaining that theirs is the “ultimate” view, ^snp4-5-1-1

a personage makes it out to be highest in the world; ^snp4-5-1-2

then they declare all others are “lesser”; ^snp4-5-1-3

that’s why they’re not beyond disputes. ^snp4-5-1-4

If they see an advantage for themselves ^snp4-5-2-1

in what’s seen, heard, or thought; <j>or in precepts or vows, ^snp4-5-2-2

in that case, having adopted that one alone, ^snp4-5-2-3

they see all others as inferior. ^snp4-5-2-4

Those who are skilled say that, too, is a knot, ^snp4-5-3-1

relying on which people see others as lesser. ^snp4-5-3-2

That’s why a mendicant ought not rely ^snp4-5-3-3

on what’s seen, heard, or thought, <j>or on precepts and vows. ^snp4-5-3-4

Nor would they form a view about the world ^snp4-5-4-1

through a notion or through precepts and vows. ^snp4-5-4-2

They would never represent themselves as “equal”, ^snp4-5-4-3

nor conceive themselves “worse” or “better”. ^snp4-5-4-4

What was picked up has been set down <j>and is not grasped again; ^snp4-5-5-1

they form no dependency even on notions. ^snp4-5-5-2

They follow no side among the factions, ^snp4-5-5-3

and believe in no view at all. ^snp4-5-5-4

One here who has no wish for either end— ^snp4-5-6-1

for any state of existence in this life or the next— ^snp4-5-6-2

has adopted no dogma at all ^snp4-5-6-3

after judging among the teachings. ^snp4-5-6-4

For them not even the tiniest idea is formulated here ^snp4-5-7-1

regarding what is seen, heard, or thought. ^snp4-5-7-2

That brahmin does not grasp any view— ^snp4-5-7-3

how could anyone in this world judge them? ^snp4-5-7-4

They don’t make things up or promote them, ^snp4-5-8-1

and don’t subscribe to any of the doctrines. ^snp4-5-8-2

The brahmin has no need to be led by precept or vow; ^snp4-5-8-3

gone to the far shore, one such does not return. ^snp4-5-8-4

### Old Age (Anthology of Discourses 4.6) ^snp4-6-0

Short, alas, is this life; ^snp4-6-1-1

you die before a hundred years. ^snp4-6-1-2

Even if you live a little longer, ^snp4-6-1-3

you still die of old age. ^snp4-6-1-4

People grieve over belongings, ^snp4-6-2-1

yet there is no such thing as permanent possessions. ^snp4-6-2-2

Separation is a fact of life; when you see this, ^snp4-6-2-3

you wouldn’t stay living at home. ^snp4-6-2-4

Whatever a person thinks of as belonging to them, ^snp4-6-3-1

that too is given up when they die. ^snp4-6-3-2

Knowing this, an astute follower of mine ^snp4-6-3-3

would not be bent on ownership. ^snp4-6-3-4

Just as, upon awakening, a person does not see ^snp4-6-4-1

what they encountered in a dream; ^snp4-6-4-2

so too you do not see your loved ones ^snp4-6-4-3

when they are dead and gone. ^snp4-6-4-4

You used to see and hear those folk, ^snp4-6-5-1

and call them by their name. ^snp4-6-5-2

Yet the name is all that’s left to tell ^snp4-6-5-3

of a personage when they’re gone. ^snp4-6-5-4

Those who are greedy for belongings ^snp4-6-6-1

don’t give up sorrow, lamentation, and stinginess. ^snp4-6-6-2

That’s why the sages, seers of sanctuary, ^snp4-6-6-3

left possessions behind and wandered. ^snp4-6-6-4

For a mendicant who lives withdrawn, ^snp4-6-7-1

frequenting a secluded seat, ^snp4-6-7-2

they say it’s fitting ^snp4-6-7-3

to not show themselves in a home. ^snp4-6-7-4

The sage is independent everywhere, ^snp4-6-8-1

they don’t form likes or dislikes. ^snp4-6-8-2

Lamentation and stinginess ^snp4-6-8-3

slip off them like water from a leaf. ^snp4-6-8-4

Like a droplet slips from a lotus-leaf, ^snp4-6-9-1

like water from a lotus flower; ^snp4-6-9-2

the sage doesn’t cling to that ^snp4-6-9-3

which is seen or heard or thought. ^snp4-6-9-4

For the one who is cleansed does not conceive ^snp4-6-10-1

in terms of things seen, heard, or thought. ^snp4-6-10-2

They do not wish to be purified by another; ^snp4-6-10-3

they are neither passionate <j>nor growing dispassioned. ^snp4-6-10-4

### With Tissametteyya (Anthology of Discourses 4.7) ^snp4-7-0

“When someone indulges in sex,” ^snp4-7-1-1

said Venerable Tissametteyya, ^snp4-7-1-2

“tell us, good fellow: what trouble befalls them? ^snp4-7-1-3

After hearing your instruction, ^snp4-7-1-4

we shall train in seclusion.” ^snp4-7-1-5

“When someone indulges in sex,” ^snp4-7-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp4-7-2-2

“they forget their instructions ^snp4-7-2-3

and go the wrong way— ^snp4-7-2-4

that is something ignoble in them. ^snp4-7-2-5

Someone who formerly lived alone ^snp4-7-3-1

and then resorts to sex ^snp4-7-3-2

is like a chariot careening off-track; ^snp4-7-3-3

in the world they call them a low, ordinary person. ^snp4-7-3-4

Their former fame and reputation ^snp4-7-4-1

also fall away. ^snp4-7-4-2

Seeing this, they’d train ^snp4-7-4-3

to give up sex. ^snp4-7-4-4

Oppressed by thoughts, ^snp4-7-5-1

they brood like a wretch. ^snp4-7-5-2

When they hear what others are saying, ^snp4-7-5-3

that sort is embarrassed. ^snp4-7-5-4

Then they lash out with verbal daggers ^snp4-7-6-1

when reproached by others. ^snp4-7-6-2

This is their great blind spot; ^snp4-7-6-3

they sink to lies. ^snp4-7-6-4

They once were considered astute, ^snp4-7-7-1

committed to the solitary life. ^snp4-7-7-2

But then they indulged in sex, ^snp4-7-7-3

dragged along by desire like a dullard. ^snp4-7-7-4

Knowing this danger ^snp4-7-8-1

in falling from a former state here, ^snp4-7-8-2

a sage would firmly resolve to wander alone, ^snp4-7-8-3

and would not resort to sex. ^snp4-7-8-4

They’d train themselves only in seclusion; ^snp4-7-9-1

this, for the noble ones, is highest. ^snp4-7-9-2

One who’d not think themselves ‘best’ due to that ^snp4-7-9-3

has truly drawn near to extinguishment. ^snp4-7-9-4

People tied to sensual pleasures envy them: ^snp4-7-10-1

the isolated, wandering sage ^snp4-7-10-2

who has crossed the flood, ^snp4-7-10-3

unconcerned for sensual pleasures.” ^snp4-7-10-4

### With Pasūra (Anthology of Discourses 4.8) ^snp4-8-0

“Here alone is purity,” they say, ^snp4-8-1-1

denying that there is purification in other teachings. ^snp4-8-1-2

Speaking of the beauty <j>in that which they depend on, ^snp4-8-1-3

each one is dogmatic about <j>their own idiosyncratic interpretation. ^snp4-8-1-4

Desiring debate, they plunge into an assembly, ^snp4-8-2-1

where each takes the other as a fool. ^snp4-8-2-2

Relying on others they state their contention, ^snp4-8-2-3

desiring praise while claiming to be skilled. ^snp4-8-2-4

Addicted to debating in the midst of the assembly, ^snp4-8-3-1

their need for praise makes them nervous. ^snp4-8-3-2

But when they’re repudiated they get embarrassed; ^snp4-8-3-3

upset at criticism, they find fault in others. ^snp4-8-3-4

If their doctrine is said to be weak, ^snp4-8-4-1

and judges declare it repudiated, ^snp4-8-4-2

the loser weeps and wails, ^snp4-8-4-3

moaning, “They beat me.” ^snp4-8-4-4

When these disputes come up among ascetics, ^snp4-8-5-1

they get excited or dejected. ^snp4-8-5-2

Seeing this, refrain from contention, ^snp4-8-5-3

for the only purpose is praise and profit. ^snp4-8-5-4

But if, having declared their doctrine, ^snp4-8-6-1

they are praised there in the midst of the assembly, ^snp4-8-6-2

they laugh and gloat because of it, ^snp4-8-6-3

having got what they wanted. ^snp4-8-6-4

Their pride is their downfall, ^snp4-8-7-1

yet they speak from conceit and arrogance. ^snp4-8-7-2

Seeing this, one ought not dispute, ^snp4-8-7-3

for those who are skilled say this is no way to purity. ^snp4-8-7-4

As a warrior, after feasting on royal food, ^snp4-8-8-1

goes roaring, looking for someone to fight— ^snp4-8-8-2

go off and find an opponent, Sūra, ^snp4-8-8-3

for here, as before, there is no-one to combat. ^snp4-8-8-4

When someone disputes about a view they’ve adopted, ^snp4-8-9-1

saying, “This is the only truth,” ^snp4-8-9-2

say to them, “Here you’ll have no adversary ^snp4-8-9-3

when a dispute has come up.” ^snp4-8-9-4

There are those who live far from the crowd, ^snp4-8-10-1

not countering views with view. ^snp4-8-10-2

Who is there to argue with you, Pasūra, ^snp4-8-10-3

among those who grasp nothing here as the highest? ^snp4-8-10-4

And so you come along speculating, ^snp4-8-11-1

thinking up theories in your mind. ^snp4-8-11-2

Now that you’ve challenged <j>someone who is cleansed, ^snp4-8-11-3

you’ll not be able to respond. ^snp4-8-11-4

### With Māgaṇḍiya (Anthology of Discourses 4.9) ^snp4-9-0

“Even when I saw the sirens <j>Craving, Malignancy, and Lust, ^snp4-9-1-1

I had no desire for sex. ^snp4-9-1-2

What is this body full of piss and shit? ^snp4-9-1-3

I wouldn’t even want to touch it with my foot.” ^snp4-9-1-4

“If you do not want a gem such as this, ^snp4-9-2-1

a lady desired by many rulers of men, ^snp4-9-2-2

then what manner of theory, <j>precepts and vows, livelihood, ^snp4-9-2-3

and rebirth in a new life do you assert?” ^snp4-9-2-4

“After judging among the teachings,” ^snp4-9-3-1

said the Buddha to Māgaṇḍiya, ^snp4-9-3-2

“none have been adopted thinking, ‘I assert this.’ ^snp4-9-3-3

Seeing views without adopting any, ^snp4-9-3-4

searching, I saw inner peace.” ^snp4-9-3-5

“O sage, you speak of judgments you have formed,” ^snp4-9-4-1

said Māgaṇḍiya, ^snp4-9-4-2

“without having adopted any of those views. ^snp4-9-4-3

As to that matter of ‘inner peace’— ^snp4-9-4-4

how is that described by the attentive?” ^snp4-9-4-5

“Purity is spoken of not as due to view,” ^snp4-9-5-1

said the Buddha to Māgaṇḍiya, ^snp4-9-5-2

“learning, knowledge, or precepts and vows; ^snp4-9-5-3

nor without view, learning, ^snp4-9-5-4

knowledge, or precepts and vows. ^snp4-9-5-5

Having relinquished these, not adopting them, ^snp4-9-5-6

peaceful, independent, one would not pray <j>for another life.” ^snp4-9-5-7

“It seems purity is spoken of not as due to view,” ^snp4-9-6-1

said Māgaṇḍiya, ^snp4-9-6-2

“learning, knowledge, or precepts and vows; ^snp4-9-6-3

nor without view, learning, ^snp4-9-6-4

knowledge, or precepts and vows. ^snp4-9-6-5

If so, I think this teaching is sheer folly; ^snp4-9-6-6

some believe that purity comes from view.” ^snp4-9-6-7

“Continuing to question while relying on a view,” ^snp4-9-7-1

said the Buddha to Māgaṇḍiya, ^snp4-9-7-2

“you’ve become confused by all you’ve adopted. ^snp4-9-7-3

From this you’ve not glimpsed the slightest idea, ^snp4-9-7-4

which is why you consider the teaching folly. ^snp4-9-7-5

If you think that ‘I’m equal, ^snp4-9-8-1

special, or worse’, you’ll dispute about that. ^snp4-9-8-2

Not wavering due to the three discriminations, ^snp4-9-8-3

they have no thought, ‘I’m equal or special’. ^snp4-9-8-4

Why would that brahmin say, ‘It’s true’, ^snp4-9-9-1

or with whom would they dispute, ‘It’s false’? ^snp4-9-9-2

There is no equal or unequal in them, ^snp4-9-9-3

so who would they take on in debate? ^snp4-9-9-4

After leaving their bastion to migrate without abode, ^snp4-9-10-1

a sage doesn’t get close to anyone in town. ^snp4-9-10-2

Rid of sensual pleasures, not expecting, ^snp4-9-10-3

they wouldn’t get in arguments with people. ^snp4-9-10-4

A spiritual giant would not take up for debate ^snp4-9-11-1

the things in the world from which they live secluded. ^snp4-9-11-2

As a prickly lotus born in the water ^snp4-9-11-3

is unsullied by water and mud, ^snp4-9-11-4

so the greedless sage, proponent of peace, ^snp4-9-11-5

is unsmeared by sensuality and the world. ^snp4-9-11-6

A knowledge master does not become conceited ^snp4-9-12-1

due to view or thought, <j>for they are not determined by that. ^snp4-9-12-2

They’ve no need for deeds or learning, ^snp4-9-12-3

they’re not indoctrinated in dogmas. ^snp4-9-12-4

There are no ties for one detached from ideas; ^snp4-9-13-1

there are no delusions for one freed by wisdom. ^snp4-9-13-2

But those who have adopted ideas and views ^snp4-9-13-3

wander the world causing conflict.” ^snp4-9-13-4

### Before the Breakup (Anthology of Discourses 4.10) ^snp4-10-0

“Seeing how, behaving how, ^snp4-10-1-1

is one said to be at peace? ^snp4-10-1-2

When asked, Gotama, please tell me ^snp4-10-1-3

about the ultimate person.” ^snp4-10-1-4

“Rid of craving before the breakup,” ^snp4-10-2-1

said the Buddha, ^snp4-10-2-2

“not dependent on the first beginning, ^snp4-10-2-3

unfathomable in the middle, ^snp4-10-2-4

they are not governed by anything. ^snp4-10-2-5

Unangry, unafraid, ^snp4-10-3-1

not boastful or regretful, ^snp4-10-3-2

thoughtful in counsel, not restless— ^snp4-10-3-3

truly that sage is controlled in speech. ^snp4-10-3-4

Rid of attachment to the future, ^snp4-10-4-1

they don’t grieve for the past. ^snp4-10-4-2

A seer of seclusion in the midst of contacts ^snp4-10-4-3

is not led astray among views. ^snp4-10-4-4

Withdrawn, free of deceit, ^snp4-10-5-1

they’re not envious or stingy, ^snp4-10-5-2

nor rude or disgusting, ^snp4-10-5-3

or given to slander. ^snp4-10-5-4

Not swept up in pleasures, ^snp4-10-6-1

or given to arrogance, ^snp4-10-6-2

they’re gentle and articulate, ^snp4-10-6-3

neither hungering nor growing dispassionate. ^snp4-10-6-4

Not training for desire for profit, ^snp4-10-7-1

nor getting annoyed at lack of profit; ^snp4-10-7-2

they are not hostile due to craving, ^snp4-10-7-3

for they are not greedy for flavors. ^snp4-10-7-4

Equanimous, ever mindful, ^snp4-10-8-1

they never conceive themselves in the world ^snp4-10-8-2

as equal, special, or less than; ^snp4-10-8-3

they have no pretensions. ^snp4-10-8-4

They have no dependencies, ^snp4-10-9-1

understanding the teaching, they are independent. ^snp4-10-9-2

No craving is found in them ^snp4-10-9-3

to continue existence or to end it. ^snp4-10-9-4

I declare them to be at peace, ^snp4-10-10-1

unconcerned for sensual pleasures. ^snp4-10-10-2

No ties are found in them, ^snp4-10-10-3

they have crossed over clinging. ^snp4-10-10-4

They have no children or livestock, ^snp4-10-11-1

nor possess fields or lands. ^snp4-10-11-2

No picking up or putting down ^snp4-10-11-3

is found in them. ^snp4-10-11-4

That by which one might describe ^snp4-10-12-1

an ordinary person or ascetics and brahmins ^snp4-10-12-2

has no importance to them, ^snp4-10-12-3

which is why they’re unperturbed by words. ^snp4-10-12-4

Freed of greed, not stingy, ^snp4-10-13-1

a sage doesn’t speak of themselves as being ^snp4-10-13-2

among superiors, inferiors, or equals. ^snp4-10-13-3

One not prone to creation <j>does not return to creation. ^snp4-10-13-4

They who have nothing in the world of their own ^snp4-10-14-1

do not grieve for that which is not, ^snp4-10-14-2

or drift among the teachings; ^snp4-10-14-3

that’s who is said to be at peace.” ^snp4-10-14-4

### Quarrels and Disputes (Anthology of Discourses 4.11) ^snp4-11-0

“Where do quarrels and disputes come from? ^snp4-11-1-1

And lamentation and sorrow, and stinginess? ^snp4-11-1-2

What of conceit and arrogance, and slander too— ^snp4-11-1-3

tell me please, where do they come from?” ^snp4-11-1-4

“Quarrels and disputes come from what we hold dear, ^snp4-11-2-1

as do lamentation and sorrow, stinginess, ^snp4-11-2-2

conceit and arrogance. ^snp4-11-2-3

Quarrels and disputes are linked to stinginess, ^snp4-11-2-4

and when disputes have arisen there is slander.” ^snp4-11-2-5

“So where do things held dear <j>in the world spring from? ^snp4-11-3-1

And the lusts that are loose in the world? ^snp4-11-3-2

Where spring the hopes and aims ^snp4-11-3-3

a man has for the next life?” ^snp4-11-3-4

“What we hold dear in the world spring from desire, ^snp4-11-4-1

as do the lusts that are loose in the world. ^snp4-11-4-2

From there spring the hopes and aims ^snp4-11-4-3

a man has for the next life.” ^snp4-11-4-4

“So where does desire in the world spring from? ^snp4-11-5-1

And evaluations, too, where do they come from? ^snp4-11-5-2

And anger, lies, and indecision, ^snp4-11-5-3

and other things spoken of by the Ascetic?” ^snp4-11-5-4

“What they call pleasure and pain in the world— ^snp4-11-6-1

based on that, desire comes about. ^snp4-11-6-2

Seeing the manifestation and vanishing of forms, ^snp4-11-6-3

a personage makes evaluations in the world. ^snp4-11-6-4

Anger, lies, and indecision— ^snp4-11-7-1

these things are, too, when that pair is present. ^snp4-11-7-2

One who is indecisive should train <j>in the path of knowledge; ^snp4-11-7-3

it is from knowledge <j>that the Ascetic speaks of these things.” ^snp4-11-7-4

“Where do pleasure and pain spring from? ^snp4-11-8-1

When what is absent do these things not occur? ^snp4-11-8-2

And also, on the topic of <j>manifesting and vanishing— ^snp4-11-8-3

tell me where they spring from.” ^snp4-11-8-4

“Pleasure and pain spring from contact; ^snp4-11-9-1

when contact is absent they do not occur. ^snp4-11-9-2

And on the topic of <j>manifesting and vanishing— ^snp4-11-9-3

I tell you they spring from there.” ^snp4-11-9-4

“So where does contact in the world spring from? ^snp4-11-10-1

And possessions, too, where do they come from? ^snp4-11-10-2

When what is absent is there no possessiveness? ^snp4-11-10-3

When what vanishes do contacts not strike?” ^snp4-11-10-4

“Name and form cause contact; ^snp4-11-11-1

possessions spring from wishing; ^snp4-11-11-2

when wishing is absent there is no possessiveness; ^snp4-11-11-3

when form vanishes, contacts don’t strike.” ^snp4-11-11-4

“Form vanishes for one proceeding how? ^snp4-11-12-1

And how do happiness and suffering vanish? ^snp4-11-12-2

Tell me how they vanish; ^snp4-11-12-3

I think we ought to know these things.” ^snp4-11-12-4

“Without normal perception <j>or distorted perception; ^snp4-11-13-1

not lacking perception, nor perceiving what has vanished. ^snp4-11-13-2

Form vanishes for one proceeding thus; ^snp4-11-13-3

for judgments due to proliferation spring from perception.” ^snp4-11-13-4

“Whatever I asked you have explained to me. ^snp4-11-14-1

I ask you once more, please tell me this: ^snp4-11-14-2

Do some astute folk here say that this <j>is the highest extent ^snp4-11-14-3

of purity of the spirit? ^snp4-11-14-4

Or do they say it is something else?” ^snp4-11-14-5

“Some astute folk do say that this <j>is the highest extent ^snp4-11-15-1

of purity of the spirit. ^snp4-11-15-2

But some of them, claiming to be experts, ^snp4-11-15-3

speak of an occasion when there is no residue. ^snp4-11-15-4

Knowing that these states are dependent, ^snp4-11-16-1

and knowing what they depend on, <j>the inquiring sage, ^snp4-11-16-2

having understood, is freed, and enters no dispute. ^snp4-11-16-3

The attentive do not proceed to life after life.” ^snp4-11-16-4

### The Shorter Discourse on Arrayed For Battle (Anthology of Discourses 4.12) ^snp4-12-0

“Each maintaining their own view, ^snp4-12-1-1

those who are skilled disagree, arguing: ^snp4-12-1-2

‘Whoever sees it this way <j>understands the teaching; ^snp4-12-1-3

those who reject this are inadequate.’ ^snp4-12-1-4

So arguing, they dispute, ^snp4-12-2-1

saying, ‘The other is a fool, an amateur!’ ^snp4-12-2-2

Which of these is a true statement, ^snp4-12-2-3

for all of them profess to be experts?” ^snp4-12-2-4

“If not accepting another’s teaching ^snp4-12-3-1

makes you a useless fool lacking wisdom, ^snp4-12-3-2

then they’re all fools lacking wisdom, ^snp4-12-3-3

for they all maintain their own view. ^snp4-12-3-4

But if having your own view makes you pristine— ^snp4-12-4-1

pure in wisdom, expert and intelligent— ^snp4-12-4-2

then none of them lack wisdom, ^snp4-12-4-3

for such is the view they have all embraced. ^snp4-12-4-4

I do not say that it is correct ^snp4-12-5-1

when they call each other fools. ^snp4-12-5-2

Each has built up their own view to be the truth, ^snp4-12-5-3

which is why they take the other as a fool.” ^snp4-12-5-4

“What some say is true and correct, ^snp4-12-6-1

others say is hollow and false. ^snp4-12-6-2

So arguing, they dispute; ^snp4-12-6-3

why don’t ascetics say the same thing?” ^snp4-12-6-4

“The truth is one, there is no second, ^snp4-12-7-1

understanding which folk would not dispute. ^snp4-12-7-2

But those ascetics each boast of different truths; ^snp4-12-7-3

that’s why they don’t say the same thing.” ^snp4-12-7-4

“But why do they speak of different truths, ^snp4-12-8-1

these proponents who profess to be experts? ^snp4-12-8-2

Are there really so many different truths, ^snp4-12-8-3

or do they just follow their own lines of reasoning?” ^snp4-12-8-4

“No, there are not many different truths ^snp4-12-9-1

that, apart from perception, <j>are lasting in the world. ^snp4-12-9-2

Having formed their reasoning <j>regarding different views, ^snp4-12-9-3

they say there are two things: true and false. ^snp4-12-9-4

The seen, heard, or thought, <j>or precepts or vows— ^snp4-12-10-1

based on these they show disdain. ^snp4-12-10-2

Standing in judgment, they scoff, ^snp4-12-10-3

saying, ‘The other is a fool, an amateur!’ ^snp4-12-10-4

They take the other as a fool on the same grounds ^snp4-12-11-1

that they speak of themselves as an expert. ^snp4-12-11-2

Professing to be expert on their own authority, ^snp4-12-11-3

they disdain the other while saying the same thing. ^snp4-12-11-4

They are perfect, <j>according to their own extreme view; ^snp4-12-12-1

drunk on conceit, imagining themselves proficient. ^snp4-12-12-2

They have anointed themselves in their own mind, ^snp4-12-12-3

for such is the view they have embraced. ^snp4-12-12-4

If the word of your opponent makes you deficient, ^snp4-12-13-1

then they too are lacking wisdom. ^snp4-12-13-2

But if on your own authority you’re a knowledge master, <j>an attentive one, ^snp4-12-13-3

then there are no fools among the ascetics. ^snp4-12-13-4

‘Those who proclaim a teaching other than this ^snp4-12-14-1

have fallen short of purity, and are inadequate’: ^snp4-12-14-2

so say each one of the sectarians, ^snp4-12-14-3

for they are deeply attached to their own view. ^snp4-12-14-4

‘Here alone is purity,’ they say, ^snp4-12-15-1

denying that there is purification in other teachings. ^snp4-12-15-2

Thus each one of the sectarians, being dogmatic, ^snp4-12-15-3

speaks forcefully within the context <j>of their own journey. ^snp4-12-15-4

But in that case, so long as they are speaking forcefully <j>of their own journey, ^snp4-12-16-1

how can they take the other as a fool? ^snp4-12-16-2

They are the ones who provoke conflict ^snp4-12-16-3

when they call the other a fool <j>with an impure teaching. ^snp4-12-16-4

Standing in judgment, <j>measuring by their own standard, ^snp4-12-17-1

they dispute with the world more and more. ^snp4-12-17-2

But a personage who has given up all judgments ^snp4-12-17-3

creates no conflict in the world.” ^snp4-12-17-4

### The Longer Discourse on Arrayed for Battle (Anthology of Discourses 4.13) ^snp4-13-0

“Regarding those who maintain their own view, ^snp4-13-1-1

disputing that, ‘This is the only truth’: ^snp4-13-1-2

are all of them subject only to criticism, ^snp4-13-1-3

or do some also win praise for that?” ^snp4-13-1-4

“That is a small thing, insufficient for peace, ^snp4-13-2-1

these two fruits of dispute, I say. ^snp4-13-2-2

Seeing this, one ought not dispute, ^snp4-13-2-3

looking for sanctuary in the land of no dispute. ^snp4-13-2-4

One who knows does not get involved ^snp4-13-3-1

with any of the many different conventions. ^snp4-13-3-2

Why would the uninvolved get involved, ^snp4-13-3-3

since they do not believe <j>based on the seen or the heard? ^snp4-13-3-4

Those for whom ethics are the highest speak of purity through self-control; ^snp4-13-4-1

having undertaken a vow, they stick to it: ^snp4-13-4-2

‘Let us train right here, then we will be pure.’ ^snp4-13-4-3

Professing to be experts, they are led on to future lives. ^snp4-13-4-4
