If they fall away from their precepts and vows, ^snp4-13-5-1

they tremble, having failed in their task. ^snp4-13-5-2

They pray and long for purity, ^snp4-13-5-3

like one who has lost their caravan <j>while journeying far from home. ^snp4-13-5-4

But having given up all precepts and vows, ^snp4-13-6-1

and these deeds blameworthy or blameless; ^snp4-13-6-2

not longing for ‘purity’ or ‘impurity’, ^snp4-13-6-3

live detached, fostering peace. ^snp4-13-6-4

Relying on mortification in disgust of sin, ^snp4-13-7-1

or else on what is seen, heard, or thought, ^snp4-13-7-2

they moan that purification <j>comes through heading upstream, ^snp4-13-7-3

not rid of craving for life after life. ^snp4-13-7-4

For one who longs there are muttered prayers, ^snp4-13-8-1

and trembling too over ideas they have formed. ^snp4-13-8-2

But one here for whom there is no passing away <j>or reappearing: ^snp4-13-8-3

why would they tremble? For what would they pray?” ^snp4-13-8-4

“The very same teaching that some say is ‘ultimate’, ^snp4-13-9-1

others say is inferior. ^snp4-13-9-2

Which of these statements is true, ^snp4-13-9-3

for all of them profess to be experts?” ^snp4-13-9-4

“They say their own teaching is perfect, ^snp4-13-10-1

while the teaching of others is inferior. ^snp4-13-10-2

So arguing, they dispute, ^snp4-13-10-3

each saying their own conventions are the truth. ^snp4-13-10-4

If someone else’s disparagement makes you inferior, ^snp4-13-11-1

no-one in any teaching would be distinguished. ^snp4-13-11-2

For each of them says the other’s teaching is lacking, ^snp4-13-11-3

while forcefully advocating their own. ^snp4-13-11-4

But if they honor their own teachings ^snp4-13-12-1

just as they praise their own journeys, ^snp4-13-12-2

then all doctrines would be equally valid, ^snp4-13-12-3

and purity would be specific to them. ^snp4-13-12-4

After judging among the teachings, <j>a brahmin has adopted nothing ^snp4-13-13-1

that requires interpretation by another. ^snp4-13-13-2

That’s why they’ve gotten over disputes, ^snp4-13-13-3

for they see no other doctrine as best. ^snp4-13-13-4

Saying, ‘I know, I see, that’s how it is’, ^snp4-13-14-1

some believe that purity stems from view. ^snp4-13-14-2

But if they’ve really seen, <j>what use is that view to them? ^snp4-13-14-3

Overlooking what matters, <j>they say purity comes from another. ^snp4-13-14-4

When a person sees, they see name and form, ^snp4-13-15-1

and having seen, they will know just these things. ^snp4-13-15-2

Gladly let them see much or little, ^snp4-13-15-3

for those who are skilled say this is no way to purity. ^snp4-13-15-4

It’s not easy to educate someone who is dogmatic, ^snp4-13-16-1

promoting a view they have formulated. ^snp4-13-16-2

Speaking of the beauty <j>in that on which they depend, ^snp4-13-16-3

they talk of purity in accord with what they saw there. ^snp4-13-16-4

The brahmin does not get involved <j>with formulating and calculating; ^snp4-13-17-1

they’re not followers of views, <j>nor kinsmen of notions. ^snp4-13-17-2

Having understood the many different conventions, ^snp4-13-17-3

they look on when others grasp. ^snp4-13-17-4

Having untied the knots here in the world, ^snp4-13-18-1

the sage takes no side among disputes. ^snp4-13-18-2

Peaceful among the peaceless, equanimous, ^snp4-13-18-3

they don’t grasp when others grasp. ^snp4-13-18-4

Having given up former defilements <j>and not making new ones, ^snp4-13-19-1

not swayed by preference, <j>nor a proponent of dogma, ^snp4-13-19-2

that attentive one is released from views, ^snp4-13-19-3

not clinging to the world, <j>nor reproaching themselves. ^snp4-13-19-4

They are remote from all things ^snp4-13-20-1

seen, heard, or thought. ^snp4-13-20-2

With burden put down, the sage is released: ^snp4-13-20-3

not formulating, not abstaining, not longing.” ^snp4-13-20-4

### Speedy (Anthology of Discourses 4.14) ^snp4-14-0

“Great seer, I ask you, the Kinsman of the Sun, ^snp4-14-1-1

about seclusion and the state of peace. ^snp4-14-1-2

How, having seen, is a mendicant extinguished, ^snp4-14-1-3

not grasping anything in this world?” ^snp4-14-1-4

“They would cut off the idea, ‘I am the thinker’,” ^snp4-14-2-1

said the Buddha, ^snp4-14-2-2

“which is the root of all judgments <j>that emerge from proliferation. ^snp4-14-2-3

Ever mindful, they would train to remove ^snp4-14-2-4

any internal cravings. ^snp4-14-2-5

Regardless of what things they know, ^snp4-14-3-1

whether internal or external, ^snp4-14-3-2

they wouldn’t be proud because of that, ^snp4-14-3-3

for that is not quenching, say the good. ^snp4-14-3-4

They wouldn’t let that make them conceited, ^snp4-14-4-1

thinking themselves better or worse or alike. ^snp4-14-4-2

When questioned in many ways, ^snp4-14-4-3

they wouldn’t keep justifying themselves. ^snp4-14-4-4

A mendicant would find peace inside themselves, ^snp4-14-5-1

and not seek peace from another. ^snp4-14-5-2

For one at peace inside themselves, ^snp4-14-5-3

there’s no picking up, whence putting down? ^snp4-14-5-4

Just as, in the middle of the ocean, ^snp4-14-6-1

no waves arise, it stays still; ^snp4-14-6-2

so too one unstirred is still— ^snp4-14-6-3

a mendicant would make <j>no pretension regarding anything.” ^snp4-14-6-4

“He whose eyes are open has explained ^snp4-14-7-1

the truth he witnessed, <j>where adversities are removed. ^snp4-14-7-2

Please now speak of the practice, sir, ^snp4-14-7-3

the monastic code and immersion in samādhi.” ^snp4-14-7-4

“With eyes not wanton, ^snp4-14-8-1

they’d turn their ears from village gossip. ^snp4-14-8-2

They wouldn’t be greedy for flavors, ^snp4-14-8-3

nor possessive about anything in the world. ^snp4-14-8-4

Though struck by contacts, ^snp4-14-9-1

a mendicant would not lament at all. ^snp4-14-9-2

They wouldn’t pray for another life, ^snp4-14-9-3

nor tremble in the face of dangers. ^snp4-14-9-4

When they receive food and drink, ^snp4-14-10-1

edibles and clothes, ^snp4-14-10-2

they wouldn’t store them up, ^snp4-14-10-3

nor worry about not getting them. ^snp4-14-10-4

Meditative, not footloose, ^snp4-14-11-1

they’d avoid remorse and not be negligent. ^snp4-14-11-2

Then a mendicant would stay ^snp4-14-11-3

in quiet places to sit and rest. ^snp4-14-11-4

They wouldn’t take much sleep, ^snp4-14-12-1

but, being keen, <j>would apply themselves to wakefulness. ^snp4-14-12-2

They’d give up sloth, illusion, mirth, and play, ^snp4-14-12-3

and sex and ornamentation. ^snp4-14-12-4

They wouldn’t cast Artharvaṇa spells, <j>interpret dreams ^snp4-14-13-1

or omens, or practice astrology. ^snp4-14-13-2

My followers would not study ominous animal cries, ^snp4-14-13-3

work as a doctor, or treat an impacted fetus. ^snp4-14-13-4

Not shaken by criticism, ^snp4-14-14-1

a mendicant would not gloat when praised. ^snp4-14-14-2

They’d cast aside greed and stinginess, ^snp4-14-14-3

anger, and slander. ^snp4-14-14-4

They’d not stand for buying and selling; ^snp4-14-15-1

a mendicant would not speak ill at all. ^snp4-14-15-2

They wouldn’t linger in the village, ^snp4-14-15-3

nor cajole people from desire for profit. ^snp4-14-15-4

A mendicant would be no boaster, ^snp4-14-16-1

nor would they speak suggestively. ^snp4-14-16-2

They wouldn’t train in impudence, ^snp4-14-16-3

nor speak argumentatively. ^snp4-14-16-4

They wouldn’t be led into lying, ^snp4-14-17-1

nor be deliberately devious. ^snp4-14-17-2

And they’d never look down on another ^snp4-14-17-3

because of livelihood, wisdom, or precepts and vows. ^snp4-14-17-4

Though provoked from hearing much talk ^snp4-14-18-1

from ascetics saying all different things, ^snp4-14-18-2

they wouldn’t react harshly, ^snp4-14-18-3

for the virtuous do not retaliate. ^snp4-14-18-4

Having understood this teaching, ^snp4-14-19-1

inquiring, a mendicant would always train mindfully. ^snp4-14-19-2

Knowing quenching as peace, ^snp4-14-19-3

they’d not be negligent in Gotama’s bidding. ^snp4-14-19-4

For he is the vanquisher, the unvanquished, ^snp4-14-20-1

seer of the truth as witness, not by hearsay— ^snp4-14-20-2

that’s why, being diligent, they would always train ^snp4-14-20-3

respectfully in the Buddha’s teaching.” ^snp4-14-20-4

### Taking Up Arms (Anthology of Discourses 4.15) ^snp4-15-0

Peril stems from those who take up arms— ^snp4-15-1-1

just look how people conflict! ^snp4-15-1-2

I shall extol how I came to be ^snp4-15-1-3

stirred with a sense of urgency. ^snp4-15-1-4

I saw this population flounder, ^snp4-15-2-1

like a fish in a little puddle. ^snp4-15-2-2

Seeing them at odds with each other, ^snp4-15-2-3

fear came upon me. ^snp4-15-2-4

The world around was volatile, ^snp4-15-3-1

all directions were in turmoil. ^snp4-15-3-2

Wanting a home for myself, ^snp4-15-3-3

I saw nowhere unsettled. ^snp4-15-3-4

Yet even in their settlement they were at odds— ^snp4-15-4-1

seeing that, I grew uneasy. ^snp4-15-4-2

Then I saw a dart there, ^snp4-15-4-3

so hard to see, stuck in the heart. ^snp4-15-4-4

When struck by that dart, ^snp4-15-5-1

you run around in all directions. ^snp4-15-5-2

But when that same dart has been plucked out, ^snp4-15-5-3

you neither run around nor sink down. ^snp4-15-5-4

(On that topic, the trainings are recited.) ^snp4-15-6-1

Whatever attachments there are in the world, ^snp4-15-6-2

don’t pursue them. ^snp4-15-6-3

Having pierced through sensual pleasures <j>in every way, ^snp4-15-6-4

train yourself for extinguishment. ^snp4-15-6-5

Be truthful, not rude, ^snp4-15-7-1

free of deceit, and rid of slander; ^snp4-15-7-2

without anger, a sage would cross over ^snp4-15-7-3

the evils of greed and avarice. ^snp4-15-7-4

Prevail over sleepiness, sloth, and drowsiness, ^snp4-15-8-1

don’t abide in negligence, ^snp4-15-8-2

A person intent on quenching ^snp4-15-8-3

would not stand for arrogance. ^snp4-15-8-4

Don’t be led into lying, ^snp4-15-9-1

or get caught up in affection for form. ^snp4-15-9-2

Completely understand conceit, ^snp4-15-9-3

and desist from hasty conduct. ^snp4-15-9-4

Don’t relish the old, ^snp4-15-10-1

or welcome the new. ^snp4-15-10-2

Don’t grieve for what is running out, ^snp4-15-10-3

or get attached to things that pull you in. ^snp4-15-10-4

Greed, I say, is the great flood, ^snp4-15-11-1

and longing is the current— ^snp4-15-11-2

the basis, the compulsion, ^snp4-15-11-3

the swamp of sensuality so hard to get past. ^snp4-15-11-4

The sage never strays from the truth; ^snp4-15-12-1

the brahmin stands on solid ground. ^snp4-15-12-2

Having given up everything, ^snp4-15-12-3

they are said to be at peace. ^snp4-15-12-4

They have truly known, they’re a knowledge master, ^snp4-15-13-1

understanding the teaching, they are independent. ^snp4-15-13-2

They rightly proceed in the world, ^snp4-15-13-3

not coveting anything here. ^snp4-15-13-4

One who has crossed over sensuality here, ^snp4-15-14-1

the chain in the world so hard to get past, ^snp4-15-14-2

grieves not, nor hopes; ^snp4-15-14-3

they’ve cut the strings, they’re no longer bound. ^snp4-15-14-4

What came before, let wither away, ^snp4-15-15-1

and after, let there be nothing. ^snp4-15-15-2

If you don’t grasp at the middle, ^snp4-15-15-3

you will live at peace. ^snp4-15-15-4

One who has no sense of ownership ^snp4-15-16-1

in the whole realm of name and form, ^snp4-15-16-2

does not grieve for that which is not, ^snp4-15-16-3

they suffer no loss in the world. ^snp4-15-16-4

If you don’t think of anything ^snp4-15-17-1

as belonging to yourself or others, ^snp4-15-17-2

not finding anything to be “mine”, ^snp4-15-17-3

you won’t grieve, thinking “I don’t have it”. ^snp4-15-17-4

Not bitter, not fawning, ^snp4-15-18-1

unstirred, everywhere even; ^snp4-15-18-2

when asked about one who is unwavering, ^snp4-15-18-3

I declare that that is the benefit. ^snp4-15-18-4

For the unstirred who understand, ^snp4-15-19-1

there’s no performance of deeds. ^snp4-15-19-2

Desisting from instigation, ^snp4-15-19-3

they see sanctuary everywhere. ^snp4-15-19-4

A sage doesn’t speak of themselves as being ^snp4-15-20-1

among superiors, inferiors, or equals. ^snp4-15-20-2

Peaceful, rid of stinginess, ^snp4-15-20-3

they are neither ditching nor taking up. ^snp4-15-20-4

### With Sāriputta (Anthology of Discourses 4.16) ^snp4-16-0

“Never before have I seen,” ^snp4-16-1-1

said Venerable Sāriputta, ^snp4-16-1-2

“or heard from anyone ^snp4-16-1-3

about a teacher of such graceful speech, ^snp4-16-1-4

come from Tusita heaven to lead a community. ^snp4-16-1-5

To all the world with its gods ^snp4-16-2-1

he appears as a Clear-eyed One ^snp4-16-2-2

who has dispelled all darkness, ^snp4-16-2-3

and alone attained to bliss. ^snp4-16-2-4

On behalf of the many here still bound, ^snp4-16-3-1

I have come seeking with a question ^snp4-16-3-2

to that Buddha, unattached and impartial, ^snp4-16-3-3

free of deceit, come to lead a community. ^snp4-16-3-4

Suppose a mendicant who loathes attachment ^snp4-16-4-1

frequents a lonely lodging— ^snp4-16-4-2

the root of a tree, a charnel ground, ^snp4-16-4-3

on mountains, or in caves. ^snp4-16-4-4

In these many different lodgings, ^snp4-16-5-1

how many dangers are there ^snp4-16-5-2

at which a mendicant in their silent lodging ^snp4-16-5-3

ought not tremble? ^snp4-16-5-4

On their journey to the untrodden place, ^snp4-16-6-1

how many adversities are there in the world ^snp4-16-6-2

that must they overcome ^snp4-16-6-3

in their remote lodging? ^snp4-16-6-4

What ways of speech should they have? ^snp4-16-7-1

Of what kind is their alms resort? ^snp4-16-7-2

What precepts and vows ^snp4-16-7-3

should a resolute mendicant uphold? ^snp4-16-7-4

Having undertaken what training, ^snp4-16-8-1

unified, alert, and mindful, ^snp4-16-8-2

would they purge their own stains, ^snp4-16-8-3

like a smith smelting silver?” ^snp4-16-8-4

“If one who loathes attachment <j>frequents a lonely lodging,” ^snp4-16-9-1

said the Buddha to Sāriputta, ^snp4-16-9-2

“in their search for awakening—<j>as accords with the teaching— ^snp4-16-9-3

I shall tell you, as I understand it, ^snp4-16-9-4

what is comfortable for them. ^snp4-16-9-5

An attentive one, a mindful mendicant <j>living on the periphery ^snp4-16-10-1

should not be afraid of five perils: ^snp4-16-10-2

flies, mosquitoes, reptiles, ^snp4-16-10-3

human contact, or four-legged creatures. ^snp4-16-10-4

Nor should they fear followers of other teachings, ^snp4-16-11-1

even having seen the many threats they pose. ^snp4-16-11-2

And then one seeking the good ^snp4-16-11-3

should overcome any other adversities. ^snp4-16-11-4

Afflicted by illness and hunger, ^snp4-16-12-1

they should endure cold and excessive heat. ^snp4-16-12-2

Though afflicted by many such things, <j>the homeless one ^snp4-16-12-3

should exert energy, firmly striving. ^snp4-16-12-4

They must not steal or lie; ^snp4-16-13-1

and should touch creatures firm or frail with love. ^snp4-16-13-2

If they notice any clouding of the mind, ^snp4-16-13-3

they should dispel it as Māra’s ally. ^snp4-16-13-4

They must not fall under the sway <j>of anger or arrogance; ^snp4-16-14-1

having dug them out by the root, <j>they would stand firm. ^snp4-16-14-2

Then, faced with likes and dislikes, ^snp4-16-14-3

they would surely overcome. ^snp4-16-14-4

Putting wisdom in the foremost place, <j>rejoicing in goodness, ^snp4-16-15-1

they would put an end to those adversities. ^snp4-16-15-2

They’d vanquish discontent in their remote lodging. ^snp4-16-15-3

And they’d vanquish the four lamentations: ^snp4-16-15-4

‘What will I eat? Where will I eat? ^snp4-16-16-1

Oh, I slept badly! Where will I sleep?’ ^snp4-16-16-2

The trainee, the migrant without abode, ^snp4-16-16-3

would dispel these lamentable thoughts. ^snp4-16-16-4

Receiving food and clothes in due season, ^snp4-16-17-1

they would know moderation <j>for the sake of contentment. ^snp4-16-17-2

Guarded in these things, <j>walking restrained in the village, ^snp4-16-17-3

they wouldn’t speak harshly even when provoked. ^snp4-16-17-4

Eyes downcast, not footloose, ^snp4-16-18-1

devoted to absorption, they’d be very wakeful. ^snp4-16-18-2

Grounded in equanimity, serene, ^snp4-16-18-3

they should cut off worrisome habits of thought. ^snp4-16-18-4

A mindful one would welcome reproach, ^snp4-16-19-1

and would dissolve hard-heartedness <j>toward their spiritual companions. ^snp4-16-19-2

They may utter skillful speech, but not for too long, ^snp4-16-19-3

and they shouldn’t provoke people to blame. ^snp4-16-19-4

And there are five more taints in the world, ^snp4-16-20-1

for the removal of which <j>the mindful one should train, ^snp4-16-20-2

vanquishing desire for sights, ^snp4-16-20-3

sounds, flavors, smells, and touches. ^snp4-16-20-4

Having removed desire for these things, ^snp4-16-21-1

a mindful mendicant, their heart well freed, ^snp4-16-21-2

rightly investigating the Dhamma in good time, ^snp4-16-21-3

at one, would banish the darkness.” ^snp4-16-21-4

## Vagga 5 ^vagga5-0

### Introductory Verses (Anthology of Discourses 5.1) ^snp5-1-0

From the fair city of the Kosalans ^snp5-1-1-1

to the southern region ^snp5-1-1-2

came a brahmin expert in hymns, ^snp5-1-1-3

aspiring to nothingness. ^snp5-1-1-4

He settled in a region under the shared dominion ^snp5-1-2-1

of Assaka and Muḷaka, ^snp5-1-2-2

where he lived on the bank of the Godāvarī River, ^snp5-1-2-3

getting by on gleanings and fruit. ^snp5-1-2-4

He was supported ^snp5-1-3-1

by a prosperous village nearby. ^snp5-1-3-2

With the revenue earned from there ^snp5-1-3-3

he performed a great sacrifice. ^snp5-1-3-4

When he had completed the great sacrifice, ^snp5-1-4-1

he returned to his hermitage once more. ^snp5-1-4-2

Upon his return, ^snp5-1-4-3

another brahmin arrived. ^snp5-1-4-4

Foot-sore and thirsty, ^snp5-1-5-1

with grotty teeth and dusty head, ^snp5-1-5-2

he approached the other ^snp5-1-5-3

and asked for five hundred coins. ^snp5-1-5-4

When Bāvari saw him, ^snp5-1-6-1

he invited him to sit down, ^snp5-1-6-2

asked of his happiness and well-being, ^snp5-1-6-3

and said the following. ^snp5-1-6-4

“Whatever I had available to give, ^snp5-1-7-1

I have already distributed. ^snp5-1-7-2

Believe me, brahmin, ^snp5-1-7-3

I don’t have five hundred coins.” ^snp5-1-7-4

“If, good fellow, you do not ^snp5-1-8-1

give me what I ask, ^snp5-1-8-2

then on the seventh day, ^snp5-1-8-3

let your head explode in seven!” ^snp5-1-8-4

After performing a ritual, ^snp5-1-9-1

that charlatan uttered his dreadful curse. ^snp5-1-9-2

When he heard these words, ^snp5-1-9-3

Bāvari became distressed. ^snp5-1-9-4

Not eating, he grew emaciated, ^snp5-1-10-1

stricken by the dart of sorrow. ^snp5-1-10-2

And in such a state of mind, ^snp5-1-10-3

he could not enjoy absorption. ^snp5-1-10-4

Seeing him anxious and distraught, ^snp5-1-11-1

a goddess wishing to help, ^snp5-1-11-2

approached Bāvari ^snp5-1-11-3

and said the following. ^snp5-1-11-4

“That charlatan understands nothing ^snp5-1-12-1

about the head, he only wants money. ^snp5-1-12-2

When it comes to heads or head-splitting, ^snp5-1-12-3

he has no knowledge at all.” ^snp5-1-12-4

“Then you, Madam, must know— ^snp5-1-13-1

please answer my question. ^snp5-1-13-2

Let me hear what you say ^snp5-1-13-3

about heads and head-splitting.” ^snp5-1-13-4

“I too do not know that, ^snp5-1-14-1

I have no knowledge in that matter. ^snp5-1-14-2

When it comes to heads or head-splitting, ^snp5-1-14-3

it is the Victors who have vision.” ^snp5-1-14-4

“So who then, ^snp5-1-15-1

in all this vast territory, ^snp5-1-15-2

does know about heads and head-splitting? ^snp5-1-15-3

Please tell me, goddess.” ^snp5-1-15-4

“From the city of Kapilavatthu ^snp5-1-16-1

the World Leader has gone forth. ^snp5-1-16-2

He is a scion of King Okkāka, ^snp5-1-16-3

a Sakyan, and a beacon. ^snp5-1-16-4

For he, brahmin, is the Awakened One! ^snp5-1-17-1

He has gone beyond all things; ^snp5-1-17-2

he has attained to all knowledge and power; ^snp5-1-17-3

his eye sees clearly in all things, ^snp5-1-17-4

he has attained the end of all deeds; ^snp5-1-17-5

he is freed with the ending of attachments. ^snp5-1-17-6

That Buddha, the Blessed One in the world, ^snp5-1-18-1

the Clear-eyed One, teaches Dhamma. ^snp5-1-18-2

Go to him and ask— ^snp5-1-18-3

he will answer you.” ^snp5-1-18-4

When he heard the word “Buddha”, ^snp5-1-19-1

Bāvari was elated. ^snp5-1-19-2

His sorrow faded, ^snp5-1-19-3

and he was filled to brimming with joy. ^snp5-1-19-4

Uplifted, elated, and inspired, ^snp5-1-20-1

Bāvari questioned that goddess: ^snp5-1-20-2

“But in what village or town, ^snp5-1-20-3

or in what land is the protector of the world, ^snp5-1-20-4

where we may go and pay respects ^snp5-1-20-5

to the Awakened One, best of men?” ^snp5-1-20-6

“Near Sāvatthī, home of the Kosalans, <j>is the Victor ^snp5-1-21-1

abounding in wisdom, vast in intelligence. ^snp5-1-21-2

That Sakyan is indefatigable, free of defilements, ^snp5-1-21-3

that captain of men understands head-splitting.” ^snp5-1-21-4

Therefore he addressed his acolytes, ^snp5-1-22-1

brahmins who had mastered the hymns: ^snp5-1-22-2

“Come, students, I shall speak. ^snp5-1-22-3

Listen to what I say. ^snp5-1-22-4

Today has arisen in the world ^snp5-1-23-1

one whose appearance in the world ^snp5-1-23-2

is hard to find again— ^snp5-1-23-3

he is renowned as the Awakened One. ^snp5-1-23-4

Quickly go to Sāvatthī ^snp5-1-23-5

and see the best of men.” ^snp5-1-23-6

“Brahmin, how then are we to know ^snp5-1-24-1

the Buddha when we see him? ^snp5-1-24-2

We don’t know, please tell us, ^snp5-1-24-3

so we can recognize him.” ^snp5-1-24-4

“The marks of a great man ^snp5-1-25-1

have been handed down in our hymns. ^snp5-1-25-2

Thirty-two have been described, ^snp5-1-25-3

complete and in order. ^snp5-1-25-4

One upon whose body is found ^snp5-1-26-1

these marks of a great man ^snp5-1-26-2

has two possible destinies, ^snp5-1-26-3

there is no third. ^snp5-1-26-4

If he stays at home, ^snp5-1-27-1

having conquered this land ^snp5-1-27-2

without rod or sword, ^snp5-1-27-3

he shall govern by principle. ^snp5-1-27-4

But if he goes forth ^snp5-1-28-1

from the lay life to homelessness, ^snp5-1-28-2

he becomes an Awakened One, a perfected one, ^snp5-1-28-3

with veil cast off, supreme. ^snp5-1-28-4

Ask him about my birth, clan, and marks, ^snp5-1-29-1

my hymns and acolytes; and further, ^snp5-1-29-2

about heads and head-splitting— ^snp5-1-29-3

but do so only in your mind! ^snp5-1-29-4

If he is the Buddha ^snp5-1-30-1

of unhindered vision, ^snp5-1-30-2

he will answer with his voice ^snp5-1-30-3

the questions in your mind.” ^snp5-1-30-4

Sixteen brahmin acolytes ^snp5-1-31-1

heard what Bāvari said: ^snp5-1-31-2

Ajita, Tissametteyya, ^snp5-1-31-3

Puṇṇaka and Mettagū, ^snp5-1-31-4

Dhotaka and Upasīva, ^snp5-1-32-1

Nanda and then Hemaka, ^snp5-1-32-2

both Todeyya and Kappa, ^snp5-1-32-3

and Jatukaṇṇī the astute, ^snp5-1-32-4

Bhadrāvudha and Udaya, ^snp5-1-33-1

and the brahmin Posāla, ^snp5-1-33-2

Mogharāja the intelligent, ^snp5-1-33-3

and Piṅgiya the great seer. ^snp5-1-33-4

Each of them had their own following, ^snp5-1-34-1

they were renowned the whole world over. ^snp5-1-34-2

Those attentive ones, <j>meditators who love absorption, ^snp5-1-34-3

were redolent with the potential <j>of their past deeds. ^snp5-1-34-4

Having bowed to Bāvari, ^snp5-1-35-1

and circled him to his right, ^snp5-1-35-2

they set out for the north, ^snp5-1-35-3

with their dreadlocks and hides. ^snp5-1-35-4

First to Patiṭṭhāna of Muḷaka, ^snp5-1-36-1

then on to the citadel of Māhissatī; ^snp5-1-36-2

to Ujjenī and Gonaddhā, ^snp5-1-36-3

and Vedisa, and Vanasa. ^snp5-1-36-4

Then to Kosambī and Sāketa, ^snp5-1-37-1

and the capital city of Sāvatthī; ^snp5-1-37-2

on they went to Setavyā and Kapilavatthu, ^snp5-1-37-3

and the homestead at Kusinārā. ^snp5-1-37-4

To Pāvā they went, and Bhoga City, ^snp5-1-38-1

and on to Vesālī and the Magadhan city. ^snp5-1-38-2

Finally they reached the Pāsāṇaka Shrine, ^snp5-1-38-3

fair and delightful. ^snp5-1-38-4

Like a thirsty person to cool water, ^snp5-1-39-1

like a merchant to great profit, ^snp5-1-39-2

like a heat-struck person to shade, ^snp5-1-39-3

they quickly climbed the mountain. ^snp5-1-39-4

At that time the Buddha ^snp5-1-40-1

at the fore of the mendicant Saṅgha, ^snp5-1-40-2

was teaching the mendicants the Dhamma, ^snp5-1-40-3

like a lion roaring in the jungle. ^snp5-1-40-4

Ajita saw the Buddha, ^snp5-1-41-1

like the sun shining with a hundred rays, ^snp5-1-41-2

like the moon on the fifteenth day ^snp5-1-41-3

when it has come into its fullness. ^snp5-1-41-4

Then he saw his body, ^snp5-1-42-1

complete in all features. ^snp5-1-42-2

Thrilled, he stood to one side ^snp5-1-42-3

and asked this question in his mind. ^snp5-1-42-4

“Speak about the brahmin’s birth; ^snp5-1-43-1

of his clan; and his own marks; ^snp5-1-43-2

what hymns is he proficient in; ^snp5-1-43-3

and how many he teaches.” ^snp5-1-43-4

“His age is a hundred and twenty. ^snp5-1-44-1

By clan he is a Bāvari. ^snp5-1-44-2

There are three marks on his body. ^snp5-1-44-3

He is a master of the three Vedas, ^snp5-1-44-4

the teachings on the marks, the testaments, ^snp5-1-45-1

the vocabularies, and the rituals. ^snp5-1-45-2

He teaches recitation to five hundred, ^snp5-1-45-3

and has reached proficiency in his own teaching.” ^snp5-1-45-4

“O supreme man, cutter of craving, ^snp5-1-46-1

please reveal in detail ^snp5-1-46-2

Bāvari’s marks— ^snp5-1-46-3

let us doubt no longer!” ^snp5-1-46-4

“He can cover his face with his tongue; ^snp5-1-47-1

there is a tuft of hair between his eyebrows; ^snp5-1-47-2

his private parts are sheathed in a foreskin: ^snp5-1-47-3

know them as this, student.” ^snp5-1-47-4

Hearing the answers ^snp5-1-48-1

without having heard any questions, ^snp5-1-48-2

all the people, inspired, ^snp5-1-48-3

with cupped palms, wondered: ^snp5-1-48-4

“Who is it that asked a question with their mind? ^snp5-1-49-1

Was it a god or divinity? ^snp5-1-49-2

Or Indra, Sujā’s husband? ^snp5-1-49-3

To whom does the Buddha reply?” ^snp5-1-49-4

“Bāvari asks ^snp5-1-50-1

about heads and head-splitting. ^snp5-1-50-2

May the Buddha please answer, ^snp5-1-50-3

and so, O seer, dispel our doubt.” ^snp5-1-50-4

“Know ignorance as the head, ^snp5-1-51-1

and knowledge as the head-splitter, ^snp5-1-51-2

when joined with faith, mindfulness, and immersion, ^snp5-1-51-3

and enthusiasm and energy.” ^snp5-1-51-4

At that, the student, ^snp5-1-52-1

full of inspiration, ^snp5-1-52-2

arranged his antelope-skin cloak over one shoulder, ^snp5-1-52-3

and fell with his head to the Buddha’s feet. ^snp5-1-52-4

“Good fellow, the brahmin Bāvari ^snp5-1-53-1

together with his acolytes, ^snp5-1-53-2

elated and happy, ^snp5-1-53-3

bows to your feet, O Clear-eyed One!” ^snp5-1-53-4

“May the brahmin Bāvari be happy, ^snp5-1-54-1

together with his acolytes. ^snp5-1-54-2

And may you, too, be happy! ^snp5-1-54-3

May you live long, student. ^snp5-1-54-4

To Bāvari and you all ^snp5-1-55-1

I grant the opportunity to clear up all doubt. ^snp5-1-55-2

Please ask ^snp5-1-55-3

whatever you want.” ^snp5-1-55-4

Granted the opportunity by the Buddha, ^snp5-1-56-1

they sat down with cupped palms. ^snp5-1-56-2

Ajita asked the Realized One ^snp5-1-56-3

the first question right there. ^snp5-1-56-4

The introductory verses are finished. ^snp5-1-56-5

### The Questions of Ajita (Anthology of Discourses 5.2) ^snp5-2-0

“By what is the world shrouded?” ^snp5-2-1-1

said Venerable Ajita. ^snp5-2-1-2

“Why does it not shine? ^snp5-2-1-3

Tell me, what is its sullying? ^snp5-2-1-4

What is its greatest fear?” ^snp5-2-1-5

“The world is shrouded in ignorance.” ^snp5-2-2-1

replied the Buddha. ^snp5-2-2-2

“Avarice and negligence make it not shine. ^snp5-2-2-3

Muttered prayer is its sullying, I say. ^snp5-2-2-4

Suffering is its greatest fear.” ^snp5-2-2-5

“The streams flow everywhere,” ^snp5-2-3-1

said Venerable Ajita. ^snp5-2-3-2

“What is there to block them? ^snp5-2-3-3

And tell me the restraint of streams— ^snp5-2-3-4

by what are they locked out?” ^snp5-2-3-5

“The streams in the world,” ^snp5-2-4-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-2-4-2

“are blocked by mindfulness. ^snp5-2-4-3

I tell you the restraint of streams— ^snp5-2-4-4

they are locked out by wisdom.” ^snp5-2-4-5

“That wisdom and mindfulness,” ^snp5-2-5-1

said Venerable Ajita, ^snp5-2-5-2

“and that which is name and form, good fellow; ^snp5-2-5-3

when questioned, please tell me of this: ^snp5-2-5-4

where does this all cease?” ^snp5-2-5-5

“This question which you have asked, ^snp5-2-6-1

I shall answer you, Ajita. ^snp5-2-6-2

Where name and form ^snp5-2-6-3

cease with no residue left behind— ^snp5-2-6-4

with the cessation of consciousness, ^snp5-2-6-5

here they cease.” ^snp5-2-6-6

“There are those who have appraised the teaching, ^snp5-2-7-1

and many kinds of trainees here. ^snp5-2-7-2

Now you, being alert, are asked ^snp5-2-7-3

to tell me their behavior, good fellow.’ ^snp5-2-7-4

“Not greedy for sensual pleasures, ^snp5-2-8-1

their mind would be unclouded. ^snp5-2-8-2

Skilled in all things, ^snp5-2-8-3

a mendicant would wander mindful.” ^snp5-2-8-4

### The Questions of Tissametteyya (Anthology of Discourses 5.3) ^snp5-3-0

“Who here in the world is content?” ^snp5-3-1-1

said Venerable Tissametteyya. ^snp5-3-1-2

“Who has no disturbances? ^snp5-3-1-3

What thoughtful one, having known both ends, ^snp5-3-1-4

is not stuck in the middle? ^snp5-3-1-5

Who do they say is a great man? ^snp5-3-1-6

Who here has escaped the seamstress?” ^snp5-3-1-7

“Leading the spiritual life among sensual pleasures,” ^snp5-3-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-3-2-2

“rid of craving, ever mindful; ^snp5-3-2-3

a mendicant who, after appraisal, is quenched: ^snp5-3-2-4

that’s who has no disturbances. ^snp5-3-2-5

That thoughtful one, having known both ends, ^snp5-3-3-1

is not stuck in the middle. ^snp5-3-3-2

He is a great man, I declare, ^snp5-3-3-3

he has escaped the seamstress here.” ^snp5-3-3-4

### The Questions of Puṇṇaka (Anthology of Discourses 5.4) ^snp5-4-0

“To the imperturbable, the seer of the root,” ^snp5-4-1-1

said Venerable Puṇṇaka, ^snp5-4-1-2

“I have come seeking with a question. ^snp5-4-1-3

On what grounds have seers and men, ^snp5-4-1-4

aristocrats and brahmins here in the world ^snp5-4-1-5

performed so many different <j>sacrifices to the gods? ^snp5-4-1-6

I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this.” ^snp5-4-1-7

“Whatever seers and men,” ^snp5-4-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-4-2-2

“aristocrats and brahmins here in the world ^snp5-4-2-3

have performed so many different <j>sacrifices to the gods: ^snp5-4-2-4

all performed sacrifices bound to old age, ^snp5-4-2-5

longing for this place.” ^snp5-4-2-6

“As to those seers and men,” ^snp5-4-3-1

said Venerable Puṇṇaka, ^snp5-4-3-2

“and aristocrats and brahmins here in the world ^snp5-4-3-3

who have performed so many different <j>sacrifices to the gods: ^snp5-4-3-4

being diligent in the technique of sacrifice, ^snp5-4-3-5

have they crossed over rebirth and old age, good fellow? ^snp5-4-3-6

I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this.” ^snp5-4-3-7

“Hoping, invoking, praying, and worshiping,” ^snp5-4-4-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-4-4-2

“they pray for pleasure derived from profit. ^snp5-4-4-3

Devoted to sacrifice, besotted by rebirth, ^snp5-4-4-4

they’ve not crossed over rebirth and old age, <j>I declare.” ^snp5-4-4-5

“If those devoted to sacrifice,” ^snp5-4-5-1

said Venerable Puṇṇaka, ^snp5-4-5-2

“have not, by sacrificing, <j>crossed over rebirth and old age, ^snp5-4-5-3

who then in the world of gods and humans ^snp5-4-5-4

has crossed over rebirth and old age, good fellow? ^snp5-4-5-5

I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this.” ^snp5-4-5-6

“Having appraised the world high and low,” ^snp5-4-6-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-4-6-2

“there is nothing in the world that disturbs them. ^snp5-4-6-3

Peaceful, unclouded, untroubled, <j>with no need for hope— ^snp5-4-6-4

they’ve crossed over rebirth and old age, I declare.” ^snp5-4-6-5

### The Questions of Mettagū (Anthology of Discourses 5.5) ^snp5-5-0

“I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this,” ^snp5-5-1-1

said Venerable Mettagū, ^snp5-5-1-2

“for I think you are a knowledge master, evolved. ^snp5-5-1-3

Where do all these sufferings come from, ^snp5-5-1-4

in all their countless forms in the world?” ^snp5-5-1-5

“You have rightly asked me of the origin of suffering,” ^snp5-5-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-5-2-2

“I shall tell you as I understand it. ^snp5-5-2-3

Attachment is the source of suffering ^snp5-5-2-4

in all its countless forms in the world. ^snp5-5-2-5

When an ignorant person builds up attachments, ^snp5-5-3-1

that dullard returns to suffering again and again. ^snp5-5-3-2

So let one who understands <j>not build up attachments, ^snp5-5-3-3

contemplating the inception of suffering and rebirth.” ^snp5-5-3-4

“Whatever I asked you have explained to me. ^snp5-5-4-1

I ask you once more, please tell me this: ^snp5-5-4-2

How do the attentive cross the flood ^snp5-5-4-3

of rebirth, old age, sorrow, and lamenting? ^snp5-5-4-4

Please, sage, answer me clearly, ^snp5-5-4-5

for truly you understand this matter.” ^snp5-5-4-6

“I shall extol a teaching to you,” ^snp5-5-5-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-5-5-2

“that is apparent in the present, <j>not relying on tradition. ^snp5-5-5-3

Having understood it, one who lives mindfully ^snp5-5-5-4

may cross over clinging in the world.” ^snp5-5-5-5

“And I rejoice, great seer, ^snp5-5-6-1

in that supreme teaching, ^snp5-5-6-2

having understood which, one who lives mindfully ^snp5-5-6-3

may cross over clinging in the world.” ^snp5-5-6-4

“Once you’ve expelled relishing and dogmatism,” ^snp5-5-7-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-5-7-2

“regarding everything you are aware of— ^snp5-5-7-3

above, below, all round, between— ^snp5-5-7-4

having cast aside consciousness, don’t continue in existence. ^snp5-5-7-5

A mendicant who wanders meditating like this, ^snp5-5-8-1

diligent and mindful, calling nothing their own, ^snp5-5-8-2

would, being wise, give up the suffering ^snp5-5-8-3

of rebirth, old age, sorrow and lamenting right here.” ^snp5-5-8-4

“I rejoice in the words of the great seer! ^snp5-5-9-1

You have expounded non-attachment well, Gotama. ^snp5-5-9-2

Clearly the Buddha has given up suffering, ^snp5-5-9-3

for truly you understand this matter. ^snp5-5-9-4

Surely, sage, those you’d regularly instruct ^snp5-5-10-1

would also give up suffering. ^snp5-5-10-2

Having met, I bow especially to you, <j>O spiritual giant; ^snp5-5-10-3

hopefully the Buddha may regularly instruct me.” ^snp5-5-10-4

“Any brahmin recognized as a knowledge master, ^snp5-5-11-1

who has nothing, unattached to sensual life, ^snp5-5-11-2

clearly has crossed this flood, ^snp5-5-11-3

crossed to the far shore, kind, wishless. ^snp5-5-11-4

And a wise person here, a knowledge master, ^snp5-5-12-1

having untied the bond to life after life, ^snp5-5-12-2

free of craving, untroubled, with no need for hope, ^snp5-5-12-3

has crossed over rebirth and old age, I declare.” ^snp5-5-12-4

### The Questions of Dhotaka (Anthology of Discourses 5.6) ^snp5-6-0

“I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this,” ^snp5-6-1-1

said Venerable Dhotaka, ^snp5-6-1-2

“I long for your voice, great seer. ^snp5-6-1-3

After hearing your message, ^snp5-6-1-4

I shall train myself for extinguishment.” ^snp5-6-1-5

“Well then, be keen, alert,” ^snp5-6-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-6-2-2

“and mindful right here. ^snp5-6-2-3

After hearing this message, go on ^snp5-6-2-4

and train yourself for extinguishment.” ^snp5-6-2-5

“I see in the world of gods and humans ^snp5-6-3-1

a brahmin travelling with nothing. ^snp5-6-3-2

I bow especially to you, All-seer: ^snp5-6-3-3

release me, Sakyan, from my indecisions.” ^snp5-6-3-4

“I am not able to release anyone ^snp5-6-4-1

in the world who is indecisive, Dhotaka. ^snp5-6-4-2

But when you understand the best of teachings, ^snp5-6-4-3

you shall cross this flood.” ^snp5-6-4-4

“Teach me, brahmin, out of compassion, ^snp5-6-5-1

the principle of seclusion so that I may understand. ^snp5-6-5-2

I wish to practice right here, peaceful, independent, ^snp5-6-5-3

unobstructed as space itself.” ^snp5-6-5-4

“I shall extol that peace for you,” ^snp5-6-6-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-6-6-2

“that is apparent in the present, <j>not relying on tradition. ^snp5-6-6-3

Having understood it, one who lives mindfully ^snp5-6-6-4

may cross over clinging in the world.” ^snp5-6-6-5

“And I rejoice, great seer, ^snp5-6-7-1

in that supreme peace, ^snp5-6-7-2

having understood which, one who lives mindfully ^snp5-6-7-3

may cross over clinging in the world.” ^snp5-6-7-4

“Once you have understood that everything,” ^snp5-6-8-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-6-8-2

“you are aware of in the world— ^snp5-6-8-3

above, below, all round, between— ^snp5-6-8-4

is a snare, don’t crave for life after life.” ^snp5-6-8-5

### The Questions of Upasīva (Anthology of Discourses 5.7) ^snp5-7-0

“Alone and independent, O Sakyan,” ^snp5-7-1-1

said Venerable Upasīva, ^snp5-7-1-2

“I am not able to cross the great flood. ^snp5-7-1-3

Tell me a support, All-seer, ^snp5-7-1-4

depending on which I may cross this flood.” ^snp5-7-1-5

“Mindfully contemplating nothingness,” ^snp5-7-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-7-2-2

“depending on the perception ‘there is nothing’, <j>cross the flood. ^snp5-7-2-3

Giving up sensual pleasures, <j>refraining from chatter, ^snp5-7-2-4

watch day and night for the ending of craving.” ^snp5-7-2-5

“One who is free of desire for sensual pleasures,” ^snp5-7-3-1

said Venerable Upasīva, ^snp5-7-3-2

“depending on nothingness, all else left behind, ^snp5-7-3-3

freed in the ultimate liberation of perception: ^snp5-7-3-4

might they remain there without travelling on?” ^snp5-7-3-5

“One free of desire for sensual pleasures,” ^snp5-7-4-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-7-4-2

“depending on nothingness, all else left behind, ^snp5-7-4-3

freed in the ultimate liberation of perception: ^snp5-7-4-4

they might remain there without travelling on.” ^snp5-7-4-5

“If they were to remain there without travelling on, ^snp5-7-5-1

even for myriad years, All-seer, ^snp5-7-5-2

and, being freed, were to grow cool right there, ^snp5-7-5-3

would the consciousness of that sort pass away?” ^snp5-7-5-4

“As a flame tossed by a gust of wind,” ^snp5-7-6-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-7-6-2

“comes to an end and cannot be reckoned; ^snp5-7-6-3

so too, a sage freed from the set of mental phenomena ^snp5-7-6-4

comes to an end and cannot be reckoned.” ^snp5-7-6-5

“One who has disappeared—do they not exist? ^snp5-7-7-1

Or are they free from disease for eternity? ^snp5-7-7-2

Please, sage, answer me clearly, ^snp5-7-7-3

for truly you understand this matter.” ^snp5-7-7-4

“One who has disappeared cannot be defined,” ^snp5-7-8-1

replied the Buddha. ^snp5-7-8-2

“They have nothing <j>by which others might describe them. ^snp5-7-8-3

When all things have been eradicated, ^snp5-7-8-4

eradicated, too, are all ways of speech.” ^snp5-7-8-5

### The Questions of Nanda (Anthology of Discourses 5.8) ^snp5-8-0

“People say there are sages in the world,” ^snp5-8-1-1

said Venerable Nanda, ^snp5-8-1-2

“but how is this the case? ^snp5-8-1-3

Is someone called a sage because of their knowledge, ^snp5-8-1-4

or because of their way of life?” ^snp5-8-1-5

“Experts do not speak of a sage in terms of ^snp5-8-2-1

view, learning, or knowledge. ^snp5-8-2-2

Those who are sages live far from the crowd, I say, ^snp5-8-2-3

untroubled, with no need for hope.” ^snp5-8-2-4

“As to those ascetics and brahmins,” ^snp5-8-3-1

said Venerable Nanda, ^snp5-8-3-2

“who speak of purity in terms of what is seen or heard, ^snp5-8-3-3

or in terms of precepts and vows, ^snp5-8-3-4

or in terms of countless different things. ^snp5-8-3-5

Living self-controlled in that matter, ^snp5-8-3-6

have they crossed over rebirth and old age, good fellow? ^snp5-8-3-7

I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this.” ^snp5-8-3-8

“As to those ascetics and brahmins,” ^snp5-8-4-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-8-4-2

“who speak of purity in terms of what is seen or heard, ^snp5-8-4-3

or in terms of precepts and vows, ^snp5-8-4-4

or in terms of countless different things. ^snp5-8-4-5

Even though they live self-controlled in that matter, ^snp5-8-4-6

they’ve not crossed over rebirth and old age, <j>I declare.” ^snp5-8-4-7

“As to those ascetics and brahmins,” ^snp5-8-5-1

said Venerable Nanda, ^snp5-8-5-2

“who speak of purity in terms of what is seen or heard, ^snp5-8-5-3

or in terms of precepts and vows, ^snp5-8-5-4

or in terms of countless different things. ^snp5-8-5-5

You say they have not crossed the flood, sage. ^snp5-8-5-6

So who then in the world of gods and humans ^snp5-8-5-7

has crossed over rebirth and old age, good fellow? ^snp5-8-5-8

I ask you, Blessed One; please tell me this.” ^snp5-8-5-9

“I don’t say that all ascetics and brahmins,” ^snp5-8-6-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-8-6-2

“are shrouded by rebirth and old age. ^snp5-8-6-3

There are those here who have given up all ^snp5-8-6-4

that is seen, heard, and thought, <j>and precepts and vows, ^snp5-8-6-5

who have given up all the countless different things. ^snp5-8-6-6

Fully understanding craving, they are free of defilements, ^snp5-8-6-7

those people, I say, have crossed the flood.” ^snp5-8-6-8

“I rejoice in the words of the great seer! ^snp5-8-7-1

You have expounded non-attachment well, Gotama. ^snp5-8-7-2

There are those here who have given up all ^snp5-8-7-3

that is seen, heard, and thought, <j>and precepts and vows, ^snp5-8-7-4

who have given up all the countless different things. ^snp5-8-7-5

Fully understanding craving, they are free of defilements, ^snp5-8-7-6

those people, I agree, have crossed the flood.” ^snp5-8-7-7

### The Questions of Hemaka (Anthology of Discourses 5.9) ^snp5-9-0

“Those who have previously answered me,” ^snp5-9-1-1

said Venerable Hemaka, ^snp5-9-1-2

“before Gotama’s teaching, ^snp5-9-1-3

said ‘thus it was’ or ‘so it shall be’. ^snp5-9-1-4

All that was just the testament of hearsay; ^snp5-9-1-5

all that just fostered speculation: ^snp5-9-1-6

I found no delight in that. ^snp5-9-1-7

But you, sage, explain to me ^snp5-9-2-1

the teaching that destroys craving. ^snp5-9-2-2

Having understood it, one who lives mindfully ^snp5-9-2-3

may cross over clinging in the world.” ^snp5-9-2-4

“The removal of desire and lust, Hemaka, ^snp5-9-3-1

for pleasant things ^snp5-9-3-2

seen, heard, thought, or cognized here ^snp5-9-3-3

is extinguishment, the state that does not pass. ^snp5-9-3-4

Those who have fully understood this, mindful, ^snp5-9-4-1

are quenched in this very life. ^snp5-9-4-2

Always at peace, ^snp5-9-4-3

they’ve crossed over clinging to the world.” ^snp5-9-4-4

### The Questions of Todeyya (Anthology of Discourses 5.10) ^snp5-10-0

“In whom sensual pleasures do not dwell,” ^snp5-10-1-1

said Venerable Todeyya, ^snp5-10-1-2

“and for whom there is no craving, ^snp5-10-1-3

and who has crossed over indecision— ^snp5-10-1-4

of what kind is their liberation?” ^snp5-10-1-5

“In whom sensual pleasures do not dwell,” ^snp5-10-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-10-2-2

“and for whom there is no craving, ^snp5-10-2-3

and who has crossed over indecision— ^snp5-10-2-4

there is no liberation that is further.” ^snp5-10-2-5

“Are they free of hope, <j>or are they still in need of hope? ^snp5-10-3-1

Do they possess wisdom, <j>or are they still forming wisdom? ^snp5-10-3-2

O Sakyan, elucidate the sage to me, ^snp5-10-3-3

so that I may understand, All-seer.” ^snp5-10-3-4

“They are free of hope, <j>they are not in need of hope. ^snp5-10-4-1

They possess wisdom, <j>they are not still forming wisdom. ^snp5-10-4-2

That, Todeyya, is how to understand a sage, ^snp5-10-4-3

one who has nothing, unattached to sensual life.” ^snp5-10-4-4

### The Questions of Kappa (Anthology of Discourses 5.11) ^snp5-11-0

“For those overwhelmed by old age and death,” ^snp5-11-1-1

said Venerable Kappa, ^snp5-11-1-2

“stuck mid-stream ^snp5-11-1-3

as the terrifying flood arises, ^snp5-11-1-4

tell me an island, good fellow. ^snp5-11-1-5

Explain to me an island ^snp5-11-1-6

so this may occur no further.” ^snp5-11-1-7

“For those overwhelmed by old age and death,” ^snp5-11-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-11-2-2

“stuck mid-stream ^snp5-11-2-3

as the terrifying flood arises, ^snp5-11-2-4

I shall tell you an island, Kappa. ^snp5-11-2-5

Having nothing, taking nothing: ^snp5-11-3-1

this is the island with nothing further. ^snp5-11-3-2

I call it extinguishment, ^snp5-11-3-3

the ending of old age and death. ^snp5-11-3-4

Those who have fully understood this, mindful, ^snp5-11-4-1

are quenched in this very life. ^snp5-11-4-2

They don’t fall under Māra’s sway, ^snp5-11-4-3

nor are they his footmen.” ^snp5-11-4-4

### The Questions of Jatukaṇṇī (Anthology of Discourses 5.12) ^snp5-12-0

“Hearing of the hero <j>with no desire for sensual pleasures,” ^snp5-12-1-1

said Venerable Jatukaṇṇī, ^snp5-12-1-2

“who has passed over the flood, I’ve come <j>with a question for that desireless one. ^snp5-12-1-3

Tell me the state of peace, O natural visionary. ^snp5-12-1-4

Tell me this, Blessed One, as it really is. ^snp5-12-1-5

For, having mastered sensual desires, <j>the Blessed One proceeds, ^snp5-12-2-1

as the blazing sun shines on the earth. ^snp5-12-2-2

May you of vast wisdom explain the teaching ^snp5-12-2-3

to me of little wisdom so that I may understand ^snp5-12-2-4

the giving up of rebirth and old age here.” ^snp5-12-2-5

“With desire for sensual pleasures dispelled,” ^snp5-12-3-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-12-3-2

“seeing renunciation as sanctuary, ^snp5-12-3-3

don’t be taking up or putting down ^snp5-12-3-4

anything at all. ^snp5-12-3-5

What came before, let wither away, ^snp5-12-4-1

and after, let there be nothing. ^snp5-12-4-2

If you don’t grasp at the middle, ^snp5-12-4-3

you will live at peace. ^snp5-12-4-4

One rid of greed, brahmin, ^snp5-12-5-1

for the whole realm of name and form, ^snp5-12-5-2

has no defilements by which ^snp5-12-5-3

they might fall under the sway of Death.” ^snp5-12-5-4

### The Questions of Bhadrāvudha (Anthology of Discourses 5.13) ^snp5-13-0

“I beseech you, the bastion-leaver, the craving-cutter, the imperturbable,” ^snp5-13-1-1

said Venerable Bhadrāvudha, ^snp5-13-1-2

“the delight-leaver, the flood-crosser, the freed, ^snp5-13-1-3

the formulation-leaver, the intelligent. ^snp5-13-1-4

Different people have gathered <j>from across the nations ^snp5-13-1-5

wishing to hear your word, O hero. ^snp5-13-2-1

After hearing the spiritual giant <j>they will depart from here. ^snp5-13-2-2

Please, sage, answer them clearly, ^snp5-13-2-3

for truly you understand this matter.” ^snp5-13-2-4

“Dispel all craving for attachments,” ^snp5-13-3-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-13-3-2

“above, below, all round, between. ^snp5-13-3-3

For whatever a personage grasps in the world, ^snp5-13-3-4

Māra pursues them right there. ^snp5-13-3-5

So let a mindful mendicant who understands ^snp5-13-4-1

not grasp anything in all the world, ^snp5-13-4-2

observing that, in clinging to attachments, ^snp5-13-4-3

these people cling to the domain of death.” ^snp5-13-4-4

### The Questions of Udaya (Anthology of Discourses 5.14) ^snp5-14-0

“To the meditator, rid of hopes,” ^snp5-14-1-1

said Venerable Udaya, ^snp5-14-1-2

“who has completed the task, is free of defilements, ^snp5-14-1-3

and has gone beyond all things, ^snp5-14-1-4

I have come seeking with a question. ^snp5-14-1-5

Tell me the liberation by enlightenment, ^snp5-14-1-6

the smashing of ignorance.” ^snp5-14-1-7

“The giving up of both” ^snp5-14-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-14-2-2

“sensual desires and displeasures; ^snp5-14-2-3

the casting aside of dullness, ^snp5-14-2-4

and the prevention of remorse; ^snp5-14-2-5

pure equanimity and mindfulness, ^snp5-14-3-1

preceded by investigation of principles— ^snp5-14-3-2

this, I declare, is liberation by enlightenment, ^snp5-14-3-3

the smashing of ignorance.” ^snp5-14-3-4

“What fetters the world? ^snp5-14-4-1

How does it travel about? ^snp5-14-4-2

With the giving up of what ^snp5-14-4-3

is extinguishment spoken of?” ^snp5-14-4-4

“Delight fetters the world. ^snp5-14-5-1

It travels about by means of thought. ^snp5-14-5-2

With the giving up of craving ^snp5-14-5-3

extinguishment is spoken of.” ^snp5-14-5-4

“For one living mindfully, ^snp5-14-6-1

how does consciousness cease? ^snp5-14-6-2

We’ve come to ask you, worthy sir: ^snp5-14-6-3

let us hear what you say.” ^snp5-14-6-4

“Not taking pleasure in feeling ^snp5-14-7-1

internally and externally— ^snp5-14-7-2

for one living mindfully, ^snp5-14-7-3

that’s how consciousness ceases.” ^snp5-14-7-4

### The Question of Posāla (Anthology of Discourses 5.15) ^snp5-15-0

“To the one who reveals the past,” ^snp5-15-1-1

said Venerable Posāla, ^snp5-15-1-2

“who is imperturbable, with doubts cut off, ^snp5-15-1-3

and who has gone beyond all things, ^snp5-15-1-4

I have come seeking with a question. ^snp5-15-1-5

Consider one who perceives that form has vanished, ^snp5-15-2-1

who has entirely given up the body, ^snp5-15-2-2

and who sees nothing at all ^snp5-15-2-3

internally and externally. ^snp5-15-2-4

I ask the Sakyan about knowledge for them; ^snp5-15-2-5

how should that sort be guided?” ^snp5-15-2-6

“The Realized One directly knows,” ^snp5-15-3-1

said the Buddha, ^snp5-15-3-2

“all planes of consciousness. ^snp5-15-3-3

And he knows this one who remains, ^snp5-15-3-4

committed to that as their final goal. ^snp5-15-3-5

Understanding that desire for rebirth ^snp5-15-4-1

in the dimension of nothingness is a fetter, ^snp5-15-4-2

and directly knowing what this really means, ^snp5-15-4-3

one then discerns that matter clearly. ^snp5-15-4-4

That is their knowledge of truth, ^snp5-15-4-5

the brahmin who has lived the life.” ^snp5-15-4-6

### The Questions of Mogharāja (Anthology of Discourses 5.16) ^snp5-16-0

“Twice I have asked the Sakyan,” ^snp5-16-1-1

said Venerable Mogharāja, ^snp5-16-1-2

“but you haven’t answered me, O Clear-eyed One. ^snp5-16-1-3

I have heard that the divine seer ^snp5-16-1-4

answers when questioned a third time. ^snp5-16-1-5

Regarding this world, the other world, ^snp5-16-2-1

and the realm of divinity with its gods, ^snp5-16-2-2

I’m not familiar with the view ^snp5-16-2-3

of the renowned Gotama. ^snp5-16-2-4

So I have come seeking with a question ^snp5-16-3-1

for the one of excellent vision. ^snp5-16-3-2

How to look upon the world ^snp5-16-3-3

so the King of Death won’t see you?” ^snp5-16-3-4

“Look upon the world as empty, ^snp5-16-4-1

Mogharāja, ever mindful. ^snp5-16-4-2

Having uprooted the theory of self, ^snp5-16-4-3

you may thus cross over death. ^snp5-16-4-4

That’s how to look upon the world ^snp5-16-4-5

so the King of Death won’t see you.” ^snp5-16-4-6

### The Questions of Piṅgiya (Anthology of Discourses 5.17) ^snp5-17-0

“I am old, feeble, and pallid,” ^snp5-17-1-1

said Venerable Piṅgiya, ^snp5-17-1-2

“my eyes unclear, my hearing faint— ^snp5-17-1-3

don’t let me perish while still confused. ^snp5-17-1-4

Explain the teaching so that I may understand ^snp5-17-1-5

the giving up of rebirth and old age here.” ^snp5-17-1-6

“Seeing them being harmed <j>on account of forms,” ^snp5-17-2-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-17-2-2

“those negligent folk are inflicted <j>on account of forms— ^snp5-17-2-3

therefore, Piṅgiya, being diligent, ^snp5-17-2-4

give up form so as not to be reborn.” ^snp5-17-2-5

“The four quarters, the intermediate directions, ^snp5-17-3-1

below, and above: in these ten directions ^snp5-17-3-2

there’s nothing at all in the world ^snp5-17-3-3

that you’ve not seen, heard, thought, or cognized. ^snp5-17-3-4

Explain the teaching so that I may understand ^snp5-17-3-5

the giving up of rebirth and old age here.” ^snp5-17-3-6

“Observing mankind sunk in craving,” ^snp5-17-4-1

replied the Buddha, ^snp5-17-4-2

“tormented, mired in old age; ^snp5-17-4-3

therefore, Piṅgiya, being diligent, ^snp5-17-4-4

give up craving so as not to be reborn.” ^snp5-17-4-5

### Homage to the Way to the Far Shore (Anthology of Discourses 5.18) ^snp5-18-0

This was said by the Buddha while staying in the land of the Magadhans at the Pāsāṇaka Shrine. ^snp5-18-1-1

When requested by the sixteen brahmin devotees, he answered their questions one by one. ^snp5-18-1-2

If you understand the meaning and the teaching of each of these questions, and practice in line with the teaching, you may go right to the far shore of old age and death. ^snp5-18-1-3

These teachings are said to lead to the far shore, which is why the name of this exposition of the teaching is “The Way to the Far Shore”. ^snp5-18-1-4

Ajita, Tissametteyya, ^snp5-18-2-1

Puṇṇaka and Mettagū, ^snp5-18-2-2

Dhotaka and Upasiva, ^snp5-18-2-3

Nanda and then Hemaka, ^snp5-18-2-4

both Todeyya and Kappa, ^snp5-18-3-1

and Jatukaṇṇī the astute, ^snp5-18-3-2

Bhadrāvudha and Udaya, ^snp5-18-3-3

and the brahmin Posāla, ^snp5-18-3-4

Mogharāja the intelligent, ^snp5-18-3-5

and Piṅgiya the great seer: ^snp5-18-3-6

they approached the Buddha, ^snp5-18-4-1

the seer of consummate conduct. ^snp5-18-4-2

Asking their subtle questions, ^snp5-18-4-3

they came to the most excellent Buddha. ^snp5-18-4-4

The Buddha answered their questions ^snp5-18-5-1

in accordance with truth. ^snp5-18-5-2

The sage satisfied the brahmins ^snp5-18-5-3

with his answers to their questions. ^snp5-18-5-4

Those who were satisfied by the Clear-eyed One, ^snp5-18-6-1

the Buddha, Kinsman of the Sun, ^snp5-18-6-2

led the spiritual life in his presence, ^snp5-18-6-3

the one of such outstanding wisdom. ^snp5-18-6-4

If you practice in accordance ^snp5-18-7-1

with each of these questions ^snp5-18-7-2

as taught by the Buddha, ^snp5-18-7-3

you’ll go from the near shore to the far. ^snp5-18-7-4

Developing the supreme path, ^snp5-18-8-1

you’ll go from the near shore to the far. ^snp5-18-8-2

This path is for going to the far shore; ^snp5-18-8-3

that’s why it’s called “The Way to the Far Shore”. ^snp5-18-8-4

### Preserving the Way to the Far Shore (Anthology of Discourses 5.19) ^snp5-19-0

“I shall keep reciting the Way to the Far Shore,” ^snp5-19-1-1

said Venerable Piṅgiya, ^snp5-19-1-2

“which was taught as it was seen ^snp5-19-1-3

by the immaculate one of vast intelligence. ^snp5-19-1-4

He is desireless, unentangled, a spiritual giant: ^snp5-19-1-5

why would he speak falsely? ^snp5-19-1-6

Come, let me extol ^snp5-19-2-1

in sweet words of praise ^snp5-19-2-2

the one who’s given up stains and delusions, ^snp5-19-2-3

conceit and contempt. ^snp5-19-2-4

The Buddha, all-seer, dispeller of darkness, ^snp5-19-3-1

has gone to world’s end, beyond all rebirths; ^snp5-19-3-2

he is free of defilements, <j>and has given up all suffering, ^snp5-19-3-3

the rightly-named one, brahmin, is revered by me. ^snp5-19-3-4

Like a bird that flees a little copse, ^snp5-19-4-1

to roost in a forest abounding in fruit, ^snp5-19-4-2

I’ve left the near-sighted behind, ^snp5-19-4-3

like a swan come to a great river. ^snp5-19-4-4

Those who explained to me previously, ^snp5-19-5-1

before Gotama’s teaching, ^snp5-19-5-2

said ‘thus it was’ or ‘so it shall be’. ^snp5-19-5-3

All that was just the testament of hearsay; ^snp5-19-5-4

all that just fostered speculation. ^snp5-19-5-5

Alone, the dispeller of darkness ^snp5-19-6-1

is brilliant, a beacon: ^snp5-19-6-2

Gotama, vast in wisdom, ^snp5-19-6-3

Gotama, vast in intelligence. ^snp5-19-6-4

He is the one who taught me Dhamma, ^snp5-19-7-1

apparent in the present life, immediately effective, ^snp5-19-7-2

the untroubled, the end of craving, ^snp5-19-7-3

to which there is no compare.” ^snp5-19-7-4

“Why would you dwell apart from him ^snp5-19-8-1

even for an hour, Piṅgiya? ^snp5-19-8-2

From Gotama, vast in wisdom, ^snp5-19-8-3

from Gotama, vast in intelligence? ^snp5-19-8-4

He is the one who taught you Dhamma, ^snp5-19-9-1

apparent in the present life, immediately effective, ^snp5-19-9-2

the untroubled, the end of craving, ^snp5-19-9-3

to which there is no compare.” ^snp5-19-9-4

“I never dwell apart from him, ^snp5-19-10-1

not even for an hour, brahmin. ^snp5-19-10-2

From Gotama, vast in wisdom, ^snp5-19-10-3

from Gotama, vast in intelligence. ^snp5-19-10-4

He is the one who taught me Dhamma, ^snp5-19-11-1

apparent in the present life, immediately effective, ^snp5-19-11-2

the untroubled, the end of craving, ^snp5-19-11-3

to which there is no compare. ^snp5-19-11-4

Being diligent, I see him ^snp5-19-12-1

in my mind’s eye day and night. ^snp5-19-12-2

I spend the night in homage to him, ^snp5-19-12-3

hence I think I dwell with him. ^snp5-19-12-4

My faith and joy and intent and mindfulness ^snp5-19-13-1

never stray from Gotama’s teaching. ^snp5-19-13-2

I bow to whatever direction ^snp5-19-13-3

the one of vast wisdom heads. ^snp5-19-13-4

I’m old and feeble, ^snp5-19-14-1

so my body cannot go there, ^snp5-19-14-2

but I always travel in my thoughts, ^snp5-19-14-3

for my mind, brahmin, is bound to him. ^snp5-19-14-4

Lying floundering in the mud, ^snp5-19-15-1

I drifted from island to island. ^snp5-19-15-2

Then I saw the Buddha, ^snp5-19-15-3

the undefiled one who has crossed the flood.” ^snp5-19-15-4

“Just as Vakkali was committed to faith— ^snp5-19-16-1

Bhadrāvudha and Gotama of Āḷavī too— ^snp5-19-16-2

so too you should commit to faith. ^snp5-19-16-3

You will go, Piṅgiya, beyond the domain of death.” ^snp5-19-16-4

“My confidence grows ^snp5-19-17-1

as I hear the word of the sage, ^snp5-19-17-2

the Buddha with veil cast off, ^snp5-19-17-3

so kind and eloquent. ^snp5-19-17-4

Having directly known all about the gods, ^snp5-19-18-1

he understands all top to bottom, ^snp5-19-18-2

the teacher who settles all questions ^snp5-19-18-3

for those who admit their doubts. ^snp5-19-18-4

The unfaltering, the unshakable: ^snp5-19-19-1

that to which there is no compare. ^snp5-19-19-2

For sure I will go there, I have no doubt of that. ^snp5-19-19-3

Remember me as one whose mind is made up.” ^snp5-19-19-4

The Anthology of Discourses is completed. ^snp5-19-40-5
